O, son, since I have completed for you this book, which is the culmination of the science of medicine, by its completion, its consequences, its explanations, and its clarifications, it seems to me fitting that I complete this treatise for you, which is a part of hand-operation, that is, surgery.
Because hand-operation is neglected in our region and in our time, almost entirely lacking, so that its science is greatly endangered, and its vestiges are lost; and nothing remains of it but some small descriptions in the books of the Ancients, which have been mostly altered and thus led to errors and hesitations, so that their intentions are obscured and their power and art are weakened.
And it seems fitting to me that I should revive it with the arrangement of this treatise, according to the method of explanation, clarification, and abbreviation; and so that it may come with the forms of the cautery irons and of the other instruments of the work, as well as by the addition of explanation, and by its preparation, which is a necessary matter.
But the reason why a good craftsman with his hands is not found in our time is that the art of medicine is long, and that its practitioner must first be exercised in the science of anatomy, which is very difficult, so that he knows the functions of the limbs and their forms, and their connection and separation; and has knowledge of the bones, the nerves and the ligaments, and their number, and their arrangement; and of the pulsating and quiet veins, and the places of their emergence.
And for this reason Hippocrates says that in name it is much, but in practice little, and especially in the art of the hand.
But we have already spoken of this at the beginning of this book; for whoever is not learned in what we have said about anatomy, is not exempt from falling into error, by which men will die, just as I have seen many, for some are trained in this science and undertake it without knowledge and without experience.
For I saw a foolish and unskilled physician incise a scrofulous abscess in the neck of a woman, and he incised some arteries of the neck, so that much blood flowed until the woman fell dead into his hands.
And I saw another physician who undertook to remove a stone from a man who was advanced in years, and the stone was large, and he came to him and extracted that stone with a piece of the bladder, so that that man died on the third day.
And I was called to extract it, and I saw that because of the size of the stone and the condition of the patient, I knew that he would die.
And I saw another physician who received a stipend from a duke of our land for surgery, and a black plaster cast was applied to a fractured thigh; and the physician, through his ignorance, extracted the fracture, over the wound, with cushions and splints with strong constriction, and did not allow the wound to breathe.
And then he unbound it according to his wishes, and then he bound it again, and then he left it for some days and ordered that we unbind the bandage; and it remained thus until the thigh and foot were suppurated, and it came to ruin.
And I was called to him, and I prepared to unbind the bandage, and tranquility was achieved, and thus a lessening of his pains.
But however, corruption was already confirmed in the limb, so that I could not restrain it, and I did not cease to see the corruption spread in the limb until the patient perished.
And I saw another physician who punctured a cancerous abscess, and it was ulcerated after a few days, until its malignancy was magnified by its master (i.e., the condition worsened significantly).
The reason is that since cancerous sores are full of melancholic humor, it is not appropriate to approach them with iron at all, but rather they should be treated by a member (a body part) that suffers in such a way that it is completely treated.
And for this reason, sons, it is necessary for you that the operation with the hand be divided into two parts: that is, into an operation which is associated with health, and into a procedure in which there is danger in the greatest cases.
And I have already explained this in every part of this book where an operation occurs that involves error and fear; therefore it is necessary for you to observe this and avoid that, so that fools do not find a way to speak and dishonor you.
Therefore, take up your instruments with diligence and with God's protection, and treat your patients with happiness and firmness.
And use the best method, by the grace of God, leading to health and laudable success, and avoid terrible diseases of difficult healing, and keep your instruments away from what makes you fear, so that hesitation in your faith and in your name does not overcome you: for it remains more for your glory, and higher in the world and behind your holiness.
Galen already said in some of his admonitions: Do not treat a bad disease, lest you be called a bad physician".
And I have divided this book into three chapters.
Begins the division of the book, and first of the first part. The first chapter ordered from head to foot, on the forms of instruments and the tools of cautery, and on what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, of suction and perforation, and of cupping glasses, and of wounds, and of the extraction of arrows and similar things, all chaptered and ordered according to the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, of restoration and dislocation, and of the treatment of sprains and similar things; a chapter ordered from head to foot, and of the forms of the instruments.
It is necessary that I say the quality of the benefits of that cautery and its drawbacks, and in what constitution cautery is prohibited.
Therefore I say that the discourse on the quality of the benefit of cautery and its drawbacks is a long and prolix discourse, and subtle in science, and a hidden secret; and many wise men have already spoken of it, and they disagree about it, and a small part of their discourses suffices for me, fearing prolixity.
And therefore I say that cautery benefits all complexions universally, whether with matter or without matter, except for two complexions, which are cold complexions without matter.
In the hot and dry complexion with matter, there is disagreement; for some say that cautery is helpful for that, and others say the contrary, that cautery is not suitable in a disease which is by quality and by dryness, because the nature of fire is quality and dryness, and it is inconvenient that a hot and dry disease should be healed with that which is hot and dry.
And I contradict those who say the contrary, that benefit is done by cautery in every hot and dry disease that comes in the bodies of men, for when you compare the body of man and its humidities to the complexion of fire, you find the body of man cold.
And I say, concerning this discourse, that experience has revealed this to me on several occasions.
However, it is not fitting that this should come upon someone who has already been exhausted and exercised in the chapter on cautery by prior practice, and has known the diversity of the complexions of men and the disposition of the diseases themselves, and the causes of those, and their accidents, and the span of time of those.
But in the other complexions, there is no fear for you, and especially in cold and humid diseases, for all the physicians are agreed upon those, and are not in disagreement about the benefit with cautery in those.
And know, sons, that the secret of healing by cautery with fire and its goodness, over cautery with burning medicine, is because fire is simple, whose action does not reach except to the member which is cauterized and does not harm the other member attached to it except with little harm.
But the action of burning medicine reaches to those members which are distant from it, and very quickly causes in the member a disease of difficult healing and quickly kills.
But fire, by its sublimity and the goodness of its substance, does not do this except insofar as it overflows. And it is already known to us by experiment and by length of service of study with the art concerning the truth of the causes; and for this reason I am excused from the length of the discourse. And if it were not that it is not fitting in my book to pause here to hide from you as a secret with fire, and the quality of its operation in bodies and its expulsion by which it expels diseases, with philosophical demonstrative discourse, which would be acute in your thoughts.
And know, sons, that they disagree about the time in which it is convenient to perform cautery, and they place the best time in springtime.
And I say that cautery is convenient at all times, because the harm caused by accidental time is submerged in the benefit of that which is achieved with cautery, and especially if the cautery is for necessary, strong and urgent pains, which do not tolerate delay because it is feared that those evils may come which are greater than the small harm coming from the time.
And this does not enter your thoughts, oh sons, this is only esteemed by the vulgar people and foolish physicians, that is, that for that illness which is healed by cautery there is no unsuitable time, and they posit that consequence.
And the cause is not at all as they think for that reason, because cautery is only in place of medicine which alters the complexion and dries the humors which are the cause by which pains come.
But however, cautery is superfluous over medicine because of the swiftness of its effectiveness and virtue of its operation and the greatness of its lord (or master).
And it is possible that his illness returns at some time, according to the complexion of the patient and the location of his illness, and his strength, and that which is prepared in his body from the aggregation of superfluities in him, and that which has come to him in his acquisition in foods and similar causes; unless the illness in which cautery is administered is a subtle illness in a limb with few superfluities and humors, such as cautery of a tooth for pain and similar to that, it is possible that in him that pain does not return, but this is done in small things (or small cases).
And a more common saying is that cautery is the last resort of medicine, and it is a righteous saying, not in the way they understand it, for they believe that it is not a cure that profits with medicine or with any other cause except cautery.
And the reason is contrary to that; and the intention that cautery is the last resort of medicine is only when we administer the methods of cure in some illnesses, and those medicines do not help, and then at the end of the process, one administers cautery, and it benefits in that case. And therefore from here we understand that cautery is the last resort of medicine, not according to the understanding of the common people and too many foolish physicians.
And the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they did not say this except for the preciousness of gold and the sublimity of its substance.
And they said that the place of cautery does not scar, although this is not absolutely true, for I have already proven this and found that it does not do so except in some bodies but not others, and cautery with gold is good and better than with iron as they said; however, when you perform cautery with fire with gold, it is not clear to you, for it is consumed according to the quantity you desire due to the softness of the gold, and because it prepares to receive cold; and if you apply fire to it, the gold melts from the fire, and some sheets fall into the fire, causing the artisan trouble due to this.
And for this reason, cautery with iron is done more easily and more appropriately for the correctness of the operation.
And I have already ordered this chapter on cautery according to chapters, and I have ordered those from the head to the feet, so that I may easily find what is requested from it.
The first chapter.
This cauterization combats the humidity and coldness affecting the brain, the accidents of which are two causes: that is, a cause of frequent and profuse rheumy flux, called coryza, flowing from the head to the parts of the eyes and ears, and excessive sleepiness, and toothache and throat pain, and universally any illness that comes from coldness, such as paralysis, sopor, and apoplexy, and similar illnesses.
The method of this cauterization is this: first, order the patient to be evacuated with laxative medicine, purifying the head, for three or four nights, depending on the strength of the patient, their age, and their constitution.
Then order him to shave his head with a razor, and then place him between your hands on a low bench and have him place his hands on his feet.
Then place the root of your palm against the brow above the root of the nose between his eyes. However, when your middle finger has reached that spot, mark the place with a probe, and then ignite the olive-shaped cautery, of which this is the form:
Then place it on the spot marked with the probe, in such a way that it descends, with which your hand should be gradually tightened, and you rotate it.
Then lift your hand slightly and examine the area, and if you see any amount of the bone's head exposed, about the size of a grain of barley, then raise your hand, and if not, return your hand with that iron, or with another like it, if it is cooled, until that which I have told you is seen of the bone.
Then take a little salt and dissolve it on the area and leave it for three days.
Then place a cotton ball soaked in wine on it and leave it there until the burn scar is destroyed. Then treat the area with tetrafarmicon ointment until it is healed.
And it is said that when the wound remains open, it expels the pus better, because it is better and more beneficial.
And some of them say that the bone marrow should be cauterized to the bone, and the cautery should be held there until some part of the bone's substance is hardened.
Then scrape away what is burnt from the bone, and then it should be treated.
And others say that with the cautery, one should reach the depth, until it first enters the bone strongly, so that the bone burns, and this is the form of a small alphecat (likely referring to a type of cauterization or lesion): And they believe that vapors from the head exhale from that place.
And the wound should be kept open for a long time, and then it should be healed until it is consolidated.
But I have not seen these two types of cautery at all, except in some men, as a way of fear; and leave that, it seems to me, better is health when it is; because the head is weakened by the solution of its continuity, as we have testified in the other members, and especially when the patient's head is of a cold nature.
And the first type of cautery is safer and better, in my opinion; and use that, and with that work, achieve health.
Chapter Two.
Of the cautery of the head.
When chronic and prolonged pain occurs in the whole head, and this is prolonged in the patient, and he uses leeches and cupping and head purges and plasters and poultices and cauteries and bandages and similar things, and especially if he has already been cauterized with a cautery that we have described and none of these things benefits him, then consider whether the patient's head is of strong constitution by nature; and if it is not cold and finds great cold, then cauterize that with another cautery a little below the previous one. And then cauterize that above the horn of his head with a cautery until the scab is parted/separated. Incise the scab and let the bone be uncovered to the extent we have described, and cauterize that with a cautery in the rear part of the head in the place which is called the vas – and this is the place where a cupping glass is placed. And ease your hand in this, and do not uncover the bone, for the patient thereby finds much greater pain, different from the pain of all other cauterizations of the head.
And I say this cautery in its place, because it is necessary that the cautery with which the horns of the head and the occiput are cauterized is a more subtle cautery than that with which the middle of the head is cauterized; and this is its form.
Chapter three.
Concerning the cauterization of a recent migraine.
When a sudden pain occurs in the head, or a spreading pain around the eye, let the head be evacuated with purifying medicines, and let the other treatment be administered, which I have described in the chapters on diseases; and if all this does not help, then cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with a sharp, burning medicine, or with iron.
But cauterization with a burning medicine is that you take a clove of garlic, and peel it and cut off its ends from both sides. Then incise the place of the pain in the temple with a lancet until a place is made under the skin, so that it is hidden.
Then bind it tightly with a compress to cause constriction, and leave it for three hours; and then untie it and remove the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days, and then apply a cotton swab soaked in butter until the place becomes sore.
And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, do this with some burning medicines which I have written about in the twelfth treatise on burning medicines.
But cauterization with iron in this manner: heat the cautery, of which this is the form:
and it is called a nail or peg, because its head is shaped like a nail, which has a certain curvature, and in the middle of it is a small eminence.
And then place it on the area of pain, and hold your hand — so that the flesh beneath is not burned — and move the iron little by little, with speed.
And if the amount that is burned by the specific heat is about half, then lift your hand so that the artery underneath is not burned, so that no bleeding occurs.
And then soak cotton in salt water and place that on the area, and leave it for three days; and then apply cotton soaked in a poultice, and then treat with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, cauterize with the tip of the knife as with a cautery, and beware of cutting the artery in this, specifically,  not-old, migraine.  (The "not-old" likely refers to a recent onset migraine.)
Chapter four.
Chapter Four: On the cauterization of chronic migraine.
When you treat a migraine with what we have said in the previous treatment, and what we have said in the divisions of illnesses, and the treatment is not effective, and sometimes because of the illness, what we have said about the first cauterization with medicine or cauterization with fire is insufficient, then you should heat the cautery knife until it is white-hot,  then mark the location of the pain with a line whose length is half a finger, or about that; and place your hand for a moment and press it until you incise the artery and reach the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to be careful of the continuation of the jaw, which is moved during mastication, and that the muscle not be injured and the nerve which serves it, and that a spasm does not occur.
And if it is about the care and caution regarding the flow of blood from the artery that is incised, because in its incision there is danger, especially for one who ignores what he is doing and is not studious and experienced; and abandons the operation at the beginning.
And I will come to the recollection of the management of accidental blood flow from the artery according to its manner, in its place in this book.
And if you see that this illness is not alleviated by this cauterization and the patient's body is tolerable, cauterize that in the middle of the head as we have recounted, and treat the wound until it is healed.
And if you wish, administer the cautery which we have mentioned in the chapter on the constriction of an artery with a cautery having two blades; this is a better and more appropriate cauterization.
Chapter 5.
Chapter 5. On the cauterization of earaches.
When pain in the ear occurs due to cold and is treated with laxative remedies and with the other treatments which I have mentioned in the division, and the pain is not relieved, then apply the cautery, and this is the one called *puncta*, of which this is the form:
And then apply the puncture, then the cauterization, and let it be moved around the whole ear as a wheel is turned, or in a circle around each part if the pain is in a particular part. Extend the cautery from the root of the ear little by little, after you have marked the area with a probe.
And if the cautery makes ten punctures in the whole ear, or whichever ear it is, then treat the area until they are healed.
Chapter 6.
Concerning the cauterization of a cough.
A cough which is cured with cauterization is only of those types which come from phlegm, as we have said in the divisions of illnesses.
Avoid cauterization of the type of cough that originates from dryness or from a spasm of the nerve.
When you treat this type of cough with leeches and purgatives and gargles, and your treatment is not effective, then you should cauterize the patient with three cauteries:  one should be at the root of the ear, the second a little below the temple, and the third at the junction of the two lips.
And place your cautery on the opposite side of the affected area, because softening [or relief] only comes to the seemingly healthy part.
And the method of cauterization is that you cauterize it with the cautery at the very tip of the superior ear, a little above the corner of the head, and another in the temple, the length of which should be according to the length of the index finger, and keep your hand with the cautery there until half the thickness of a quill pen is burned.
And this is the form of the cautery, and it is a kind of small knife, the form of which is shown above/previously. However, it is more subtle to do it a little like this; and it is necessary to cut away a little of the superfluous thickness.
Then care for the area with what has been previously described until it is healed.
Chapter 7.
On the cauterization of chronic apoplexy.
When apoplexy is chronic and you have treated it with what we have said and your treatment is ineffective and the patient does not have a fever, then cauterize the patient with four cauteries: one on each of the temples of their head; and one in the middle of the head, as we have said; and one on the neck. And the manner of cauterization is done as described above.
And it is also cauterized above the stomach; and it is further behind; and then let it be treated with what follows.
Chapter 8.
On cauterization for forgetfulness caused by phlegm.
So that large enemas and purifying pills for the brain are given to the patient at the beginning.
And then shave the patient's entire head and apply to their neck a plaster of cetre as described in the treatise on plasters.
And apply that several times, for that is a manner of cauterization. And do this according to the same order which I have already mentioned.
And therefore if he is healed by these means, it is good; and if not, then make for him three cauterizations on the back part of his head, which descend from the highest part of the head to the lowest part of the neck. And place between each cauterization the width of a finger, and then treat the cauterization with what has been prescribed.
But if you add [more medicine] and the patient tolerates it, then apply cauterization to the middle location; and if you want a stronger application, then cauterize on both sides (or temples).
Then treat it until it is healed; and let the cauterization be olive-shaped according to the form given before.
Chapter 9.
Concerning the cauterization of paralysis and the softening of the whole body.
As you proceed in the purification of the head with leeches and with what we have said; and then shave the patient's head, and then cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with a cautery on all the temples of the head, and with a cautery on the back part of it, and with three cauteries on the vertebrae of the neck.
And if it is necessary in the illness of softening of the body to find that and the patient tolerates it and the illness is severe, then cauterize it with four cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and finally with a cautery until it is covered with sweat from the patient, and raise your hand. And then care for it until it is healed, and this should be an olive-oil cautery.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the cauterization of epilepsy.
The epileptic is not cauterized unless that epilepsy is due to phlegm.
And therefore it is necessary that you purify his brain at the beginning with herbs and with the other treatment which we have described in the section when the patient is strong and can tolerate the purging of medicines.
And if it is a child who cannot tolerate medicines, then administer gargles and masticatories that purify the brain, before that for many days, with improvement of the diet.
Then shave the head of the patient, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head according to the previous method, and with another cautery on his occiput, and cauterize above each temple. And if the patient is strong and can tolerate it, then cauterize him with the cauteries which I have mentioned, having paralysis and softening of the body over the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back.
And let the cautery be olive-shaped, as previously described.
But if the patient is a child, then apply a small cautery according to this method.
Chapter 11.
On the cauterization of melancholia.
When the causes of melancholia are corrupt humors and a thick phlegm, then cauterize that with the cauteries which we have described in the treatment of paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholia is a superfluity inclining towards melancholic humors, and the patient's body is humid, then give him to drink that which cleanses his brain, according to what has preceded in the division [of treatments].
Then shave the patient's head, and then make a decently-sized poultice for him, and apply that to the middle of his head.
And the patient should be seated upright and held firmly on all sides.
Then apply a liniment; and it is most worthy to use old sheep's tallow. And heat that over the fire with a moderate temperature, which the said sufferer will feel when it is applied to him. Then pour that onto the middle of his head within the circle, and leave it there until it cools.
And do this to the patient once a week, along with his other treatment, until he is healed; or if you wish, cauterize it, making small punctures according to the need, provided that you hold your hand with a cautery thus prepared: this is a type of cautery that moistens the brain evenly.
Then apply that cotton soaked in sheep's tallow or goose grease.
Chapter 12.
Concerning the cauterization of water descending into the eye.
When the beginning of water in the eye is declared to you by the signs which I have mentioned in the division, then begin and give the patient something to drink that will purify his head, and keep it from all humidity.
And make him sweat in a bath for several days.
And then order him to shave his head, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head; and then cauterize it with two cauteries on the two temples if the beginning of the flow of water is in both eyes, or on one side if the beginning of the water is in one eye.
Incise with a cautery all the veins and arteries which are below the temples; and let the cauterizations be far apart in the width of the temples.
And beware of a flow of blood when it is cauterized; and if you see a flow of blood from it, stop it with some medicine, which is possible for you.
And I come with the wisdom that is in the extraction of arteries and with the excision of them and the caution of blood flow.
And sometimes it is cauterized in the neck below the two bones with two cauteries placed separately.
Chapter 13.
Concerning the cauterization of old tears (likely referring to chronic sores or ulcers).
When the tears of the eyes are old and persistent, and through the veins and arteries which are in the manifest location of the head, and it is verified that this is a superfluity of cold phlegmatic humors, then cauterize that with the cautery which we recounted in the beginning chapter concerning water falling into the eyes, and with a cautery in the middle location of the head and with two cauteries in the neck below the two temples, and with two cauteries in the neck below the two bones. And if it requires additional treatment, then cauterize it in all the area of the corner of the eye above the end of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Chapter 14.
Concerning the cauterization of nasal odor (or foul smell from the nose).
When you treat this with what we have said in the division and your treatment is not effective, then you prepare and give the patient decoctions to drink for three nights; and then shave his head, and cauterize it with a moderate-sized cautery, with an olive-shaped cautery. And then cauterize that with a clove-shaped cautery, with two cauteries above the two eyebrows, a little below the hair.
And beware of the artery, that you do not burn it.
And of the clove-shaped cautery, this is the form.
Chapter 15.
Of the cautery of mollification of the eyelids of the eyes.
When the eyelid is softened by illness or by humidity, then cauterize that eyelid with a lunar cautery; and this is its form:
And if you cauterize that, above the two eyebrows a little, with two cauteries in each part, and let them be elongated from the temples. And let the length of each cautery be according to the size of the eyebrow.
And do not press your hand too much with the cautery, but only to the extent that a third part of the eyelid is hardened.
Chapter 16.
Concerning the cauterization of the eyelid of the eye when the hairs are turned inward and prick the eye.
When the hairs of the eye are turned inward so that they prick the eye, cauterization in these cases is done in two ways, for cauterization is either done with fire or with a burning medicine.
If you cauterize it with fire, instruct the patient beforehand to shave his hairs as those who cleanse them do, until they are lengthened and evened; and if it pricks him near their roots, then you bind his eyes with a bandage so that he is not bothered until the hairs have grown.
Then, when they are grown and evened, place the patient's head in your lap, and then mark on the eyelid of his eyes with a probe, with a mark which is shaped like a myrtle leaf, and let the beginning of the mark be near the hairs.
And then place lint soaked in egg white or in quince mucilage on the eye.
And then heat the cautery, of which this is the form.
And then cauterize over the figure that you have marked, little by little, in many times, until the surface of the skin which is like a myrtle leaf is burned, completely apparent.
And a sign of the success of your work is that you see the eyelid of the eye already contracted and the hairs of it raised above the puncture of the eye.
Then raise your hand and leave it like that for three days, and then place a bandage over it until the scar is healed. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if some matter returns through the hairs near their origin and the eyelid is softened, then perform the cautery again on that area in the same way as you did initially.
But if the hairs are below the eyelid, then cauterize that area until it is raised to its natural position and is even, and the hairs do not prick the eye.
But cauterize with a burning medicine; this means that you instruct the patient to pull the hairs until they are elongated and even, and then a leaf-shaped bandage of cloth is made, shaped like a myrtle leaf.
Then take known soap and quicklime, about one ounce of each, and mix those ingredients well, and do this quickly so that it does not get cold; and then spread this mixture onto the cloth which is shaped like a myrtle leaf, and place that over one or both eyes; and place under the eye a cotton pad soaked in egg white, and the patient's head should be in your lap.
And place your finger on the medicine and press it gently, but do not move it until the patient feels the bite of the medicine, for the bite is like fire.
And when you perceive that he feels the burning, then release the medicine and rub it with your finger; when the burning ceases, then remove the medicine and wash the eye with water; and then observe if you see that the eyelid is already raised, as if washed by cautery or incision, for then it is well; and if not, return to applying the medicine to the place where the medicine has not yet taken effect and does not darken until your work is even and the eye is contracted.
Then place cotton with a bandage over it until the scab from the cauterization is removed, and then treat it with healing ointment or with another until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you, during your operation, to watch with vigilant diligence that nothing of the medicine falls into the eye, and if the eyelid after a few days is softened and some of the hairs stick to the eye, then repeat your medicine on that part of the eyelid which is softened, exactly as you did at the beginning, and then cover it again until it is healed.
And this is the method for myrtle leaf (treatment).
And know that the eyes of men are diversified according to their size, and therefore your treatment should be adjusted accordingly.
And the way of rectitude will not be hidden from him who studies this art.
Chapter 17.
Concerning the cauterization of a fistula in the tear duct of the eye.
When you treat a fistula with what we have said in the divisions of diseases and your treatment is ineffective, then you must cauterize it in this manner: instruct the patient to place their head in your lap, and have a minister hold their head between your hands in such a way that their head is not moved or shaken.
And then place a pledget soaked in egg white or gum arabic on their eye.
And then heat the cautery, of which this is the form:
which should be concave, like the shape of a reed or a vulture's feather, at the end with which the cauterization is performed.
And if you wish, let it be penetrating to the other end. And if you wish, let it be hollow like a probe.
However, the concave one is better for your work.
Then press the fistula if it is open, and draw out the pus and dry it. And if it is not open, then open it and extract the corruption from it.
Then place a cautery, well ignited, upon it, and hold your hand a little below the cautery, from the eye to the side of the nose, so that your hand does not stray or the patient is disturbed. Then place the cautery on the hailstone in the eye and cauterize it.  (Note:  "grays del huel" likely refers to a hailstone-like growth or lesion in the eye).
And so, if at the beginning your cautery penetrates to the bone, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice, if necessary; and leave it thus for three days, and then apply a bandage over it with drying ointment until it is healed.
But if ten days have passed and it is not healed, then apply a sharp, corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed and scraped as is appropriate in its chapter.
And there is another method of cauterizing a fistula which some earlier [doctors] described:  Approach the location of the fistula and open it, and then place into that fissure a fine tube, which is shaped like this: And pour into it the quantity of one ounce of melted lead; and hold your hand with a [tool, possibly forceps] with good retention, and don't hurt the patient so that the melted lead does not reach his eye; and it is necessary that you place upon the patient's eye a compress soaked in egg white or water, because the lead will adhere to the place of the fistula and heal it with marvelous healing.
And if the fistula is healed by what we have said of the cautery and the treatment, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to administer a perforation of the nose and reduce the fistula to the body of the nose as it will come to its proper place.
Chapter 18.
Of the cauterization of the fissure of the wells [likely referring to sores or ulcers].
Many times fissures occur on the lips, and most commonly on the lips of children.
Therefore, if you treat these fissures with what we have said in the divisions and your treatment is not effective, then ignite a small, knife-like cautery according to this form that is shown:
And let its concavity be according to the thinness of a knife.
Then carefully place that instrument in those fissures until the cautery reaches the bottom of the fissures.
And then treat that with cerots until it is healed.
Chapter 19.
Concerning the cauterization of a fistula in the mouth.
When in the root of the uvula, or in the palate, or in the roots of the teeth, an abscess develops and then breaks, and then becomes chronic, and pus flows and a fistula is formed, and then you treat it and the treatment is not effective, then you should apply a cautery according to the size of the fistula, and then place that heated cautery into the opening of the fistula, and hold your hand there until the heated iron reaches the bottom of it, and then do this once or twice; and then treat it with what we have said about treatment until it is healed.
And so if the wound is incised and heals, it is good; but if not, then it is necessary to open the area and remove the corrupted bone as it will be described in its chapter.
Chapter 20.
Concerning the cauterization of teeth and the softening of gums.
When the gums are softened by moisture and the teeth are loose, and you treat them with medicines and it doesn't work, then place the patient's head in your lap, and then heat the cautery, the shape of which will be described later, in such a way that you place the small cup over the tooth and quickly introduce the heated cautery into it, and hold your hand there for a short time until the patient feels the heat of the fire has reached the root of the tooth.
And then remove your hand, and then repeat the cauterization several times as needed.
And then let the patient fill their mouth with salt water and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out, because the loose tooth is fixed, the softened gum is tightened, and the corrupt moisture is dried.
Chapter 21.
On the cauterization of toothache.
When toothache is due to coldness, or there is cold or worms in it, and you do not succeed in curing it with medicine, then cauterize it in one of two ways: either cauterize with a cow's horn [presumably a heated horn], or cauterize with fire.
Cauterization with a horn is done by taking a cow's horn and boiling it in a pot of iron. Then take cotton and wrap it around the end of a probe, then dip it in the boiling horn, and apply it quickly to the aching tooth until it cools.
Then repeat this several times until the power of the fire reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, dip wool or cotton in the aforementioned horn, and place that on the aching tooth, and place ignited iron on top of it until the iron reaches the bottom of the tooth's cavity.
However, cauterization with fire is done by using a small tube of brass or iron, and ensure that there is some thickness in the center of it so that the sharpness of the fire does not reach the patient's mouth.
Then heat the cautery to the desired shape, and apply it to the tooth, holding your hand there until the cautery cools; and do this several times because the pain will be reduced either that day or the next.
And it is necessary that, for the application of that cautery, the patient fill his mouth with good wine and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out; and this is the form of the cautery.
And cauterize with whichever extremity you wish, as is possible for you.
And this is the shape of the cannula:
Chapter 22.
Concerning the cauterization of extrophuli (likely referring to a type of skin eruption or lesion).
When extrophuli are caused by phlegm and cold humors and are not responsive to digestion by medicines, and you wish to digest them mildly, then ignite the cautery, the shape of which is as follows: it has two open ends so that smoke escapes from one end of the cautery through the other end, and place this ignited cautery upon that abscess once, and again if needed, until it reaches the bottom of the abscess.
And if the abscess is small, then apply the cautery according to the size of the abscess, and then leave it for three days and apply a cotton swab soaked in butter over it until what the fire has burned is softened.
And then treat it with ointment and bandages until it is healed.
Chapter 23.
Concerning the cauterization of hoarseness and the constriction of the throat.
When humors gather in the pulmonary passage, and especially when this is accompanied by coldness of constitution, then the patient should be evacuated initially with laxative medicines, and then cauterize below the root of the throat in the constricted area.
And be careful not to reach the throat with the cautery, nor burn his windpipe, but only a little.
And cauterize that area with another cautery near the junction of the neck at his last vertebra. And let his cauterization be done with a nail, following the previous manner.
And then treat that with what has been prescribed until it is healed.
Chapter 24.
Concerning the cauterization of lung disease and cough.
When cough and lung disease are caused by cold dampness, and the patient has no fever or consumption [tuberculosis] and the disease is chronic, then cauterize with two cauteries on the clavicle in the previously mentioned places, long and wide; and with another cauterization in the middle of the chest between the two nipples; and let the cautery be a nail according to the previous method.
And if you wish, your cauterization can be done by puncturing with the cautery that is called a puncture, the form of which precedes in the puncturing of earache.
And let the punctures be deep in those places; and then cure it with the same remedies that were used before until it is healed.
And let the cautery have three prongs because cauterization is done with it:  with one application you can make three cauterizations according to this method:
Chapter 25.
Concerning the cauterization of the testicle, which is the head of the penis.
When the head of the penis is dislocated because of lubricous humors and is not fixed in the moment of its return from its dislocation until it is in the habit of returning, and then with a small movement it is dislocated again so that it swells, as we have testified, then first you must reduce the dislocation, and then the patient should be turned on his back or on his healthy side; and then raise the scrotum which is in the inner part of the testicle to the upper parts with the fingers of your left hand, if the joint is dislocated towards the inner parts.
And then heat the cautery which has two heads; and this is its form:
And then cauterize the scrotum until it penetrates from one side to the other; and the figure of the cauterization will then be of four cauterized heads.
And sometimes it is adjusted according to this count and therefore there are eight cauteries.
And then place over the cautery cooked porridge with salt, and then keep the patient calm, and the limb should not be moved for some time until it is healed.
And if the dislocation is in the upper parts and this is done late, then cauterize over the muscle with a nail-shaped cautery or with many pointed cauteries, because the joint is strengthened, the humidity is removed, and the patient is healed.
Chapter 26.
Concerning the cauterization of the stomach.
When cold and numerous humors enter the stomach, exceeding its capacity and multiplying within it, and if you continue the methods of treatment and do not cure it, then the patient should lie on their back, extend their hands and legs, and then cauterize that area with three cauteries: one cautery below the feet, the size of a finger, with a nail-shaped cautery, and two cauteries below on either side of the cautery, so that a triangular figure of cautery is formed; and space them out so that they do not touch, which would cause putrefaction.
And let the depth of the cautery be two-thirds the thickness of a finger.
And let the shape of the cautery be according to this form without addition. And if you wish, cauterize with a large cautery in the middle of the stomach according to this form; and this should be a cautery in the shape of a circle according to this form:
And let the stomach be cauterized, puncturing it where the cautery is feared: and it is that you mark on the stomach points according to the quantity you want with a probe, and then cauterize those points; and then treat it with the previously mentioned treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 27.
On the cauterization of the cold liver.
When pain arises in the liver due to coldness, or humidity, or excessive flatulence, so that it is thrown out of its normal composition by superfluous egress, and the patient is treated with what we have said and it does not help, then it is necessary that the patient be supine on his back, and mark with a probe three cauteries according to this form and this same quantity on the liver under the hypochondria, where the body lies.
And let the space which is between each cautery be according to the thickness of the finger, and let the cautery be according to the length of the right index finger.
And do not press too much with the cautery.
And if the quantity of what is burned is the amount of half the width of the little finger, and no more, and if the patient is standing on his feet; and if he is not standing, then let him lie down, having already spread his legs, and raise his arms.
And it is possible for you to cauterize these areas with a knife-shaped cautery, when you possess subtlety and prudence in the art.
And be careful not to proceed too much in the cauterization, and let the width of the whole little finger be burned so that the whole body is burned and it reaches the intestines, because the little finger is small here; and know this.
Chapter 28.
Concerning the perforation of a liver abscess with a cautery.
When an ulcer or abscess develops in the liver and you want to know if that abscess is in the liver's flesh or in its tunic, then if it is in the liver's flesh, the patient experiences heaviness and dull pain.
And if it is in the liver's tunic, the pain is very sharp and sometimes even exhausts the doctors in its treatment.
Therefore, the patient should be turned onto their side; and then mark the location of the abscess with a probe; and then ignite the cautery, which is shaped like a rake, the form of which was shown on the previous page. And cauterize with that cautery until the entire area is burned, until it reaches the pus, until healthy matter emerges.
And then treat it with the treatment for other abscesses until it is healed.
And it is not fitting that this type of cauterization be used except by one whose study in the art of medicine is prolonged, and in whose hands many illnesses have been treated; however, let someone with some experience be bold in this operation, but this one is better left to us.
Chapter 29.
On the cauterization of pleurisy.
The ancients said that a cautery made from the roots of birthwort was effective against cold pleurisy in this manner: that you take a long, dry root of birthwort, as long as can be found, and of the thickness of a finger.
Then immerse it in oil and heat it in the fire, and cauterize with a cautery in the place that is between the continuation of the clavicle and the neck; and with two small cauteries below the chin, slightly leaning towards the part below the beard, and with two cauteries in the area that is between the fifth and sixth rib, leaning slightly towards the back; and with another cautery in the middle of the chest; and with another on the stomach; and with three cauteries on the back; and with one cautery in the place that is between the two shoulders; and with two cauteries on the two sides of the chest, below the cautery which is between the two shoulders.
And it is not necessary to press your hand with the cautery, but let it be evident in the skillful operation.
And some ancients said that some men used an iron cautery shaped like a ray and heated it, and inserted it into the place that is between the ribs, until it reached the abscess and was purged of its sickness, just as we have said in the case of an abscess of the liver.
But this perforation with a cautery is not without danger, for one may easily cause death or a fistula in the area, which is incurable.
Chapter 30.
Concerning the cauterization of the spleen.
When you treat the illness of the spleen with what we have said concerning treatment in the division and it does not improve, then cauterization is appropriate in three ways, all of which are correct. One of these is to make three or four cauteries in a line, according to the length of the spleen, following the shape of the cauteries for the liver, the shape of which has been previously described.
And let there be between each cautery the width of a finger or a little more, and the manner of cauterization should be the same as we described for the cauterization of the liver.
And do not press your hand with the cautery; and let the patient lie on their side.
And another way of cauterization is to ignite the cautery having two points, which we described in the chapter on muscle dislocation, and raise the cautery which is at the location of the spleen where the patient's left side lies.
And let your elevation, with which you raise the cautery, be according to the length of the body, so that cauterizations are made according to the width of the body.
And then insert the two points, well-ignited, into that space so that with them you penetrate the cautery from the other side.
And then extract the cautery; and thus four cauterizations are made.
And if you wish, cauterize with a cautery having three points.
And then you care for the cautery after it has stopped shedding pus for many days, for this is more important than the other preceding causes in the healing process.
Chapter 31.
On the cauterization of a dropsical patient.
Cauterization is not used for dropsy, except in a certain specific instance.  (The "3" after "alcinte" is likely a scribal error or annotation and doesn't affect the meaning).
When you treat a dropsical patient with the methods of treatment that we have described in the division and your treatment is ineffective, then you should cauterize with four cauteries in a circle around the navel, and with one cautery over the stomach, and with another cautery over the liver, and with another cautery over the spleen, and with two cauteries between the vertebrae of the back, and one cautery on the right foot, and with another cautery over the stomach. And the depth of the cauterization should be approximately equal to the thickness of a finger.
And then leave the cauterization open so that it discharges pus for a long time.
And do not remove the patient from treatment after cauterization with what is necessary, for two intentions must be added to him, and his healing should be hastened with help.
The shape of the cautery with which the abdomen is cauterized should be nail-shaped, as its shape is described above; and that with which the back is cauterized should be olive-shaped, as its shape is described above.
Chapter 32.
Concerning the cauterization of the feet and thighs.
When the feet are swollen with dropsy and filled with yellowish water, it is necessary to cauterize the feet on the upper side of the foot, not in the space between the little toe and the ring finger; and raise your hand with the cautery and do not twist it; and then raise your hand, and do not entirely remove it, because the yellowish water will flow out.
The cautery is according to this form: Then make two cauterizations on both thighs, and on each thigh a cauterization should be made with the knife-like end of the cautery. And let the cautery be according to the length of the thigh: one below the knee and the other below the middle of the thigh; and on each thigh two cauteries.
And leave the wound open without dressing for a long time so that some water may drain from it.
And then treat it with those things which we have mentioned.
Chapter 33.
Concerning the cauterization of the abdomen.
When the abdomen is cold and damp to the point that it weakens the virtues—that is, the digestive and retentive virtues which are in the stomach and intestines—and this is treated with methods of healing and does not improve, and sometimes the patient suffers needing cauterization to exhibit virtue, then cauterize that with a large cautery over his stomach as previously described in the cauterization of the stomach, with a circular cautery and with four small cauteries in the circuit of the navel, with a thin, nail-like cautery; and with a cautery over the hypochondrium, above the large loins, or with two cauteries.
And if sometimes the humors are exuberant and the patient tolerates it, then cauterize that over the pubis and over the intestines, and quickly and strongly applying two cauteries over the stomach and near the groins, for this is a perfecting cure of benefit from which you will not err.
Chapter 34.
Concerning the cauterization of old hemorrhoids.
When in their nature there are old hemorrhoids or one of thick, cold humors or corrupted humors, and they are treated with what we have said in the division and your treatment does not improve, then cauterize with three cauteries over the lower part of the coccyx, small and slightly triangular, and with a cautery above the navel, falling between two fingers.
And if you fear that his stomach is cold and his food is not digested, and you see his face inflamed, then cauterize that over the stomach with a large cautery as previously described; and with one cautery on the liver, and with another cautery on his spleen with a nail-like cautery, and leave the cautery open for some time, and then treat him until he is healed.
Chapter 35.
Concerning the cauterization of a fistula near its excision.
When you excise a fistula, then you ignite the cautery that resembles a ray (or, a spoke/rod). Then insert that ignited [cautery] into that excised fistula, and apply your hand until the cautery reaches the opening of the vein from which the blood comes.
And do this once or twice, for if the fistulas are numerous, then cauterize each of them as we have recounted, and then treat them with ointments until they are healed.
And if you cauterize with a large cautery on a certain part, it is slower in healing.
Chapter 36.
Chapter 36.  On the cauterization of fistulas that are naturally formed in the private parts.
When nature does not heal the area as we have described, and the patient is frightened by it, it should be treated with cautery.
Therefore, when someone has an old fistula in the private parts, and it is running with pus and corrupt humors, then what is most fitting is to probe it with a thin probe, and then ascertain the depth with the aforementioned probe.
Then ignite the cautery which is shaped like a ray (or, a pointed cautery). And then insert that ignited cautery into the fistula, according to the depth that the probe indicated.
And repeatedly apply the cautery until those corrupt tissues are burned, once or twice or three times, depending on your judgment of what is necessary.
And beware of burning nerves and veins; or if the fistula goes towards the bladder or towards the intestine, then beware of all those areas; and do not do this unless the fistula is in fleshy tissue and you believe it is not penetrating deeper.
And then care for the area until it is healed.
And if the area is sealed, and the material has been excised from it, and it remains so for a long time, then you will know that it is completely cured.
And if the materials have not been removed, then know that it is penetrating or in the depths of the corrupted bone, or that it comes from those causes which will be recalled in their place (later in the text).
Chapter 37.
Concerning the cauterization of sores.
When in sores pain arises from cold or from great inflammation, and because of this the patient's body is weakened, then it is necessary to cauterize that sore with a cautery over each sore with a nail-shaped cautery, of which the preparation (or mention) was previously given.
And quickly cauterize again a little below, so that three linear cauterizations result, and then they will be more helpful in the healing.
Chapter 38.
Concerning the cauterization of the bladder.
When weakness and softening occur in the bladder due to cold and humidity, such that the patient cannot retain urine, then cauterize that area in the lowest part above the bladder, where the pubic hairs begin, and cauterize with one cautery on the right side, and with another on the left.
And let the length of each cauterization be according to the size of the walnut; and cauterize that area with a cautery in the lowest part of the back, or with two cauteries if necessary.
And let the cautery be a nail-shaped instrument, as is appropriate/customary.
Chapter 39.
Concerning the cauterization of the hand.
When cold and dampness affect the hand, and because of this conception is prevented, and her menses are corrupted, and their onset is delayed, and pain occurs before their arrival, then it is necessary that the area be cauterized with three cauteries in a circle around the navel, as we have described in the cauterization of the bladder; and with a cautery on the area below the back, or with two cauteries, and let the cautery be nail-shaped.
Chapter 40.
Concerning the cauterization of a dislocated hip.
When mucous humors are poured into the area of the hip, and are the cause of its displacement, and a sign of this is that one thigh is longer than the other when compared. And the location of the dislocation is found where it bulges.
Then it is necessary that you cauterize the patient on the part of their hip with a circular cautery, after you have marked with a probe the circumference of the area as it is swollen, so that the area falls within the middle of the circle.
And let the cautery be of the kind previously described for the cauterization of the stomach.
And if this cautery is not at hand, then cauterize it with three cauteries using a large olive-shaped cautery.
And let the cautery be done according to the size of the thickness of the whole leg; and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 41.
On the cauterization of the sciatic vein.
When pain comes in the hip area, and it is caused by cold and dampness, the patient is treated with what we have said and do not disagree, and this is ancient practice; then you must purge the patient of thick humors with foul-smelling pills or with artificial or similar pills; and then cauterize it.
And the manner of cauterization in that case is according to two methods: either cauterization with burning medicines, or cauterization with fire.
If according to two known methods, of which one is that you cauterize that area on the patient's hip with three triangular cauteries according to this form; and make them of good depth. And let the space which is between each cautery be the amount of the thickness of a finger, whether the cautery is olive-shaped or large.
But sometimes let the cautery be in the middle of the place, on the head of the hip, so that there are four cauteries.
And if you wish, cauterize with the circle whose form precedes in the cauterization of the hip dislocation, that is, let it be a continuous circle and burn the thickness of the whole hip joint.
And this is the method of cauterization in sciatica when the pain is adherent to the hip and is not extended to the thigh.
But if it is extended to the thigh, then cauterize that with two cauteries on the thigh, on the place to which the patient points as being the location of the pain; and with one cautery on the groin, about four fingers slightly below the left side.
And if the cautery is knife-shaped, let the depth of the cautery be according to the thickness of the hip joint.
And if the patient indicates that the pain is extended to the area near the toes, then cauterize there where indicated with a cautery in three or four points, or more if needed.
And if the patient indicates the pain to be below the buttock towards the thigh, then cauterize there with a knife-shaped cautery.
And be careful in all cauterizations that you do not burn the nerves or large arteries with the cautery, lest this cause harm or contracture to the patient.
And I have seen one and another of those who were cauterized on the ankle and burned deeply during cauterization, and the leg swelled until the tumor reached the foot, and the whole foot was perforated and corrupted. And then a strong case of diarrhea occurred, and then death.
And if the pain is in two places at once, cauterize each according to this manner.
And the wise men say concerning the cauterization of the ankle, concerning the cautery of which this is the manner: make a circle of iron resembling the half of a palm in circumference; and let the opening be according to the thickness of a date pit, or a little less.
And within that circle let there be another third; and let the space which is between them be the quantity of a hazelnut; and let those circles be open on two sides; and let the length of those iron pieces be a hazelnut or two hazelnuts, and make in those iron pieces decently prepared marks on the circles; and this is its form:
Then let it be heated in the fire until it glows red and throws off sparks, and then let it be placed on the part of the ankle, the patient lying on their side. Therefore cauterize that with three round cauteries at once.
Then leave that for three days; then plaster it with a bandage and leave the wound open for many days; and then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
Says Albucasis: Oh, son, of this type of cautery we have used little, because of the fear of its application, and because we have found few who dare to apply it.
But of the good cauteries to him who suffers them, and find with that the location of it.
But cauterize with adherent medicines such that you make two circles having perforated armour in which the point of the iron tool is inserted; the length of the walls in height should be two nuts or thereabouts.
And if the space which is between each is the quantity of the thickness of a finger; and let them be open below and above, and let them contain themselves between them according to this form:
Then place over the affected part of the hip, the patient lying on his healthy side, and press your hand upon the affected part of the hip; then take warm water and pour it between the two circles, and the quality of that should be with little fire little by little, and hold it for the quantity of an hour, and let the patient suffer the biting and burning of it—for this produces a burning sensation like that of fire—until the burning ceases.
Then raise your hand with the instrument and wipe the warm water from the whole hip with fresh water.
Then leave it for three days and place a poultice on it until the blackness of the burn is softened, and leave it for some days, and pus will drain from it; this is the final treatment.
Then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if the pain extends to the thigh or leg, apply to it a bath containing water, in the same way as it was done for the hip.
And this is the method of warm water, and I have already written about it in the treatise on the rectification of medicines. Take alkali and quicklime, equal parts of each, and crush them and put them in a new pot, the bottom of which is perforated with a small hole, according to the amount that will go into the ray (presumably a kind of instrument or container).
And place under the bottom of the pot another glazed pot, and pour over the alkali and quicklime fresh water which covers them by a finger's breadth; and then when you have compressed them with your hand with good compression, let the sharp water descend from it into the glazed pot below.
And then collect all that water, and pour it over fresh quicklime and alkali, for then it will be a very strong lye, which is used in many medical operations and in cauterizing other members, because it operates like fire.
And some medicines with which cauterization is also performed are:  honey, vinegar, and quicklime with soap, mixed together.
And Galen recounted an operation and cure from an ancient physician by which sciatic nerve and vein pain is cured, and he greatly praised it, saying it needed no other treatment, and that it is healed at once, as he recounted, for that man entered a bath and was immediately healed from it.
And it is so that fresh rue should be taken, and if fresh is not found, a fresh fig should be taken; and it should be well crushed with a little fat and placed on the hip where the pain is, or on the thigh, or on the leg, and bound on.
And it should be left on for three hours or until the patient feels relief from the burning.
Then put him in a bath, and when his body is reddened, expose him to the sun, for the pain ceases and he is healed.
And know that one should not use cautery with these medicines except after the evacuation of the body.
But Dioscorides said that when the varicose vein is cauterized with goat's dung, it benefits him well; and the cautery should be applied in this manner: wool should be taken and soaked in old oil, and placed between the toes and the ankle.
Then take dried goat dung and burn it until it becomes charcoal; and then place it on the wool and leave it until it is extinguished. Then repeat this, and do not cease doing this until the sensation of burning reaches the ankle and relieves the pain.
And this method of cautery is called cautery of embers (or glowing coals).
Chapter 42.
Of the cauterization of toothache.
Sometimes pain is in the teeth due to various causes, such as a blow or a percussion, or excessive evacuation, and causes similar to those, by infusion of cold and humid matter.
And cauterization is only suitable for that single type which is caused by the effusion of cold matter.
Therefore, after evacuation of the patient with purging pills and similar,  cauterizations should be made on the tooth where the pain is located, three lines according to its width, provided that you mark with ink, in each line, five cauterizations or more, according to the patient's tolerance and their constitution.
And the cauterization should be done with a pointed cautery.
And if you wish, cauterize with three or four cauteries using a nail-shaped cautery according to this form:
Chapter 43.
On cauterization at the beginning of a swelling/tumor.
This illness often happens to small children; and a sign of its beginning in children is that it occurs in their limbs with elevation and movement.
And you find in the last vertebra of the back and neck that it already begins to rise above the other vertebrae.
And so when you see this and want to prevent it, then cauterize that with a cautery which is circular according to this form: so that the cautery takes in all parts of the vertebra equally.
And if you wish, cauterize in the area around the vertebra with the pointed cautery two or three lines. And let the points be close to each other.
And then treat the area until it is healed with what we have said.
But beware that cautery is not administered in swelling caused by a spasm of the nerves.
Chapter 44.
On the cauterization of gout and joint pain.
When joint pain is caused by cold humors which are infused into each member of the body, then when pain comes in the feet, it is customary for doctors to call this gout properly.
And so, if gout caused by cold is treated with a method of cure and the pains are not alleviated, then cautery alleviates them.
And it is so that you cauterize that area for evacuation in the area around the joint of the foot with many cauteries.
And let the cautery be olive-shaped, of which this is the form:
And if it is necessary that a puncture be made on the surface of the foot, then do it with a pointed cautery. And if the pains ascend to the knees or other joints, and this often happens, then cauterize that area on each knee with three or four cauteries on each side with this olive-shaped cautery.
And if many cauteries are necessary, then cauterize that area and do not press your hand too hard with the cautery beforehand; it should only be around the thickness of a finger.
And if the pains ascend to both knees, then administer the cautery which we have mentioned in its chapter.
And if the pains are only in the hands, and it is called  *suagra* (likely a type of hand ailment), then puncture in the circular area of the wrists, as if winding around, puncture in those two lines.
And if the pain remains in the fingers, then puncture in the area of the wrists on each side and on the palm of the hand.
And if the pains reach the elbows or muscles in a few days, then cauterize them in every part of the affected area.
And do not neglect the patient's good regimen and reception of medicines, for if good regimen is followed and the inflammation is evacuated with this cautery, he will be healed, God willing.
Chapter 45.
On the cauterization of fractures.
When a rupture occurs in the groin, and the intestines protrude before the scrotum, and this is beginning to happen, then you should order the patient to abstain from eating that day and to use emollient substances so that the rupture may subside from his belly.
Then hold the patient between your hands above his bottom, and command him to hold his breath until the scrotum or intestines are retracted.
Then return it with your fingers and gently reduce the rupture over the pubic bone, noting that the extremity should resemble a half-circle in the upper parts of the body.
Then heat the cautery, whose form is this:
until it is very red and throws off sparks. Then return the intestine or hernia to its cavity, and place your hand over the area so that the intestine does not protrude.
And you have already opened the space between the patient's thighs and placed a cushion underneath; and let another servant be at their legs, and another at their feet, who holds their hands.
Then place the cautery on that mark with your hand, holding the cautery straight, and hold it there until you reach with it the bone of the penis within the middle of the perineum; and repeat this another time if you have not reached the bone on the first attempt.
And observe wisely the position of the intestine during your cauterization, so that you do not burn that intestine, lest death or other great harm come upon the patient.
And know that if you do not reach the bone with the cautery, your operation is not complete.
And it is necessary that the cautery for children be small according to their size and measure.
Then care for the cauterized area for three days with a poultice until the burn scab is healed.
And afterwards treat it with other ointments until it is healed.
And the patient should lie on their back for forty days until the area and wound are sealed/healed.
And it is necessary that you give him nourishment according to the length of his treatment, which will cleanse his belly, and that the intestine does not protrude outwards during the effort of healing.
And then, when he wants to get up after forty days, apply a properly made bandage, and keep it on for another forty days.
And let him be moderate in work, and in the repletion of food and drink, and in vehement voice, for when he uses this entire regimen of healing he will be completely healed.
And I will describe the treatment of ruptures in other parts of the abdomen; and at the beginning, if you want them not to spread, then cauterize the rupture with a round cautery according to its size, and let that which is exuded from the cautery be like the third part of it; and then treat it with what we have said, and it is not unsuitable.
And some earlier authors said that the rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery according to this shape, since it is marked with a probe:
and a line is placed through the widest part of the rupture, and another line above it; and place your hand with a nail cautery, but the first cautery is lighter and better.
Chapter 46.
On the cauterization of torsion (or twisting).
When pain develops in some member due to a fall or percussion, and that pain persists for a long time and does not respond to treatment with medicines, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And let the cautery be adapted to each member according to its size and smallness, and its strength and weakness, and the location of the torsion and its pain.
Therefore, if it is healed by the first cautery, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery on it; for it is customary for those pains to be pointed from one member to the next closest, so it is necessary to pursue them with a cautery until the patient is healed.
Chapter 47.
On the cauterization of leprosy.
Lepers are sometimes helped greatly by cautery, especially if the type of leprosy is caused by putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.
Therefore, if you wish to cauterize a leper, consider whether the leprosy is spreading and whether it can be cured by what we have said in the division, and if you do not leave it, and you fear that the corruption might affect the whole body and its entire constitution, then cauterize it on the head with five cauteries: one in the middle of the head, and a second lower down, towards the forehead at the hairline; and two more above the ears (on the temples); and one behind, on the nape of the neck.
And finally, with the cautery, press your hand slightly inwards so that a small impression is made on the bone, and the scabs are dislodged, so that the exhalation of thick vapors is relieved; and cauterize the spleen as needed.
And if the leprosy is already widespread on the patient and manifestly apparent, then you must cauterize it with the cauteries we mentioned on the head; and with a cautery on the tip of the nose, and with two cauteries on the two cheekbones, and with two cauteries on two vertebrae of the neck, and with six cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and with one large one on the vertebrae near the sacrum, and one on the sacrum, and with two on the hips, and one on each hip, and with two on the groins and one on each groin, and with two on the knees, and with two on the muscles (thighs?), and two on the ankles of his feet; and cauterize it on all the joints of the fingers and toes, and on each heel of his feet, and on the wrists of his hands.
And be careful of the nerves that are on the heel, so as not to burn them.
And sometimes it is cauterized with one cautery on the pubic bone and with another on the stomach, and another on the liver.
And know that the more precisely you apply the cauteries, the more helpful and beneficial it is; and know that the patient does not feel pain from the cautery, as others do, because his body is already numb.
And the cauteries should be large or small according to the limbs and joints, as prescribed by the types of instruments.
Then treat the cautery with cooked barley flour mixed with honey and with other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
Concerning the cauterization of numbness (or stupor).
When a limb is numb or asleep and has been treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, and is not healed, then you should cauterize that numb limb with cauteries according to the size or smallness of the limb.
And let your cautery penetrate the thickness of the skin a little. And afterwards, treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And sometimes a numbness affecting the hands and feet, and the vertebrae of the back, is cauterized near the exit of the nerves which supply that limb, and the numbness is relieved.
But do not attempt this except one who is skilled in the anatomy of the limbs and the exit of the nerves moving the whole body.
Chapter 49.
Concerning the cauterization of albaras, which is morphea.  (Note:  "Albaras" and "morphea" likely refer to a skin condition; a more precise modern medical term would be needed for a definitive translation).
When albaras is chronic and you do not apply any medicinal ointments to it, then cauterize it with a cautery iron a little, according to the size and nature of the affected area, until the whiteness and its discoloration are relieved, and its color is changed.
Then treat it with flour of barley and lentils with rose oil and with arnagrosa leaves, and with pigeon blood or with ewe's blood, of each an equal part and all mixed together; and then apply a linen cloth over the area until it is healed. (Note: "arnagrosa" likely refers to a specific plant, the exact identification requires further botanical research).
Chapter 50.
Concerning the cauterization of cancer. (or a cancerous sore/growth)
When a cancer is beginning and you wish to remove it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery iron around its circumference as it is swollen.
And the wise men say that it should be cauterized with a pointed cautery iron in the middle; but this has not been seen by me, for I fear that it will be ulcerated, and I have already seen this happen on several occasions.
And it is more proper that it be cauterized around its circumference with a circle, as we have said, or with many cauteries.
Chapter 50.  (Assuming a standard Roman numeral system)
Concerning the cauterization of a swelling/tumor. (Adubilati likely refers to a type of swelling or tumor)
When a swelling is delayed in maturation, and this is due to the superfluity causing it, or due to the age of the patient, for he is old and decrepit, with little blood, or due to the time of year, and you wish to hasten the maturation of the swelling; then cauterize it around its circumference with many small, pointed cauteries; and then leave it, for it will hasten maturation.
But if you wish to perforate it with a cautery, then you heat the cautery of which this is the form: and place that in the middle of the swelling until it penetrates the skin.
And if the cautery is in that place which is lower down, so that the body of the arm may be relieved; and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 52.
Concerning the cauterization of a corrosive (or, a corroding sore/ulcer).
A corrosive is a corruption moving in the limb, which corrodes it as fire consumes dry wood.
Therefore, if you see this corrosive in a place that suffers cauterization with fire, then you immediately cauterize with many small, and large nails, according to what is appropriate in the place where the corrosive is.
And then cauterize it completely until you eradicate all the corrosion and no cause of it remains.
And then leave it for three days, and place on the cauterized areas crushed sulfur mixed with oil until the scabs are all eradicated and all the corruption.
And then treat it with flesh-generating ointment.
But if after three days you see that healthy flesh grows where there is no corruption, it is good; but if not, then repeat the cauterization on what remains in the corrupted areas.
And corrosion is sometimes treated with a sharp medicine, for that serves in place of cautery. But however, cautery with fire is of more easily obtained benefit.
