And I have already spoken of its treatment with sharp medicine in the division, and therefore take that when you have need of it.
Chapter 53.
Of the cauterization of nails and other similar things.
Many times there happens in the parts under the foot an illness which is called a nail, and it is a thick, inward-growing cause which hurts the foot; and cauterization in such cases is according to two methods, that is, cauterization with fire and cauterization with hot water.
But cauterization with fire is that you heat the concave cautery similar to a vulture's beak, which should be made of iron according to the size which contains the nail in its entirety, and should be of fine edges.
And then place that heated instrument upon the nail, and then move your hand with the cautery in a circle around the nail until the cautery reaches the depth of the nail. And leave it there for three days until it begins to shed pus. And then plaster it with crushed wild mallows mixed with salt, and leave the plaster on it for one night, for this will eradicate it from its roots.
And then treat the area with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
And if it does not heal, and this often happens on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet, then you should apply cautery with water to those areas.  Take a small tube of bronze or iron or a vulture's quill, and place it over the nail; then pour a small quantity of hot water into the tube, and hold your hand, and you should move it with the pressure of your hand completely, and the edge of the tube should be pressed against the root of the nail, and the hot water will find its way to the root of the nail; and let the patient endure the bite of the hot water for a little while, about an hour, and then remove it, for the nail with its roots will be eradicated.
And do this to those, and then to the others, until you have treated all of those which are in the body.
And then treat those, once the roots of the nails are eradicated, with those ointments which generate flesh.
Chapter 54.
On the cauterization of tremor.
When someone suffers from tremor due to cold in the nerves, or from a fever of a cold cause, or from another similar cause, then you should cauterize that person with four or five cauteries on the spinal vertebrae, one cautery between each vertebra; and with a cautery on their feet, and on their stomach, with an olive-shaped cautery.
And their tremor will subside, and hasten the healing of the cold illness.
Chapter 55.
On the cauterization of accidental swellings in the body.
When in the body there are swellings and pustules, which are formed from thick, corrupted matter; then at the beginning, as it appears, it should be cauterized on the head of the fistula with a thin cautery with myrtle wood, the end of which is ignited with fire; or with a long root of hellebore; or with a lenticular cautery.
And sometimes a cautery is also applied at the beginning of its expulsion in this manner; and it is not believed, and the superfluity is broken up by making these, and it heals from these.
However, this should be done near evacuation with phlebotomy (bloodletting).
Chapter 56.
Chapter 56. On the cauterization of blood flow accidentally caused by an arrow wound.
Often, there is bleeding from an arrow wound that is incised near the wound, which flows outwards, or near the incision of abscesses or cautery of the limb, and similar things, and it is difficult to stop that.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, then press with your hand on the opening of the arrow wound, and place your finger tightly on it many times until the flow of blood stops under your finger, and it does not come out.
Then put small and large olive-cauteries in the fire, many in number, and blow on them until they are very hot.
And then take one of them, small or large depending on the location and wound where the artery is broken, and apply it to that vein quickly after you have removed your finger, and hold the cautery there until that blood is stopped and restrained.
And if the blood is expelled from the opening of the artery when you remove your finger and remove the cautery, then take another cautery quickly from among those that are prepared in the fire, and do not cease to do this one after another until the blood is restrained.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is there, and that another harm comes to the patient.
And know that when blood flows from the artery, it is not possible to restrain that blood, especially when the artery is large, except in one of four ways: either with a cautery as we have said, or with extraction when it is not extracted, for when it is extracted the extremities are better cauterized and the blood is restrained, or that it be tied with thread or with a ligature by strong constriction, or that medicines be applied whose virtue is to restrain blood; and they should be tightened with a cushion with decent constriction.
But he who administers decent constriction with a ligature and with a stricture with cloths or by the application of burning substances and the like, does not confer it except late.
But if this happens to someone and the physician or medicine is not present, then gently open with the index finger over the opening of that vein as we have said, and attend to its strong compression until the blood is stopped from the upper part of the wound, and the finger is not removed from over the artery; and over the artery let it be sprinkled with very cold water until the blood is congealed and thickened; and consider what need there is of cautery or medicine.
Of incisions and perforations and cupping and of phlebotomy and of wounds and their similars.
We have already discussed in the first chapter every illness in which cautery with fire and with burning medicine is suitable, and its occasions and causes, and the necessary instruments with the forms of the cauteries; and I have ordered the chapters from the head down to the feet.
And I will proceed in this chapter in the same manner, so that the one who seeks this will be aided, then concerning that which he will have asked.
And before I begin this, it is necessary, son, that you learn that in this chapter is what is in the first chapter on cauterization, but in a less terrifying way. (or "...but more terrifying than in the first chapter on cauterization" depending on intended nuance of "terror")
And because it is necessary that there be great caution, for in the operations which are in this chapter, many times there is evacuation which is with blood, with which life is maintained, through the opening of a vein or incision upon an abscess, or opening of a fistula, or purification of a wound, or extraction of an arrow, or incision of a stone, and the like. And with all of these are accompanied fear and trembling, and in most of them there is danger of death.
And I admonish you that this hesitation should not fall upon you, for sometimes it happens through your handling of men with many kinds of illnesses. And of them there is one who is angry with his illness and death is light for him, and this is because of the vehemence of what he finds in his illness, and because of the length of his illness, and with the illness is a fear which signifies death.
And of those there is one who gives you his possessions and says, you hoping for his health, and his illness is pernicious.
Therefore it is not fitting that you consent to that which comes to you from him of whom this manner is; and let your caution be greater than covetousness and your greed; and do not presume anything about this except through certain knowledge which verifies this to you, and to which a laudable success may follow.
And use in the cure of all your patients prognostication and indication of him to whom health is restored, for in that is to you help in the acquisition of fame and memory, of praise and of glory.
O, sons, may God inspire in you his understanding and not withhold from you direction and protection, for this is in his hand.
And I have ordered this chapter, distinguishing it by chapters as preceded in the chapter on cauteries, from the care of the head to the feet, so that this which you ask for and of which you have need may be relieved for you.
Chapter one.
Of the treatment of the water which is contained in the heads of children.
Know that this illness often happens to children at birth and when the nurse constricts the child's head without gentleness.
And sometimes it happens for a hidden cause which is unknown.
But I have only seen this illness in children; and in all those whom I saw, death followed, and thus it is, in my view, a deadly affliction for them.
And I have seen that a child whose head was filled with water, and his head was always thus enlarged until he could no longer support himself because of the size of his head, and moreover, the humidity increased until he died.
And this humidity is either accumulated between the skull and the bone, or it is accumulated below the bone on the brain.
Because the operation in that case, if the humidity is between the skull and the bone and the abscess is small, then it is necessary that you make an incision in the middle of the head with a fissure in the width, and let the length of the fissure be about two nuts so that the humidity flows out, and this is the form of the spatula:
And so if the humidity is more increased and the abscess is larger, then there are two fissures in this manner:
And if the humidity is below the bone, the sign of it is that you see the sutures of the head opened on all sides and the water is squeezed out when you compress it with your hand on the inner parts, and this is not hidden from you. It is necessary that you make an incision in the middle of the head with three fissures in this manner:
and then extract all the humidity from the incision; and then tighten the fissures with cloths and cotton wool; and then apply a dressing of wine and oil until the fifth day, and then loosen the bandage and treat the wound with tents and ointments, and bind the head evenly; and let the patient eat dry food with little humidity until the member is strengthened and healed.
And there is another method of incision, that you consider where the magnitude of the abscess and the collection of water appear, because sometimes it is more in the occiput, or in the front part, or on the right, or on the left; and therefore aim with the incision where the abscess and the collection of water appear, and do it as best you can.
And observe that you do not cut the artery and cause a flow of blood, and the patient dies from that blood flow with evacuation of humidity.
Chapter Second.  (or Chapter 2)
When someone experiences cataracts or rheums in the eyes or feet, and this is chronic, and the cure is not achieved by medicines, then the last resort is the incision of those two arteries.
And therefore, when you wish to incise them, have the patient raise his head, and then rub the area with a rough cloth so that the arteries appear, and then tighten the patient's neck with a cloth; and then observe where the vein pulsates; and the location of each, above the areas near the ears, is pressed, and thus they are hidden except in some men.
Then mark the artery with a probe, and then incise each one with a sharp lancet from each side, with an incision reaching to the bone, and this should be done along the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the lancet under the artery and sever it at the incisions above.
And let the length of the incision be about two finger-widths, because when a vein is incised, the blood exits with a pulsatile flow, which spurts out frequently.
But if the artery does not appear to the touch, then you must measure from the ear a space of three finger-widths, and then mark it with a probe, and make the incision to the bone.
And the amount of blood to be removed is about six ounces, and quickly remove more or less, and all this is according to the strength of the patient and the fullness of their artery.
And then examine the wound, and if any cause of infection remains on the bone, then incise it so that a hot abscess does not develop.
Then bind the wound tightly with feathers and linen bandages, and then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Of the extraction of arteries which are in the temples.
When someone suffers from chronic migraine or sharp headaches, due to acrid humors and a certain quality in the tissues of the temples, or a severe and similar old ailment, and it is treated with medicinal remedies and this does not help, then we should attempt in these illnesses the extraction of the arteries in the temples, or their cauterization as we have shown.
And the method of the operation and extraction of these is that the patient shaves the hairs which are on the temples; and attend to the artery appearing in the temple because it is shown to you by its pulse, and it is hidden except in few men by the intensity of cold.
And if it is hidden from you, then the neck of the patient should be constricted with some cloth; and then rub the area with a rough cloth or evaporate the area with warm water until the artery appears to you manifestly. And then you take the spatula of which this is the form: And then you scrape the skin with it easily until you reach the artery; and then pour oil on it and extract it to the superior parts until you extract it from the skin and uncover it from the tunics which are beneath it, from every part.
But if the artery is subtle, then wrap it with the end of the oncii (a surgical instrument), and then incise it in a portion according to the quantity by which its ends are elongated and contracted, and there will be no flow of blood, because if they are not incised, the blood will not rise at all.
And then evacuate from one to three ounces of blood.
And if the artery is large, then it is necessary that you tie it in two places with strong double silk thread or with cords at length, and do not allow putrefaction before the consolidation of the wound and the resulting flow of blood.
And then incise the superfluity which is between the two ligatures; and do this at that time or at another time.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery up to the bone with a knife-shaped cautery until its ends are incised, and it will be in place of that same work.
And it is a better procedure unless the patient has a fever or is of a heated complexion; for cautery is among those things that help drying humors, and therefore it is better in this instance.
And it is necessary after the extraction of the arteries that you fill the place with old linen, and that powders are decently placed upon it, and then it should be cured with drying medicines causing the flesh to grow, and with bandages until it is healed.
And if, while you are operating another time, there is a flow of blood from the artery, then you must take care to restrain that blood, either with a cautery, or by filling the place of the wound, and pressing with your hand until the bleeding is stopped. And if none of those things are at your disposal, then place your finger on the place until the blood is congealed, and bathe the place with very cold water until the sharpness subsides, and it is constricted as needed.
And what is lighter in the extraction of an artery is that you cauterize the vein with this cautery having two blades, after you have marked the two places with a probe, and place each, very hot, until they reach the bone and cut the vein in two places, so that what is between the extremities is elongated, because they are not completely consolidated with this cautery.
And this is the form of the cautery:
And let the sharpness of the two blades be equal. But however, if it has less sharpness than two knives, it is better, because if it is sharp like two knives close to each other, it will not cut the flesh with speed.
Therefore, since in those [cauteries] there is some thickness, the heat of the fire is held in them and the flesh is cut easily.
And this method is better in every respect and gentler.
And let the space which is between the two knives be the amount of the thickness of a finger.
Chapter four.
Concerning the treatment of sharp and acrid eye inflammations.
When there are acrid tears, and it is the inflammation of those veins which are on the outside of the head, and you do not find any cause for treatment in medicine, and sometimes the patient's face reddens and they feel a crawling sensation on their forehead as if it were ants crawling, and their eyes are strained, moist, so that the hairs around them are wet, and the eyelids are inflamed by the sharpness of the tears, the ancients used to employ this operation in the treatment of these things, and they called that treatment “spassa”.
And it is that you command the patient to shave the hairs which are on their forehead, and then incise three fissures in the forehead, equally spaced according to the length of the forehead. And if the length of the fissure reaches two fingers: one fissure should be equally distant from the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second according to the length of a little of the movement of the muscles which are in the lacrimal duct, and the third on the other side.
And be careful of the incision of the arteries which are on both sides, and keep your hand away from the continuation of both jawbones. And if the space of each incision is the amount of three adjusted fingers.
And if you have a piece of prepared sponge or very dry cloths with which to dry the blood.
Then insert a spatula with two sharp ends, of which this is the shape of its incision: which is from the part of the temples to the second incision. Excoriate with that [tool/spatula] the entire area between the two incisions with the [surgical] knife which is on the bone, and then do this from the middle incision to the second incision. And then remove that spatula and insert into the first incision again another instrument which is called a curette, sharp on one side and not sharp on the other.
And place the sharp edge of that [curette] against the flesh attached to the bone, and its blunt edge inside the bone, and push it in until it reaches the middle fissure, and sever with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes.
But if the incision is continued to that part of the bone, then do this in the middle fissure to the other fissure.
And after a sufficient quantity of blood has flowed, squeeze the places of the congealed blood clots.
And then place in each fissure a pledget of old linen, and place on them powders soaked in wine and oil and vinegar, and ensure that a hot abscess does not develop.
And on the third day, untie the bandage and administer a large infusion of warm or tepid water, and then treat it with basilicon ointment with rose oil and others until the wounds are healed.
Chapter 5.
Chapter 5: On the treatment of tears from cataracts and those descending into the eyes from the inner parts of the head.
When someone experiences sharp and persistent cataracts, and the patient's eyes appear small and sunken, and their vision is weakened, and the eyelids are ulcerated and shedding skin, and there is acute, throbbing pain in the forehead and consequent sneezing, then know from these symptoms that the materials and cataracts come from many veins originating in the inner parts.
And the best cure in these cases is this: order the patient to shave their forehead, and then incise a fissure in the middle of the forehead, a small one in width (and begin with the incision from the left temple to the right temple; and let the incision be between the bones; and lengthen your hand from the temples' lacerations which are bruised due to chewing), until the bone is uncovered.
Dry all the blood with a sponge or a dry cloth and then fill the space between the two lips of the fissure with old cotton or linen cloths. And then bind it above with powders soaked in wine and oil, so that a hot abscess does not develop.
And when you unbind it and see that the abscess has diminished, then it is necessary to rub the bone until flesh begins to grow there, and then treat it with a drying regimen that causes the flesh to grow, such as if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony and mix them, and from them an emplaster is made and is applied to these wounds to assist in the birth of the flesh.
Chapter 6.
On the treatment of that which falls into the ears.
Every cause that falls into the ears is one of four types, for it is either a mineral stone or something similar to a stone, such as iron and glass, or it is from a plant such as a pea or bean, or it is a flowing substance such as water and sour wine and things like them, or some animal.
And therefore, when a stone falls into the ear or another similar cause of those things that do not adhere to or grow in the ear, and you expose the ear to the sun, and if you see the stone, then instill into it some violet oil or sesame oil, and then assist in its extraction, and induce sneezing with a potion, and block the nostrils before the onset of sneezing. And then place some pieces of cloth or wool around the ear, and pull the ear upwards, for many times it is expelled by these means.
And if it is not expelled, then assist in its extraction with the instrument called a slender ear-picker, of which this is the form:
And if it is expelled with the ear-picker, it is good; and if not, then assist in its extraction with a thin probe of little thickness.
And therefore, if it is not expelled by this, then make a small copper tube and insert the end of the tube into the opening of the ear and seal around the tube with wax and softened oil so that nothing is open but the tube. And then extract it with your breath with strong suction, for many times this has been accomplished as we have recounted.
But if it is not expelled in this manner, then take a small amount of lit tinder or bird-lime, which is used to catch birds, and place it on the end of a thin rod, and then carefully wrap it around that properly positioned cotton. And then carefully insert that into the opening of the ear with ease, and then dry the ear of its moisture.
And so, if it is not expelled by all the means we have recounted, then you must consider cutting it out before it becomes a hot abscess or spasm.
And the manner of cutting it out is that you first bleed the patient from the cephalic vein and extract the blood according to the patient's strength, and then hold the ear between your hands and turn the ear upwards, and make a small incision at the root of the ear below its cartilage, in a well-prepared place. And let the incision be crescent-shaped until the stone is reached, and, when the stone is found, remove it if you can with appropriate instruments; and then close the incision immediately, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the cause lying in the ear is a growth of fat that is swollen, then administer its treatment and extraction; but if it does not yield to such treatment, then take fine spun thread according to this method: and use that to excise the growth that has fallen into the ear.
And do not do this when you think that it is already moistened by the vapor of the ear, and place it on cotton swabs, and then extract it with tweezers or a thin instrument, or with an incision as we have said, for its extraction is thereby eased.
But if water has entered the ear, the patient should use sneezing initially, and fill their ear with some old cotton, and lie on the ear into which the water has entered, and if it is expelled by this, it is good; and if not, then take several smooth pebbles the length of a finger, and warm them a little in the fire, and the patient inserts one of them into the opening of their ear and hops on one foot on that side and strikes with another stone on the stone which is inside the ear. Therefore, do not cease this with the stone until all the water is expelled.
Therefore, do not cease this with the stone until all the water is expelled. And if the water is not expelled in this way, take some paper or a reed, and place one end of it in the ear and hold the other end to a fire until the majority of it is burned.
And then place another reed there and do this as many times as necessary until all the water is expelled, and extract it with a cannula as was said above of the stone extractor.
But in the extraction of a creature entering that ear, if it is small, like a louse or similar, cure it as we have said in the division; but if it is of large size, which appears to the sense of sight, then administer its extraction with forceps and hooks, and the cause of this is easier than all other causes that befall the ear.
But in the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you cure it with what we have said in the division and in the treatise on distillations and your cure does not work, then you should expose the ear to the sun.
And then, if some cause of the worms appears, extract it with forceps or with fine hooks; but if no cause appears, then take a cannula narrow below and wide above, of which this is the form: And insert the narrow end into the ear according to the amount the patient can tolerate; and then suck with it with a strong suction, and do this many times, until all the worms are expelled.
But if it does not obey you in this way, then you should plug and close the opening of the cannula with wax as we have said; then administer distillations which the ancients have already proven in the cure of worms, and you will find this in the treatise on distillations.
And let your effusion, with which you pour the medicinal oils into the ear, be with this instrument, of which this is the form: And make it of silver or bronze, narrow below, in which there is a small hole, and flat above.
And let it be such that it pushes whatever is in the concavity of the cannula to the outside where it will be necessary. And if you wish, take a ray (possibly referring to a thin, possibly flexible instrument) and wrap its end decently with cotton, and then pour oil into the concavity of the ear, or juice of some herb, or whatever you want of these medicines into the cannula — and that is in the ear —, and then introduce the ray with cotton on top and press with your hand, with moderate compression, until the oil is expelled into the concavity of the ear and the patient feels the oil inside.
And what is poured into the ear should already be slightly warmed by fire. And be careful that the substance which you distill into the ear is not too cold, for ear medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
Concerning the treatment of ear obstruction.
Sometimes children are born from their mothers' wombs with an imperforate (unopened) ear, and this also happens in some adults, an obstruction in the ear canal due to a wound or an outgrowth of flesh; and this obstruction sometimes occurs in the entrance of the ear canal, which prevents the outflow of earwax, and sometimes it is in the outer parts.
And that which occurs at the entrance, preventing the outflow, is in most cases difficult to cure.
And in the case where the outflow is possible, you should place the patient's ear in the sun and observe it. And then, if you see an apparent obstruction, open it with a fine spatula, of which this is the form:
If its end is narrow, with one side sharp and the other not; and the spatula should be two fingers wide so that it does not injure the ear.
And then, if the obstruction is due to flesh grown in the ear or its canal, then remove that with fine hooks and incise it vigorously until all the flesh is removed.
And if the obstruction is in the outer part of the ear, then take a thin reed and heat it slightly with fire, and then insert it into the ear canal.
And then, if you have felt an obstruction with what the patient easily finds, due to difficulty in hearing, force the obstruction out gently; and be careful not to injure the nerves of the ear.
Then place in the ear a linen cloth, according to the size of the opening, which you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment; and do this for several days until you are sure of the healing of the wound; or take some wool, soak it in rose water, and pulverize it finely over it and use it; and if you see the ear is already abscessed, then you must remove the wool and replace it with other wool, which you will wrap in a cerate made with rose oil, until the abscess is completely healed; and then cure it until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs, then submerge a sponge or cloth in cold water and apply it to the ear, and use the other treatment which restricts the flow of blood, which is described in the treatise on distillation.
Chapter 8.
On the treatment of a stye which develops on the eyelids.
Styes which develop on the eyelids are sometimes moist, sometimes dry.
Therefore it is necessary that you hold them with forceps or with fingers, and incise them with a spatula at their roots, and then apply a dry poultice to the affected area.
But if pain with bleeding occurs, then cauterize them with a thin, lenticular cautery.
And the best cauterization is near the incisions, and often they return when some cause remains at their roots; and when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter 9.
Treatment of swelling that comes to the eyelids of the eyes.
Often there develops in the eyelids of the eyes some condition which resembles a swelling in its firmness and hardness, and because of this swelling it is called [a swelling/tumor].
And it is an accumulation of thick humidity in the eyelid below and above.
Therefore, the operation on them is that you consider then if the swelling proceeds from something that appears on the eyelid and is spread to every part; and its cause is light.
Then make an incision on it in width, and then peel it off completely easily until it comes out, and then suspend it with hooks and excise it.
And if the swelling is large, then you adjust it with sutures; and treat it until it is healed.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it because the ointment for it restores it and causes it to heal.
And if the swelling is leaning toward the inner parts of the eyelid beneath the inner surface, then evert the eyelid and suspend the swelling with hooks, unless you decide on incision, and press it on all sides, and if the eyelid is lengthened by incision, this does not harm in any way.
Then, after the incision, wash the eye with salt water; and treat the area with a healing agent until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the treatment of styes on the eyelid above the eye.
A stye is a swelling which is engendered in the membranes of the upper eyelid, and mostly in children, it irritates their eyes and causes them to have a watery discharge; and they cannot look at the brightness of the sun because tears flow from them.
And therefore you see them lying on their faces all the time, and sleeping on their faces; and their lower eyelids, below the eyebrows, are moist,  the area is swollen, and an impending stye is apparent in the eye; and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel the stye within.
And a certain manner of operation is this: the patient rests their head in your lap, and you place a cloth between you, and make a circle of the appropriate size to contain the stye from all sides, and place that upon it, and press with your fingers all around so that the stye is concentrated in the middle of the circle. Then, in the middle of that swelling, you make a small incision with a lancet, the cut being no larger than that made in bloodletting.
But in the beginning it is necessary that you incise only until you reach the stye, for in most cases the stye is discharged through the incision in the form of a white pus.
Then extract it with cloths which you have wrapped around your fingers at the edges and roll it between your right and left hand until it is gone.
And be careful not to press too hard on the incision so that with the lancet you do not reach the eye and injure it.
But if the stye does not appear at the beginning of the incision, press gently on the incision until the stye is discharged; and then extract it as we have said; and then soak cloths in wine and vinegar and place them on the area and compress them with powders.
And some crush salt and place it in the cavity of the incision so that it dries up the remaining moisture.
And then care for it until it is healed.
But if a hot abscess occurs at the site, then treat it with soothing plasters until it is healed.
Chapter 11.
Concerning the methods of touching the eye.
When in the eyelids of the eyes there are growths beneath the natural hairs and they are inflamed, then these harm the eye and cause various illnesses, such as constant tears and softening of the eyelids and membrane, until this causes destruction of the eye, which is called tactus (a touching or affliction) of the eye, and it is treated in four ways: either by cautery with fire; or with a sharp medicine as before stated in the chapter on cautery; or the tactus is done by incision and suture; or by cannulas as I shall explain.
And therefore it is necessary that you place the patient's head in your lap; then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand, and if it is turned, it is good; and if not, insert a needle into that in which there is a pimple [or abscess] from the lower part of the eyelid, and make the needle penetrate with the pimple upwards, and this should be — near the edge of the eyelid where the hairs grow — near the hairs, and pull the pimple to the upper parts with the eyelid, and turn it with a ray [of light, perhaps implying a tool or method to properly expose the growth].
And then make an incision in the inner part of the eyelid below the inflamed hairs with a small, sharp instrument from the larger lacrimal gland to the smaller lacrimal gland; and then extract the pimple and place beneath the eyelid small pieces of cotton or cloth, and then sew up the eyelid with thread, and it should be like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, the shape should be according to the amount of swelling of the eyelid, for this varies in people, for there are some who require that a large amount be incised from the eyelid according to the amount by which the eyelid is softened.
And then make [an incision] with the small instrument along the two lines which you have sewn, and begin from the larger lacrimal gland to the smaller lacrimal gland. And if your incision is near the natural hairs,  like the thickness of a king's [hair/beard — implying something thick and noticeable].
And then place the lancet in one of the two corners of the wound, and then scrape it all, and then join the two lips of the wound with glue or fine wool thread, and wipe away the blood, and apply what superfluously remains of the threads to the two eyebrows with some adhesive substance, if you wish to do so, and if not, do not worry.
And then leave the suture of the threads for three or four days, and then care for it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without sutures, and treat it with something that dries and constricts, because the eyelid is raised by the sealing of the wound and its aggregation; but suture is better.  And therefore the ancients advised this.
However, let there be diligence on the part of the surgeon, and it is a good operation, and there should be no fear.
And there is another method of treatment, and it is that you mark on the eyelid a figure like a myrtle leaf, and then raise the eyelid with three separate and gathered lancets according to this form:
But if it is not possible to hold the lancets, and this is not suitable, then take a needle on which there is thread and place that in the middle of the figure, and insert the other thread near the larger lacrimal duct, and the second thread near the smaller lacrimal duct, and adjust the threads between your fingers with equality, and then with them, lifting your hand with equal elevation, raise the entire area that has been marked as we have recounted.
And then join the two lips of the wound with a suture, and treat it until it is healed.
But if an abscess occurs near your incision or fissure, treat that abscess with a plaster and similar things, and with ointments.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelid that its hairs are inverted; then it is necessary that you use on it what we have said of incision; but suture in each case is more skillful.
To cauterize with canes is according to this method: and it is that you turn back the eyelid and incise the growth that is within, as I have recounted; and then skillfully make two small canes or splinters, whose length should be according to the length of the eyelid and whose width should be less than the width of the speculum. And you have already incised at the ends of each part, where you have the threads.
Then carefully add each [cane] with subtlety over what protrudes from the eyelid of the eye, and bind the canes to each part with a strong ligature, and leave it for some days, for the growth because of the binding will die and dry up and will rot until it falls off by itself.
But if it is delayed, then break it with forceps, and then care for the wound until it is healed.
Then, once it is solidified, the eyelid is raised and the hairs do not prick.
And this is the form of the two canes which you will make according to this form: and have the quantity of length and width neither more nor less.
Chapter 12.
Chapter 12. Of the elevation of prickly hairs in the eye with a needle when the hair is hard.
When there are unnatural, hard, rough, and prickly hairs in the eyes, then the operation is that you take a fine needle, and place on it a fine silk or linen thread, and then you adjust its ends very subtly, so that neither one gets in the way. And let the length of the threads be about the length of a palm.
And then you prepare another short, fine thread underneath, and tie its ends if you wish.
And then have the patient place their head in your lap, and be near the sun so that your work is clearly seen, for it is a subtle and delicate operation.
And then insert the needle into the root of the offending hair, carefully guiding it, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly until you pull it out above the natural hair.
And then pull the hair out and place it in a little container; and let the hairs be one, two, or three, and no more.
And then pull your hand with that until the hair is removed, along with the little container, from the eyelid, with the natural hair.
But if the little container is pulled and the hair is not removed, you will pull the little container to the lower parts with the thread which you have composed with that, until the little container is removed from the wound.
And observe that with the vision of the eye, for then you will remove the little container and the thread, and clean each (wound) and close the eye, and leave it closed for two or three days, until the hair adheres to its place and good flesh grows over it.
And if the hair is shorter, then you attach a long hair from the natural hairs, and it is elevated with that, or you leave it until it is lengthened to a certain extent, and then you elevate it.
Chapter 13.
Of the treatment of a stye which appears on the upper eyelid.
When a stye appears in the eye, and it is a contraction of the eyelid when it is contracted upwards, or to the right or left side, due to poor consolidation of a wound or incision or cautery, and it is called harelip, and this stye is either natural or accidental, and it is caused by the consolidation of a wound or incision or cautery, or by something similar poorly treated.
The method of operation is that you should incise that consolidation and separate its lips, and place between them a small piece of linen cloth, and bind them until they are healed.
And it is not advisable to treat it with remedies that dry or constrict; for if you do this, the stye will return worse than it was before.
Use on it remedies that soften, such as fenugreek, and bathe it with the water in which mallow and linseed are boiled, or with diachylon ointment, which should be liquefied with one of the oils, and dip the cloth in it, and treat it with that.
And the key to your treatment is that you ensure, by all means, that it does not consolidate in a different form than it was initially.
And this operation, which we have described, is only a rectification of the stye so that it does not return to its previous form.
Chapter 14.
Concerning the treatment of a stye which is formed on the lower eyelid.
This stye, which forms on the lower eyelid—which is truly called a stye—is of one kind natural, and another accidental.
But an accidental stye is caused by a wound, or by a cut, or by cautery, or by other similar things.
And the method of operating on it is that you take a needle with double thread, and insert that into the flesh, passing it from the left lachrymal duct to the right lachrymal duct, until the thread reaches both ends of the flesh.
Then stretch the flesh upwards with the needle and stitch it with the needle, and incise it with a broad spatula. And then if the shape of the eyelid returns to what it should be, it is good; but if not, take a ray [likely referring to a thin splint or similar instrument] and place it in the divided area, and with it reshape the eyelid, and sew the two fissures on the side of the lower eyelid.
And let the ends of the two fissures of the two angles of the incision, which you have made so that they meet, form an angle so that when they come together, their figure is formed.
And this figure is the Greek letter: lambda (Λ).
Then remove the flesh on that side by the amount that the sharp lower edge is, that which should be the eyelid.
Then join the separated parts and sew them with a wool thread; and then treat them with what we have said of emollient medicines and poultices until it is healed.
And if it is excised by fissure, suture, or cautery, then you must make a flat incision on the skin of the eyelid as previously described. Then separate [the wound] with a poultice between two lips as we have said.
In summary of the discourse on the treatment of an abscess, whether above or below, let the operation proceed as prepared in the manner of an abscess, and this is in the form of various types.
And the skilled artisan always prepares a device in every way possible so that he may restore the shape to its natural form, or near to it, and the patient according to what suits him in terms of the operation and instruments in the best conditions.
Chapter 15.
Of the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or to the cornea.
When this consolidation occurs in many men such that the upper eyelid is solidified to the white of the eye, and the ease of movement and its natural functions are prevented, then it is necessary that you insert the end of a probe under the eyelid and lift it, or stretch it with an unguent.
And then you incise the adhesion with a fine spatula, which should not be sharply pointed, but rather slightly blunt, so that the patient is not disturbed during the operation, and keep the spatula from injuring the eye with its sharpness.
And let your incision gently scrape away that adhesion until the eyelid is returned to its natural form and the entire adhesion is healed.
And then you put salt water in the eye with which you wash it; or distill into it some honey dissolved in water.
And then separate the eyelid from the eye with a small piece of linen, and place wool infused in egg white on the eye, and then on the third day use consolidative remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Concerning the incision of the nail and the fleshy eminence of the tear ducts.
Know that a nail is of two types, for it is either nervous, and resembles a hard, fine chip; or it is non-nervous, resembling a congealed white humidity, which when iron touches it, or it is forced to be removed with an instrument, is cut away and the instrument is not inserted into it.
And both types begin only from the main tear duct until it gradually covers the sight and prevents light and eye movement.
But the manner of operating in its incision is that the patient places his head in your lap, and then open his eyes, and raise the eyelid with your hand, and then grasp the nail with a slightly curved instrument and extend it towards the upper parts. And then take a needle and insert into it a horsehair, or ox hair, or cow hair, or something similar, or strong thread, or double the end of the needle little by little and insert it into the middle of the nail, and penetrate it with the needle, and tie the nail with thread; and extend it to the upper parts, and scrape with the hair the side of the nail which covers the pupil. And thus you follow it with the hair into its back part.
And then you incise the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a fine spatula or with small scissors, and leave the natural flesh of the tear duct so that through the incision of it, a continuous flow of tears does not occur.
And sometimes the nail, with the instrument alone extended, or with thread, as we have said, and then it should be scraped with a lion spatula, of which this is the form:
And be careful that with the spatula you do not touch the cornea and that a rupture occurs in it, and that the pupil is perforated.
Then, into the incision, put a little crushed salt or a powder made of  [likely a plant, needs more context for precise translation - perhaps "cinquefoil" or similar] and leave it there until the next day; and then treat it with what is necessary until it is healed.
And if the nail is not sensitive and you cannot insert the needle, nor is it fixed with the instrument, then many times this type of nail is cured with medicines, and is healed as I have recounted in the section/chapter.
And if you wish to incise it, then you open the wound of the patient and take a thin lion spatula of cold iron sharpened according to this form: with subtlety/precision. And if you see that it is rarefied and is hidden and rooted in that place, then distill from your alembic onto the wound a powder made of [likely a plant, needs more context for precise translation - perhaps "cinquefoil" or similar] or with rose powder or with crushed salt, and cover the wound until the next day.
Then repeat the operation on it until all that is there is removed, unless conversely a hot abscess forms in the wound, for then you leave it and treat the abscess first until it is healed.
And then repeat your operation on it reasonably until it is healed.
The eminence of the lachrymal flesh, if it is noticeable in the wound as a great harm, should be suspended with hooks; and incise it in one part, and do not extend the incision so that you do not cut the flow of tears.
And then distill rose and [likely a plant, needs more context for precise translation - perhaps "cinquefoil" or similar] powder into the lachrymal [gland/duct - needs more context] until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Concerning the incision of a carbuncle and that which is born of flesh joined in a wound.
Sometimes in the eyes of some, red flesh is grown, and it is overgrown until it covers the pupil, or approaches or presses upon the eyelids, and quickly compresses the eyelids to the outer parts and is similar to a balustrade rose [likely referring to a type of fleshy growth resembling a rose in appearance].
But the operation on him is that the patient places his head in your lap; and then open the eyes of that one and gather all that flesh with scissors convenient for this, and hold that with forceps or with tweezers; and then incise the flesh at the beginning until all is finished with the lancet, and be careful of the eye that you do not injure it during your operation.
And let your incision with which you incise those be with a spatula, which we have recommended in the incision of the nail, or with small scissors with which hair is collected, according to how the form of it comes to this chapter.
Then when your incision is completed and all that flesh is removed, then fill the eye with crushed salt, or instill into it red powder similar to that of corrosive medicines, and place over the outside of the eye a cotton compress with egg white until it is assured of a hot abscess [or boil].
And if some cause of granulation remains and hot blood is seen, and you fear a hot abscess, then leave the eye and cure that abscess with what diminishes that abscess, and then iterate on that with the operation until it is healed, and similarly do with the fleshy excrescence which happens in the eye of this kind.
But however, you must avoid all treatment of the weak eye by natural or accidental means, working with iron, and do not put your hand to any of these operations until you see that that member is tolerating that operation.
Chapter XVIII.
Concerning the collection of pus in the eye.
Pus is a viscous blood which settles on the eye and prevents its proper functioning, and weakens the eye over a period of days.
Therefore, it is necessary at the beginning that you consider if the eye in which there is pus is strong and has no other illness than pus; then you may collect that pus.
And it is that you command the patient to place their head in your lap, and then suspend those veins with an oncia or two, according to your judgment; and let that oncia be of subtle doubling according to this form:
Or two oncias gradually in one piece according to this form:
And then those veins should be collected with fine forceps, with subtlety, and wipe away the blood little by little, until you see those veins being removed from the eye and are retracted.
And beware that you do not injure the eye with the ends of the spatula; and if your operation is in the midday before the sun, and you are very firm in your operation, that you incise only those veins.
And when you have completed [the procedure], then you distil into the eye verdigris or vitriol so that with its acidity it corrodes what remains of the splinter.
But if this is not possible for you, collect everything at that time, and then you plaster everything with something that soothes his pain, and leave it for a few days until his pain is subsided and a hot abscess is avoided.
And then repeat on him according to the same manner until he is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps:
Chapter 19.
Concerning the reduction of nasal dislocations.
When you treat a dislocation with cautery or with a sharp, burning medicine, as narrated previously, and it is not healed, then do not  make incisions into it, but incise the abscess above its maturation, and extract all the moisture which is within it and the pus, until the bone is exposed.
And then, when the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, then you scrape it with an instrument of which this is the form:
And it is named a rasping head, and it should be made of Indian iron, and its head should be round like a chisel, which should be pointed by filing or sharpened by subtle filing (as it is in the following form).
And place that [instrument] upon the corrupted part of the bone, and then rotate it between your fingers, and press your hand little by little until you know that the corruption is scraped away.
And this should be done several times, and then restore the area with drying and styptic substances.
Then, if the area is consolidated and good flesh has grown there and the body of the pus is excised and remains for forty days, and is not renewed and is not festering and no accident occurs there, then you know that it is healed.
And if not, then there is nothing else to do but to reduce the fissure to the shape of the nose, and according to this method: that is, you uncover the underlying bone with an iron or with a sharp medicine, and when the bone is uncovered, then you take a trephine according to this form:
Let the end of the iron [trephine] be triangular, and its shaft be thin and tapered to the end, like this.
And then place that [trephine] on the bone, and let it be near the lacrimal bone; and extend your hand a little from the eye, and then rotate your hand with the trephine until it penetrates the whole bone, and the patient will feel this because they will feel wind escaping when you have their hand.
Then restore the area with styptic, drying medicines as you have been taught.
Then, when the flesh has grown and the area has hardened, the matter which was flowing to the outer parts returns to the nose, and this is easier for the patient.
Chapter 20.
Concerning the reduction of the protrusion of the eye.
When the eye protrudes completely and there is no accompanying injury, impairment, or diminution, then you should leave the patient alone and observe him, and apply cupping glasses to the neck without scarification and suction for good suction; and apply to the eye an ointment made of  catarrh medicine (or possibly a specific herb), aloe, enses (likely an herb or resin), and sarcocolla, and firmly attach the plaster to the eye with much powder.
And the manner of the powders is that you take many linen cloths, and each cloth should be folded into four layers; and make [a poultice] from these.
And then place those [cloths] one upon another according to the amount the eye requires. And then bind them tightly with a strong cloth with firm constriction; and repeat the cupping with suction without scarification; and then remove the cupping glasses and leave the eye bound day and night.
But if in the meantime the ligament is softened, then it should be bound very tightly.
And then remove the bandage at the end of the day and night, as we have said.
And if you see the eye has already been reduced [in swelling], it is good, and if not, then repeat the plaster with the powders and the binding and the cupping until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Concerning the incision of the eminence of the uvea tunic.  (Note: "uvea tunic" refers to the uvea, the vascular layer of the eye.)
When there is a rupture in the uvea tunic, and it is prominent and protrudes beyond the eyelids like a grape, and the person's appearance is marred by it, and you wish to incise it, then the operation is as we have recounted: that you introduce the needle into the root of the uvea from the lower part to the upper part, and then place another needle with double thread from the lacrimal side, and make that penetrate and leave the first needle according to its position; and then incise the area of the double thread and lift with it the part of the uvea in the circle of the needle, and tightly bind that.
And then remove the needle and place on the eye wool infused in egg white and allow the thread with the uvea to remain.
And then treat the eye with that which comforts it until the wound is healed.
And sometimes gather from all sides with a circle of cloth the large, protruding eminence, and then pierce it with a thin spatula that reaches the pupil of the eye, and drain the albuginea (whitish) humor, and the eye will descend and enter immediately.
And then bind it tightly so that it may heal.
Chapter 22.
Concerning the treatment of alconati (likely referring to a specific ailment or injury;  further context would help refine the translation.  It might refer to a type of ulcer, eye condition, or something else entirely).
This illness that is called alconati is nothing but a swelling that is located in the eye, similar to descending water, but it is not that.
But the manner of working on it is that you make the patient sit on a prepared and straight seat, and then take his head in your hands on both sides and gently manipulate it until you see the swelling descend to the lower parts, and then it should be dried so that it does not descend, and the light should be obstructed, and the patient should see things as they are seen.
And if it does not descend downwards, know that it is water (referring to the fluid associated with the swelling).
But if the descent of the swelling is not prepared with what we have said, then make the patient sit between your hands and then take a fine spatula and use it on the pannicle, gently scraping under the juncture of the conjunctiva with the cornea in the crown (in the hollow space), until the swelling is expelled.
Then, when it is expelled, you instill warm water into the eye in which you have mixed honey, or water in which you have previously boiled fenugreek and honey.
And then it should be treated with other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
On the treatment of the descending water from the eye (or,  On the treatment of a watery eye).
We have already discussed the types of eye discharge in the division and which should be pierced with a surgical instrument, then that according to the health. (A more idiomatic translation might be: We have already described the types of eye discharge and which ones require treatment by perforation; the approach depends on the patient's overall health.)
Then you should make the patient sit between your hands on a chair and tie his/her healthy eye, holding it firmly.
Then place your left hand near the eyelid of his/her eye if the eye with the discharge is the left eye; or with your right hand if the eye is the right one.
And then with your right hand take an almond, if the eye is the left one; or with your left hand if the eye is the right one.
And then place the tip of the almond near the cornea, at the edge of the white of the eye, on the side of the tear duct, and then gently press the almond and roll your hand with it.
And then penetrate into the white of the eye and feel that the almond has reached the empty space/cavity.
And it should be that the distance of the almond to the preon (likely referring to a specific anatomical point, possibly the pupil or a point near the pupil) is the measure of the space which is from the pupil to the end of the blackness (the iris), and that is the cornea of the eye; for you will see with that pupil through the clarity of the corneal tunic.
And then place the almond into the place where the water (likely referring to aqueous humor) is, and then you draw it down one time and then another, and then if the water descends immediately, then show the patient that on which he will see immediately, and the almond in his eye.
And then rest a little; then if the water has descended, then you remove it another time before the almond is ejected/removed.
And then when it is dried and does not descend, then you extract the almond easily, and gently rotate it with your hand, and then dissolve a little clear rock salt in the water and with that wash the eye inside; and then place outside the eye cotton or tow or wool infused in rose oil and egg white and bind the eye with this.
But we and those of our time place on that broken surface egg white.
And if the almond does not obey you at the entry of the eye because of its hardness — for there are some men whose eye is very hard — then it is necessary that you take the extract which is called *alberid* (a likely corrupted or dialectal word, possibly referring to a specific plant or substance), of which this is the form:
Then perforate that joint with it, and do not thrust your hand into the hole, but only make a subtle passage for the almond.
And then introduce the almond as we have said.
And when your work is complete, prepare for the patient a darkened, quiet resting place where he may sleep on his back, and prohibit all movement.
And position him so that his body is aligned, and he does not turn his head to the right or left at all.
And let the bandage remain in place according to its disposition until the third day.
And then, unbind it in that dark room and expose the wound briefly, show him some things, and then re-apply the bandage by the seventh day.
And it is not fitting that you do this at the hour of the cures or immediately after the infusion. Having said this, you must avoid this, because the water might easily escape due to great and vehement movement/agitation.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you must unbind the wound before the seventh day, and treat it with something that will reduce the abscess.
Then you clean his wound and place a poultice on his face through which his vision will be guided,  keeping him in the dark room for some days.
And then let him be removed from that dark room gradually and  converse about the causes of his illness.
And know that in a cataract, the pupil is not exempt from vision sometimes, and then you should be bold about the operation.
And it has come to us from some of those who are in Artach (a place name), and some say that they make fire in Ayrac (another place name), a perforated pot, with which water is drawn.
However, I have not seen anyone in our land who has perfected this, nor have I read this in any of the books of the ancients; and it is possible that this is new.
And these are the forms of the kinds of poultices, so that he who is ignorant of them may know them:
And let them be made of bronze or iron properly, and let their extremities be with this triangular subtlety, the sharp extremity thus, as that is the form.
Chapter 24.
Concerning the treatment of rotten flesh in the nose.
When various fleshy growths are produced in the nose, some of which resemble a very small scorpion, or a polyp fish; and this illness is called polyp because it resembles that represented fish. And from it is cancerous flesh, scabrous, of dark color; and from it is that which is lion flesh, not of dark color.
Therefore, in the case of these fleshy growths that are not cancerous or fraudulent, you should make the patient sit between your hands and place them in the sun, and open their nostrils, and prick those growths with your fingernail. Then extract that to the outside, and then incise what you have grasped of that with a thin, sharp spatula from one side until you know that all the flesh has been removed; if any part remains from which excision is possible, then scrape that with one of the subtle instruments easily until nothing remains. But if it bleeds profusely or forms a hot, malignant abscess, then you apply what is necessary; or if it is one of the malignant abscesses, then you incise and cauterize it until the bleeding is stopped and all the cavities are removed.
Then let there be poured into the nose near the incision, strong wine and water or wine. But if the nose is open and a moisture proceeds from it into the palate, then know that it is healed.
But if the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then you know that inside there is rotten flesh in the excess pus above the bones of the nostrils to which excision has not reached.
Therefore, it is necessary that you introduce a linen thread into it, to which some thickness is added, and tie knots in the thread, and place between each knot the quantity of a finger or less, and guide the patient and introduce one end of the thread into the nostrils with a rod or with another instrument which is possible for you.
And after this is done, let it draw its scent until it reaches the canals of the nostrils, and it is expelled through their throat, for many times children in schools do something similar to this action, and it is an easy thing for anyone who wants to do it.
And then place the two ends of the thread, one end protruding from the nose and the other remaining in the nose, and then administer sawdust of flesh with the knots that are on the thread, and do this until you know that the flesh is now with the knots of the thread.
And then extract the thread, and place near the nose, near the extraction of the blood, a poultice, and do this for three days or more until the ointment corrodes all that is rotten of the flesh.
And finally, at the end of the treatment, place a lead cannula in the nose for some days until it is healed.
But if you need a more drying cure, use that one.
And this is the form of the instrument, a head-purger, with which oils and medicines are distilled into the nostrils:
And make this of silver or brass, which should resemble a small open lamp, like a small bowl, and the spout similarly.
And if you wish, make a closed cannula like a tube, and the cannula of the head-purging instrument should be flat and open;  it should have a handle at the back, with which you hold it when you heat the oil or water, or whatever you want of the juices and sucticable substances.
Chapter 25.
Of the boils in the extremities of the nose.
Many times a boil is formed at the extremities of the nose, and it is magnified and increased in a few days to the point that it is very difficult to treat.
And for this it is necessary that you incise it at the beginning of its appearance, and eradicate all of it. And then place on the area either a cautery or a burning medicine which will stay in place.
But if its incision is delayed until it is magnified, then consider if it is hardened, hard, dark colored, and insensitive; then do not touch it with iron, for it is a cancerous abscess; for many times he who incises this abscess suffers great harm from it.
And if the abscess is of a reddish hue and is not dark colored, and if incision is possible in all of it, then evacuate the patient and incise it without fear, and treat the area with what dries and constricts until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
Chapter 26. Of the suturing of the nose, lips, and ear when their continuity is broken by a wound or similar injury.
Know that when a solution of continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, you should perform this operation cautiously, only on certain individuals.
Therefore, when such a thing happens to someone, consider if it is a fresh wound with its own blood; then you should approximate the two lips of the wound with sutures, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the lips of the solution of continuity are already separated, and each is healed, then you must scrape away some of the apparent skin until blood appears. Then approximate the two lips with sutures and tighten them; on these, sprinkle dragon's blood and crushed egg shells, and place over the powder a piece of diachylon plaster, or some other consolidating plaster, and leave it bound for two or three days.  Then unbind it, change the plaster, and leave it until the stitches are naturally shed.  Finally, treat with ointment until healed.
But the manner of suturing is that you should close the solution of continuity either with needles, as we have described in the suturing of the abdomen, or close the solution of continuity with thread, as has been shown to you.
Chapter 27.
Of the extraction of those things which enter between the two lips in the inner parts.
Many times there appear in men, in the inner parts of their lips, certain hard abscesses resembling goose grease, and some of these are smaller and some larger.
Therefore it is necessary that you should evert the lips and separate each of those hardnesses on all sides, and afterwards fill the place of excision with crushed sage until the bleeding is stopped, and afterwards let it be washed with sharp vinegar and the place be treated with something styptic until it is healed.
Chapter 28.
Of the incision of the flesh grown in the gums.
Often a growth appears on the gum, a fleshy excrescence which the ancients called an albulus; then it is necessary that you suspend it with a hook or hold it with forceps and incise it to its root, and allow the serum or blood to flow.
And afterwards place crushed sage or other styptic or drying powders on the area.
And if that flesh returns, then incise it a second time, and cauterize it, for after cauterization it will not return.
Chapter 29.
Concerning the treatment of teeth with iron.
When on the surface of the teeth, both the inner and outer parts, and between the gums, rough, decayed, and sometimes blackened crusts are attached, and they become citrine or green, so that corruption spreads to the gum, and the teeth become decayed.
And therefore it is necessary that you make the patient sit between your hands and rest his head on your lap, and scrape the teeth on which those crusts and things resembling sand appear, until nothing of them remains, and this removes the blackness, the greenness, and the others, until they are cleaned.
Therefore, if this which is in the beginning of the treatment is removed, it is good; and if not, then you repeat the treatment on those teeth on the following day, and the second, and the third, until it reaches the desired end.
And know that the teeth require many instruments of diverse forms and shapes, according to what is prepared for your operation. And because the instrument with which the tooth is scraped from the inner part is different from the instrument with which it is scraped between the teeth, according to other forms, and these forms of instruments should be prepared by you, which you will see immediately:
Chapter 30.
On the extraction of teeth.
That you cure the tooth of its pain with all skill and delay its extraction, for there is no cause that supplants its sight when it is extracted, for it is a sublime substance, insomuch that its extraction is excused.
But if the patient is forced to have that tooth extracted, you must be firmly certain which tooth is diseased, for many times the pain deceives the patient and he thinks it is in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one and then the pain does not cease until the diseased and aching tooth is extracted, for we have already seen this through the operation of barbers.
Therefore, when you are certain which tooth is diseased, and you know it well, then you should scarify around the tooth with a spatula in which there is some virtue, until it is loosened from the gum on all sides, and then remove it with your fingers, or with fine forceps, beginning little by little until it is completely removed.
And then apply strong, large forceps for good traction or strong support, and place the patient's head between your knees so that you hold him well and he does not move, and then pull the tooth out straight in such a way that you do not break it, nor cause the patient greater pain.
And then, if it is not dislodged, take one of those instruments and place it under the tooth on all sides with ease, and strengthen its removal in the way that was done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is broken or decayed, then you should fill that hole with cloth and tighten it with the end of a thin rod so that it is not crushed at the time of your tightening on it with forceps.
And you should open carefully with scarification around all parts of the gum and be very careful not to break it and leave a part of it behind, and to return to the patient because the injury would be greater than the initial pain.
And beware that you do not do what some ignorant barbers do in their haste and boldness in their extraction, without administering what we have recommended; for many times they cause great illnesses in men which are more difficult to cure, and the roots of their teeth remain, all or some of them, or they extract them with some bone from the jaw, as we have witnessed many times.
And afterwards, after its extraction, the mouth should be washed with warm wine or sour wine with salt.
And if bleeding ensues from this, as often happens, then crush some quantity of sage and sprinkle it on the wound, and if the sage is not effective, cauterize it.
The shape of the forceps and fine tongs with which the tooth is grasped at the beginning should be of long extremity, short and thick handle, so that they do not bend when you work with them.
And let them be, as I say, of thick handle so that when you squeeze with them they are not bent; [let them have] short extremities.
And let them be made of Indian iron, carefully made with straight extremities, and at the extremities of these let there be teeth, of which the ones interlock with the others and tighten by strong constriction; and sometimes the extremities of these have the shape of the beak of a bird called a *ciconia* (that is, a stork), and are of strong constriction.
Chapter 31.
Of the eradication of teeth and the extraction of broken jaw bones.
When you must eradicate a tooth which is broken and some part remains, then you should place a soft poultice on the area for a day or two until the area is softened; and then introduce pincers or forceps, the extremities of which resemble the beak of a bird called a *ciconia* or stork, the shape of forceps:
The ends of them should be made inside like a file or rasp.
And if it does not yield to you easily with these forceps or pincers, then you should dig around the root and remove all the flesh and introduce the instrument which is called an *atali*, that is, a small lever, of which the form is this:
And let it have a short, somewhat thick end, and let it not be bent so that it is not broken.
Therefore, if by this the root is removed, it is good; and if not, help yourself with these other instruments, of which these are the forms: the form of the first instrument is of a triangular end in which there is some thickness:
The form of the second instrument is that it should be triangular and of a more subtle end.
And you are already aided with this instrument having two prongs, of which this is the form approximately as follows: and with other instruments which precede in tooth scraping; for it is easily done with this instrument which is similar to a large hook, and this is its form:
of a triangular, curved or short end, which should have some thickness, and not be bent so that it is not broken.
And know also that the instruments for loose teeth are similarly made to the other instruments.
But the wise and subtle artisan in the art, finds for himself according to what the operation and the diverse diseases signify to him; for there are diseases to which the ancients did not give or devise a proper instrument, because of the forms of diverse species.
But if the bone of the jaw or of the other bones of the mouth is broken or fractured, then it should be treated in its place with what is convenient to it from among the instruments and forceps which we have mentioned in the extraction of tooth roots, and help yourself with a splint, of which this is the form:
And let there be some gradual thickening in it, and let it be constricted or held with the mouth so that it does not escape or flee until the bone is entirely set; and then restore the area with medicines suitable for this.
But if there is a small rottenness in the bone, then scrape away that rottenness and its blackness, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 32.
On the treatment of teeth that protrude above the others.
When teeth protrude, not following their natural course, their shape is flawed, and this is especially true for women, and especially for venerable old women; therefore you must consider: if a tooth is next to another tooth, and neither closure nor alignment is possible, then extract it; but if it is not attached to another tooth, then you should incise it with an instrument of which this is the form; and it is similar to a boar's tusk, and should be made of Indian iron with a very sharp point.
And let your incision, with which you incise it, be done in several days because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that no other teeth are damaged.
And if the protruding tooth is amenable to filing, then file it with an Indian iron file of which this is the form:
And let the whole thing be of Indian iron, and very sharp; and let it have very fine points, like a file with which needles are made, with which the tooth should be filed little by little over many days easily, so that no other tooth is damaged and broken. Then smooth it at the end, and scrape it with some rasps.
And if it is a tooth of which a part is broken and injures the tongue, then it is necessary that you file it until the roughness of that fracture is removed and smoothed, and is filled so that the tongue is not injured nor the speech impaired.
Chapter 33.
Concerning the restoration of damaged teeth with gold or silver wire.
When the front teeth suffer concussion and loosening from a blow or fall, and the patient cannot bite on what he eats, and you have treated this with styptic medicines, and your treatment has not been effective, then so that they do not fall out, devise that they are strengthened with gold wire and not silver, because silver turns green and quickly powders after a few days, but gold is never changed.
And let the wire be of such fineness and thickness according to the amount that can fit between the teeth; and the manner of tying is that you take the wire and pass the doubled wire between two healthy teeth, and then let it grow with two twists of the wire between the damaged ones, or if one or many, until the wire reaches the healthy tooth on the other side. And then repeat the weaving to the point where you began, and tighten with your hand gently and skillfully so that it is not damaged.
And let your tightening, with which you bind the wire around the roots of the teeth, be such that it does not loosen.
And then cut off the superfluous ends of the wire with forceps or scissors, and smooth those ends in each place with a file, and bend them and hide them between the healthy tooth and the damaged tooth, so that they do not injure the tongue. And then leave it thus tightly bound.
But if it is loosened or broken, tighten it or them with another wire in the same manner at all times.
And this is the form of the teeth and the figure of the straightening of two healthy teeth and two damaged teeth, thus showing these bound together:
And when it is done that one or two teeth return to their proper places, and are grown as we have recounted, and that they are damaged.
And this is only done by skillful and wise artisans.
And sometimes some material from cow bones is used, and it is shaped like a tooth, and is placed in the place where the tooth is missing, and is fastened as we have said, and remains and serves for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Of the incision of the ligament which is in the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament, which is under the tongue, is sometimes natural, with which one is born, or accidental and occurs from a wound which has already healed.
And the operation is that you open the patient's mouth, their head being in your lap; and raise their tongue, and then incise that nervous ligament according to its width until the tongue is freed from its constraint.
But if in that place there is any hardness or knot, and this is due to the consolidation of a wound, then you apply an ointment and incise it along its width until you have incised the ligament and the knot is dissolved.
And be careful that no fissure is made in the bottom of the flesh and that you do not incise too deeply and cause a flow of blood.
And then wash the site of the incision with wine and water, or with cold water; and then place under the tongue a linen cloth; that is to say, a piece of cloth which the patient should keep under their tongue every night so that it does not consolidate again.
But if bleeding occurs, place crushed vitriol on the area; but if the bleeding persists, then cauterize the area with a lancet-shaped cautery which is suitable for this. And then treat it with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
On the extraction of a ranula from under the tongue.
Sometimes an abscess resembling a small tumor occurs under the tongue, preventing the tongue from its natural function; and it is quickly enlarged until it fills the mouth.
And the operation is that you open the patient's mouth, sitting facing the sun, and examine the abscess.
