And if you see it is dark or black in color, hard, and the patient feels no pain in it, then do not touch it, for it is cancer.
And if it is inclined to whiteness, in which there is moisture, then you place the hook there, and incise it with a fine spatula, and incise it on all sides; and if blood flows during your operation, then you place crushed vitriol on it until the bleeding is stopped.
Then return to your work until you have completely removed it, until it is washed with sharp wine and salt; and treat it with the other appropriate cure until it is healed.
Chapter 36.
Chapter 36.  On the extraction of an abscess from both ears and from that which is located in the throat, and of other abscesses.
When a swelling occurs in the throat, these are glands that are gathered together like cotton, which protrude outwards and are called tonsils; when you treat them with what we have said in the previous section and it is not healed, then consider whether the abscess is hard, dark in color, and insensitive; if so, do not touch it with an instrument.
And if it is red and the root is thick, then do not touch it with an instrument because of the flow of blood; rather, let it mature, and then you will either extract it, or it will break open by itself.
And if it is white, soft, and its root is thin, then that is one which should be incised.
And the operation for it is that you consider, before your operation, whether the abscess has already matured completely or if it has diminished in any way; then make the patient sit before the sun, and his head should be in your lap, and open his mouth, and the assistant, holding between his hands, should press down the patient's tongue with a very fine instrument, of which this is the form:
And it should be made of silver or bronze, and should be as thin as a knife.
Then, as you press the tongue with this and the abscess is shown to you, and you observe it carefully, then take tweezers and place them in the tonsil, and pull it outwards as much as you can. If you pull out something from the membranes, then immediately incise it with an instrument of which this is the form:
If you pull out something from the membranes, then immediately incise it with an instrument of which this is the form: and it resembles forceps, however its ends are curved, so that the opening of each is opposite the other, very sharp, which should be of tempered iron.
But if this instrument is not at hand, then incise it with a spatula of which this is the form: And let it be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other.
And after you have incised one side, incise the other in the same manner, and immediately after the incision, let the patient gargle with cold water.
But if there is a flow of blood, let him gargle with a decoction of pomegranate rind or myrtle leaves and similar styptic substances until the flow of blood is restrained, and immediately treat it until it is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses appear in the throat besides those two [near the ears], and incise those as I have said in the incision of the two sides equally.
And I once treated a woman with an abscess which had formed in her throat; this abscess was dark in color, with little feeling, which had already closed her throat, and the woman breathed with a narrow passage, and I had already forbidden her food and drink of water, and she was near death if she had lasted another day or two.
And the abscess had grown so much that it had formed two points, from which it was exiting through two openings.
Then I treated it with a fistula and placed an instrument in one of its openings, and pulled it, and a large portion was pulled out of it, and immediately I incised it from where I understood from the other opening, and immediately I opened its mouth, and I compressed its tongue, and immediately I placed the instrument in that abscess.
And immediately I excised a large part of it, and only a little blood flowed from it. Therefore, the throat of that woman was freed, and she immediately began to drink water, and soon ate food; therefore, I did not cease to incise that abscess many times for a long time, and another abscess succeeded it, which I incised until it was eliminated.
Then I took a cautery and cauterized her throat; therefore, it stopped adding [more pus or infection]; and immediately I walked around and I did not know what God had done in that regard.
Chapter 37.
Concerning the incision of a uvula when it is abscessed.
When catarrh descends to the uvula, and it is abscessed and elongated, then it is called perpendicular; and if it is thick and round in the lower part, then it is called a bubo.
But when it is cured with what we have said in the division, and your treatment does not succeed, and you see that the abscess is already outside of it and is subtle, then it is necessary that you incise that which is aggregated round; and if it is of that length, and is bloody, of dark color or black, and there is no feeling in it, then you must delay the incision of it, because in that there is danger for the patient.
And when you see it according to the manner that I have described, of its whiteness and length, you should make the patient sit and press their tongue with an instrument which preserves its shape and then place the lancet in the uvula, draw it downwards and incise it with one of the two instruments which are described in the incision of the tonsils.
And it is necessary that you do not incise more than what is adjusted to the natural limit without addition, for if you incise more, you will injure his speech.
And after the incision, use what we have recommended in the incision of the tonsils, and cure it until it is healed.
But if the patient is frightened by the incision, it is necessary to use ingenuity in cauterizing him without fear, and the manner of cauterizing him is only with sharp water (likely referring to a caustic substance), and it is that you place the patient's head on your lap, and then press their tongue with the instrument which we have mentioned, and then take the water which we have mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, and apply it with that live coal (likely a heated instrument), and apply it neither thickly nor thinly, and with that fill the concavity of the instrument of which this is the shape:
Let the extremity of it be a concavity in which the medicine is placed, like the concavity of a spoon, and place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula, and let the patient lie on his side so that the saliva or mucus from his mouth flows away from the applied medicine, so that no substance descends from it onto his throat and harms him.
And then take your hand with the medicine, and press it on the uvula for the space of half an hour until you see it become blackened and dried by the action of the medicine.
And if you wish, take cotton and wrap it around the end of the spoon, and submerge the cotton in the medicine, and insert the spoon with the cotton into a tube so that the cotton adheres to that tube, and do this as many times as necessary until you achieve the desired cauterization of the uvula, and then leave it as it will dry and fall off after three or four days.
And if you need to reapply the medicine, reapply it, and after the cauterization wipe around the uvula with cotton soaked in vinegar, and cleanse with it what remains around it from the medicine, and wash with cold water, and treat externally with an embrocation, and internally with gargles until it is healed.
And this method of treatment is safer than incision and less frightening.
Sometimes the uvula is cured by a method more subtle than cautery or incision, for it is cured by fumigation in this manner: take calamint, hyssop, oregano, rue, Arabic gum, chamomile, and similar herbs, and mix all these ingredients, or some of them, in a pot, and submerge them in sharp vinegar and boil; and let the pot be well sealed with appropriate lute, and in the middle of the pot there should be a hole, over which a concave instrument of brass should be placed, in this manner; it may be made of silver or brass.
And let the end [of the concave instrument] be inserted into the mouth of the patient so that the vapor may reach the uvula through the little tube, until the uvula is sufficiently fumigated.
And then repeat this many times until it is dry, and take care not to perform this treatment at the beginning of the development of an abscess, for it would aggravate the abscess too much, and you should not do that, but rather you must cauterize the visible part of the abscess.
But if you do not have this instrument at hand, then take a reed and fashion a hen's egg-shell at its end, so that the patient's mouth may be covered, for the eggshell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Chapter 38.
Of the extraction of thorns and that which adheres in the throat and of other causes.
Many times, a fish bone or some other thing adheres in the throat.
Then it is necessary that you extract that which is apparent of those things, upon which the sight falls, after you press the tongue with an instrument in front of the sun, so that that which is in the throat may be revealed to you.
And that which does not appear and is hidden from you in the throat, it is necessary that you make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, because very quickly the thing attached to the throat will be expelled by vomit; or that the patient swallow a piece of turnip or lettuce root or a bit of dry bread; or take a piece of dried sea sponge, and bind it strongly with gall, and then have the patient swallow it, because when it arrives at the place where the thorns are, they are extracted, then they are expelled. And moreover, take an eel and bind it with gall, and let it be inserted and gently extracted; or take a leek and gently insert it and gently remove it.
But if they are not expelled by those means which we have mentioned, then use a lead instrument according to this form:
And let it be a little thicker than a finger, and at its extremity let there be a concavity which you insert into his throat with ease.
And let him have his head raised up, and be careful not to touch his throat bone, so that it becomes loose and with that you push the bone or spine, or that the physician does that with his hand.
But however, it is better that the patient himself inserts it, because of his knowledge of the location of the attached cause, and pushes downwards, and pulls his hand with the instrument upwards, all this according to the quantity which is prepared for him until it is expelled.
Chapter 39.
On the extraction of a leech attached to the throat.
When a leech is medicated with what we have said in the division of treatment with medicines and does not loosen its hold, then consider placing the patient's throat in the sun, after you have compressed his tongue with the instrument which we have recommended to you.
Then, if you see the leech, extract it with a small hook or with gentle, decent movements.
And if it is not possible for you to extract it with that, then take a concave cannula and insert it into the patient's throat, and then place a heated iron in the concavity of the cannula, and do this as many times (until the leech is dead and detaches itself from the throat), and abstain the patient from water all that day.
Then take a vial full of cold water, and open his mouth into it, and wash his mouth with it; and do not swallow any of it and stir the water occasionally with your hand, for the leech, as soon as it feels the water, will fall off.
But if it is not expelled by what we have recommended, then fumigate the patient with cyme [likely refers to a type of herb or incense, possibly  *cymus* referring to a flower head] or with asafoetida, using the instrument that we recommended for the fumigation of the uvula.
Do this, so that it will fall out.
And there is another method of operation in the fumigation of this, which is that you take a pot in which there are burning coals and the pot is covered with a lid in the middle of which is a hole, and the end of the instrument is fitted into that hole; and then let it go above the fumigation; and let the patient put his mouth to the end of the cannula, and close his mouth so that the fumigation is not expelled until you know that the fumigation has reached the leech, for the leech will immediately fall off.
And if it does not fall off, then you repeat the fumigation many times, and abstain the patient from drinking, and eating salty things and garlic, and do not drink water, and thus it is necessary that with this regimen it will be expelled.
And this is the form of the instrument with which a leech is extracted from the throat when it is seen; and it is similar to forceps like this, except that it has some curvature in it: which is placed in the throat, and its end is similar to a stork's beak, which is hollow, in which there is a roughness of a file, so that, when it grasps something, it does not let go.
Chapter 40.
In which is the summary of the discourse on the appearance of abscesses and on their incision.
Abscesses are of many species, of many manners, according to what is remembered of them, of each one in this book.
And they are diverse in perforation and incision in two ways, of which one is by the species of abscesses themselves; and because of what we understand of the humors.
And the second manner is according to the locations in which they occur in the body, for an abscess occurring in the head is different from an abscess occurring in the buttocks; and an abscess in a fleshy location is different from an abscess occurring in a joint; and each of them has its own judgment for operation.
And moreover, of abscesses, there are some which it is necessary not to perforate except near the maturation of the pus within them or their completion.
There are other abscesses which should be opened when they are raw and not matured according to their completion, such as abscesses that are near the joints, because when an abscess is near a joint, its being is prolonged until that which is in its circumference can be removed, and very quickly the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted, wherefore a cause of contraction of that limb is made; or it is an abscess near a principal member, and if you delay its perforation until it is matured, you will harm the principal member; or it is near the anus, therefore perforate it even unmatured; and it is not necessary to perforate it raw, of uncompleted digestion, except that which is near the anus, so that its pus may not spread and be elongated to the inner parts of the anus, and a fistula be made, or that it be made in such a way that it may not be healed. Moreover, you must know the time of the perforation of the abscess, and this is when it is matured.
And know that it is matured when there is a lessening of the pain and the cessation of the fever, and also the diminution of the redness and of the throbbing, and the acuteness of the head of the abscess, and the other signs.
And because it is necessary that you know that the perforation should fall in the lowest place of the abscess and from where the pus comes, for the cure of the swelling.
And the perforation should always be along the length of the flesh or body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands or feet, and away from the tendons, the cords of the nerves, the arteries; and, finally, in all even places in which there is no reflection; for in those which are reflected, use perforation according to that member.
But when the abscess is in a fleshy place, then it is better that you leave the perforation of it until its digestion is completed, as we have said; for if you perforate it before that, the pus is prolonged and much filth remains, and quickly its lips are hardened and its depth increased.
But some abscesses are perforated according to the width of the flesh or body, due to necessity, or according to what the member requires.
And it is necessary that you administer a small perforation in small abscesses, and in large abscesses a large and wide perforation, or many incisions, according to the diversity of the size of the abscesses.
And others are abscesses in which it is necessary that the cone be hollowed out and incised, when it is made like bread and is made to the point of suppuration, such as those which happen in cases of buboes and swellings.
And some of those are incised with a three-angled incision; and others are incised in the shape of a myrtle leaf, like the abscesses of the armpits.
And some of them are those in which a round incision and a lunar incision are administered, and similar to those.
But that which is not like the aforementioned superficial abscesses, you must perforate it with a superficial or simply flat perforation.
And when the abscess is large and has collected much pus, you should perforate it and not worry about removing all the pus at once; having extracted a portion, then pack the abscess until the next day, and then extract a larger portion, and do this many times gradually until it is all discharged.
And especially if the patient is of weak constitution, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an old person in decrepitude, because the animal spirit is often dissolved with the egress of the pus, and the patient dies very quickly, and you open them. (referring to the abscesses)
Therefore, observe this chapter with great caution, and concerning your perforation in which you perforate these abscesses, you must clean the wounds and consider then if the wound of the abscess is small, or is a flat incision, then you administer lint or old linen; and if the abscess is large, and the fissures of the perforation are many, then you must place a wick in each opening until they join together.
And if a part of the intestine is severed in the abscess or you have hollowed it out in the circumference, then you must fill it with old linen or thread made from linen cloth, as when you have drawn out splinters or roots, without moisture; and bind it up until the third day; and then treat it and cure it with the ointments that are necessary until it is healed.
But if you encounter a flow of blood during your operation, then you should apply cold water and sour wine in which you soak a linen cloth, and apply this over the place of the bleeding many times.
But if the flow of blood persists, then you must administer the regimen and treatment and powders which I have recounted in many places in this our book and division.
But if it is in winter, and the location of the abscess is a place of many nerves, then you must mix powders in hot wine and oil, and apply those to the locations.
And if it is in summer, and the locations are fleshy, then you should apply powders mixed with water and oil or with wine and oil, and all this should be cold until the third day, as we have said, and then you should open the abscess and cleanse it and administer to its treatment what is suitable for it until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary for the knowledge of the treatment of abscesses universally, but according to the way of distinction I have already said of each abscess in what manner is the way of its treatment by chapters individually.
Chapter 41.
Concerning the incision of abscesses which appear in the scalp.
In the scalp, small abscesses are found, and they are a species of nodes which contain tunics that are adherent to them; and their species are many.
And among them are some that contain a humidity similar to slime. And among them are some that contain a humidity similar to pus, and similar to them; and among them are those that are stony, hard.
And in the incision of all of them, and in the extraction of those, there is no fear, since the extraction of those does not cause contrary effects.
And the operation in the incision of these is that you probe them with an instrument whose shape is suitable for this, which is called a probe, until you know what it contains.
For if what it contains is humidity, then incise it lengthwise with a superficial incision according to this form:
And begin the incision from line B to line G. Then, when the humidity is evacuated, you scrape out that skin which contained that humidity, and do not leave any of it at all, for many times it returns if some of it remains.
And then immerse a swab in Egyptian ointment, if it is available, and if not, in salt water; and fill the wound with this and leave it there until the next day, for with this you corrode all that remains of that film/membrane.
And then repeat applying that swab with Egyptian ointment a second and third time if needed, until you are confident that there is nothing remaining of that membrane; then cure the wound with ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess contains fatty nodules, then you incise it in this way: with an incision in the form of a cross, and insert forceps between the two lips of the incision and scrape it from every part, and exert your strength in extracting the membrane which contains it.
But if the arrowhead opposes you, then do what I recounted to you firstly.
And similarly treat a lithified abscess with incision and cure as we have told you; and incision on a lithified abscess is easier, because it has little blood and little humidity.
And sometimes some of these abscesses are in the head of some men, in which there is no humidity at all; the reason is that I incised an abscess in the head of an old woman and found the abscess like a dull, rough, white stone, which could not be broken, and if anyone were struck with it, it would hurt them.
But in other accidental abscesses in the head other than this, this is not the chapter, such as the abscesses that appear on the heads of children and behind the roots of the ears, but the incision of all these should be a superficial incision.
And make the perforation of those from the lowest part of them so that the body is relieved of the pus in the lowest parts.
And then treat those with what is appropriate for their cure.
Chapter 42.
Chapter 42. Of the incision of scrofulas which happen in the neck.
Often these abscesses happen in the neck and in the armpits and in the groin; and sometimes they happen in other parts of the body.
And those which happen in the neck, many are generated among themselves, and every scrofula of those is within its own proper tunic, and just as in the nodes and abscesses of the head, as we have recounted.
And the species of these scrofulas are many, for some of them are stony, and some of them contain humidity, and some of them are malignant which do not obey treatment.
Therefore, in that which you see of good disposition to the touch, of which the color that is manifest is similar to the color of the flesh, and is softened in every part, and is not attached to the nerves of the neck, nor to the veins, nor to the artery, and is not deep, it is necessary that you incise it with a superficial incision from the upper part to the lower part of the body from the line G to the line B, and scrape it from every part, and extend the lips of the flesh with hooks, or with many hooks if you have need of them, as we have said in the abscesses of the head, and extract it little by little.
And it is most important that the vein or nerve not be incised.
And let the spatula not be very sharp, so that it does not injure your hand with the incision, or that it disturbs the patient, and that you incise only what is necessary to incise; for if you incise a vein or artery and prohibit yourself from your operation, then place on the wound crushed vitriol or other powders which stop the flow of blood, and bind the wound, and leave it until the acuteness of the abscess is reduced and the wound is softened and begins to be suppurated. When the blood is cut off.
Then return to your operation until you have completed it.
And then inquire with your finger if there is any cause of other small scrophulas, then incise those and clean those.
And if there is a large vein at the root of the scrofula, then it is necessary that you do not incise that scrofula at its root. Furthermore, it is necessary that you tie it with strong doubled thread and that you leave it until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old linen which you have already submerged in Egyptian ointment, and then take care of it.
And if you incise the whole scrofula, then you must adjust the two lips of the wound and suture it as soon as there is no remaining superfluity.
But if you see that there remains superfluity because of the size of the scrofula, you must then consider incising its root, and extract it, and administer suture and what has been said.
And for those scrofulas that contain humidity, perforate it with a superficial perforation where the place of its maturation appears to you, and place the perforation so that it is the lowest part of the body, as we have said.
Then administer a perforation, perhaps with ointment and similar things, so that it corrodes what remains of the corruption. And then, when you see the wound already cleansed, you cure it with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
Chapter 43.
Concerning the incision of the core of the abscess accidentally occurring in the throat.
The ancients described this incision in the core, and no one in our land has been seen to have performed it.
And this is the written account of the words of those (ancients).
But in men having a quinsy, the extraction of the core should be avoided, because it is not beneficial, since all the veins and major blood vessels of the patient are located there.
But in those who have abscesses located in the mouth, throat, or ears, when the illness is not in the throat (windpipe/trachea), it is necessary to use the incision of the core to avoid the damage that is done by perforation.
Therefore, when we want to do this, we should incise the core below three circles of the circles of the core, or four, with a small incision in width, in the area between two circles in quantity, so that the incision is in the soft tissue (muscle), not in the cartilage, and this location is suitable for the incision, because it is free of flesh and its blood vessels are far away.
And if the physician is timid, then he should extend the membrane of the throat with a hook, and then carefully split the membrane until he reaches the canal, but he should avoid the blood vessels if he sees any of them.
And then incise the soft tissue as we have recommended; and the incision of the canal is indicated by the inflammation which comes from it, with that which is ruptured from it, and by the loss of voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
Therefore, when the time has come in which perforation is feared, bring together the two lips of the wound's membrane, and sew only that, without the cartilage.
And then administer medicines that promote the growth of flesh until it is healed.
And Albucasis says that the summary of the discourse which we have recounted, the explanation is only that you see that some of these abscesses have already obstructed the throat of the patient, and the patient has come close to death, and his breath is cut off, and some proceed to incise the epiglottis so that the patient may breathe over the place of the wound for some respiration, and be saved from death.
And for that reason I order the wound to be left open until the malignancy of the illness is consumed; and its malignancy is for three days; and then I order the wound to be sewn, and cure it until it is healed.
And this which I have seen with my own eyes, is that a servant took a knife and put it in her throat and incised with it a part of the windpipe, and I was called to her cure, and I found her groaning as one groans from being wounded, and I uncovered the wound and found the blood which had come out of the small wound.
Then I was assured that she had not incised a vein or artery, and wind was escaping from the wound, and I cured it until it was healed.
And nothing happened to the servant except hoarseness in her voice, and after a few days she returned to a better state of health.
And now I say that in the dissection of an epileptic there is no fear.
Chapter 44.
Chapter 44. Concerning the incision of an abscess which occurs in the throat externally, and is called a throat hernia.
This abscess, called a throat hernia, is a large abscess according to the complexion of the body, and is very common in women.
And it is of two kinds, for it is either natural or accidental.
And the natural kind is not possible to cure.
And the accidental kind is of two types, one resembling fatty nodes, and the other resembling an abscess which is caused by the knotting of an artery, and in this there is danger. And one should not presumptuously use iron on it except in the case of a small one. And you should probe and investigate it with your finger, and if you find it similar to a fatty node, and it is not connected to any of the veins, then you incise it like the nodes, and extract it with what contains it from the skin and blister; and if not, then its entire nature should be investigated, and then treat the area with whatever is needed for healing until it is healed.
Chapter 45.
Concerning the incision of types of nodes.
There are many types of nodes, and I have already described all their types in the division (or classification).
And it is necessary that I recount thus the difference between a node and an ulcer, as it causes doubt.
Therefore I say that with an ulcer there is heat and fever and vehement pain until the boiling (inflammation) of the superfluity is sedated; and putrefaction is complete, then the fever, pain, and heat cease.
But with a node there is neither heat nor fever nor pain, and it contains that  leonine [or lion-like]  cyphose which is in its own sac.
And it is according to the color of the body, and its beginning is like a swelling and is made like a blister, whether larger or smaller, and they are of two types, for they are either greasy and contain humidity, and the colors of the humidity are many, as I said in the division (or classification).
Therefore it is necessary, when you arrive at the cure of a node, that you express that and investigate it at the beginning with an instrument which is called an intromes, according to how its form will appear in the chapter which is near this one.
And the manner of investigating abscesses and all nodes is that you take this instrument and insert it into the most humid place which you find in the abscess, and you will revolve your fingers with it little by little until you know that the instrument has penetrated the core.
And then place your hand according to the measure of the size of the abscess, and then extract the intromes and consider what has come out in its wake.
And if the flowing humidity, of whatever color it may be, is expelled, then you should incise it with a superficial incision as I have said in the case of other abscesses.
And if you find no humidity in the wake of the intromes, then know that it is fatty (or greasy).
And dry it out with an incision in the form of a cross, according to this form: Just as I have shown you, and suspend it with hooks, and remove the skin from all sides easily, and keep the skin or sac, if you can, over it and extract that pus with the knot.
But if it is broken during the operation, it is not possible to extract all the pus, and this often happens, and then you extract it in pieces until nothing remains.
Because if anything remains, little or much, the knot (or swelling) will return accordingly.
And if you see that some small part remains, then fill the wound with some sharp corrosive powders and bind the wound tightly, and place over it a hot poultice, and treat it with other treatments until it is healed.
And if it is a large knot (or swelling), then you sew the two lips of it together and treat it with that which causes it to heal by granulation (or causes flesh to grow).
But if a pulsating swelling is present, or a non-pulsating one, then you cauterize and fill the area with crushed vitriol and leave it tightly bound for two or three days until the wound dries and the boiling of the blood subsides; and then return to incising what remains of the swelling.

Chapter 47.
Concerning the care of the breasts of men which resemble those of women.
Sometimes the breasts of some men are inflamed due to the habit of sleeping, as they begin to become hairy, and then the breasts are inflamed, and resemble the breasts of women, and remain as purulent, festering sores.
And to one who has this, it is necessary that he incise those with a lunate incision, according to this shape, from line B to line G, and then scrape away all the thickness, and then fill the wound with incarnative medicine, and then join the two lips of the wound with suture, and treat that until it is healed.
But if the breast sags downwards and is softened by its size as happens in women, then let it be cut into two halves at its most prominent point, with two incisions resembling a lunar shape, each contained within the other by the end of each, so that the larger line contains the smaller according to this form, from line B and line G. And then scrape the thickened skin which is between the two lips of the wound, and remove the fat, and administer what we have said about the suture and powders that are necessary in the healing until it is healed.
And if you do not complete the incision of what needs to be incised because of the patient's restlessness and bleeding, then you must fill the wound with cotton soaked in corrosive ointment and leave it until it corrodes the remaining fat, and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
Of the perforation of abscesses that appear under the armpit.
These abscesses that appear under the armpit are a type of scrofula, hard, containing a sac-like skin.
And some of those contain humidity; and those that contain humidity should be incised with a lunar incision according to this figure: from line G to line B. And when all the pus in the abscess has been evacuated, then you fill it with old linen and leave it there until the next day.
And then the linen should be removed and it should be treated with a suitable ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess is of the type of nodes and of scrofula, then you incise it as we have said in the section on scrofula above.
But if the wound is extensive so that it produces pus, then administer cautery to it as we have said before.
Chapter 49.
Concerning the incision of abscesses that occur from an arrow or a vein.
When an artery is broken, and the flesh that is over it is consolidated, then often an abscess occurs. And the signs by which it is known that inflammation and abscess are due to an artery or a vein: for when an abscess is due to an artery, it is long and aggregated in the front of the body; and when you press the abscess with your fingers, you feel in it as if it were a creaking sound.
And that which is due to a vein is round on the surface of the body.
And incision of this abscess is an error, especially in that which is below the armpits, and in the groins, and in the neck, and in many places of the body; and it is very large, therefore its treatment with iron should be avoided, and that which is in the extremities, or on the head, should be avoided.
But concerning that which is there due to inflammation of the opening of the arrow wound, make a long incision in the flesh; and then widen the incision with hooks and near the arrow, and free it from the tunics until it is uncovered.
And then place a needle on it and make it penetrate to the other side, and tighten the arrow with double thread in two places — which method I have shown you in the extraction of two arrows which are in the temples.
And then puncture the place which is between two ligaments with a spatula until all the blood which is there comes out, and the abscess is dissolved.
And then administer treatment in which pus is generated until the ligaments fall away; and then treat that with ointments suitable for this until it is healed.
And if it is an abscess due to a severed vein, then it is necessary that you hold with your hand that which is possible of the abscess with the flesh, and then insert the needle below the place which you have held under your hand, and make it penetrate; and let there be double thread in it so that you extract it from the other side, and then tie the abscess with good thread as I have told you in the treatment of a grape-sized [abscess] in two parts with two ends of the thread.  (The "uva4" likely refers to a size comparison – perhaps a small cluster of four grapes, or just generally small).
But if you fear that it will pull out the thread, then insert the thread with that needle below the whole abscess below the incision of the first needle, and tighten your threads in four places.
Then dry the abscess in its middle so that when that which is in it has come out, then you incise the superfluous flesh, and leave that which is tied in it.
And then place on it a powder which you have submerged in wine and oil, and then administer the treatment which is done with poultices and ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 1 of the abscess which happens because of a twisted nerve.
Just as an abscess happens in an artery and in a vein, similarly an abscess happens in the nerves when it happens in their percussion or happens because of excessive work, and similarly.
And it is made worse in the places of the joints or of the heel, or in any place where the joints are weakened.
And the abscess is serious, and it resembles the color of the other body parts, and it is in the worst dispositions of them and when it is squeezed with force, the patient feels a similar stupor.
And the abscess is not located in the front of the body, and therefore it is below the ribs, and it is weakened in every part and does not go to the front or back parts.
Therefore, that which is in the joints should not be invaded with iron, for contraction will very quickly result.
But that which is in the head or on the forehead, finish [treat] it with a spatula. But if it is a small abscess, then take it with forceps and excise it from the root.
And if it is large, then you suspend it with a hook, and incise it, and then remove it; and close the wound with sutures, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 51.
Of the incision of an abscess which happens to the one which happens in the belly.  (More literally: Of the incision of an abscess which happens from that which happens to the belly.)
It happens to some men many times in their bellies and in other parts of their bodies,  growths which are called fungals by similarity to fungi, of which the root is subtle and the head is large; and their lips are sometimes inverted, and some are small, and some are very large.
And I have seen one, and in his belly were two growths which were very similar to fungi, and there was no difference between them, white, with a thin root.
And their lips were inverted and split, and humidity flowed from them constantly.
Therefore I incised them and found in one a weight of eighteen ounces, and in the other six ounces.
And the operation in their incision is that you consider then if the patient is humid, and the color of the growth is white, humid, with a subtle root; then you incise it with a broad spatula, and let cauteries be in your presence in the fire, because many times after the section of it much blood is expelled; and if the blood overcomes you, cauterize it.
And if you see the patient fearful and timid, guard yourself from incision with iron; but then take freshly made lead plaster, and bind the growth with it, in the manner which we have described; and leave it for two days.
And then adjust the tightness of the lead thread; therefore, do not cease tightening the lead strongly around the growth, until it is cut off and falls by itself without effort.
But if the growth is of thick root, then in its incision there is some cause for fear and dread, and especially that which is in the belly.
But it is possible that you incise half of it, and then cauterize it so that it does not return.
And then care for it until the wound is healed.
And beware that you do not presume to incise a growth which is of dark color, little feeling, and thin appearance, for that is likely cancerous.
And I come to the remembrance of cancer concerning this.
Chapter 52.
On the treatment of umbilical hernia.
Umbilical hernia is caused by many things; either by fissures of the diaphragm which is above the belly, and from it the membrane or intestine protrudes as it does in other ruptures; or by blood which flows from a vein or artery as previously mentioned; or by constricted wind within it.
But if it is due to a fissure of the diaphragm and a protrusion of the membrane, then the color of the swelling is similar to the color of the body; and it is soft without pain, and it appears in various locations. And if it is due to the protrusion of the intestine, then its location is as we have recounted, of even greater variability, and when you press it with your fingers it is hidden, and quickly returns; and it is very easily replaced, and is greatly increased by the entry of a bath and strenuous exertion.
But if it is due to humidity, then it is soft and is not hidden when you press it with your hand, nor is it constricted, nor does it grow.
And if it is due to blood, then the swelling appears with these signs, tending towards blackness.
But if it is due to rotten flesh, then the swelling is serious, large and hard, and is fixed in a certain place.
But if it is due to flatulence, then touching it feels soft.
And the operation on it is that you consider then if the swelling of the umbilicus is from arterial blood, or from a vein, or from flatulence; then it is necessary that you abstain from its treatment, because in that there is fear and dread, as I have taught you in the chapter in which I have described swellings from an artery and from a vein.
And if the swelling of the umbilicus is due to the intestine or the membrane, then you must order the patient to hold their breath and lie down; and then mark with a probe the circumference of the entire umbilicus, and then order him to lie between your hands on his back, and then surround with a broad spatula the circumference of the umbilicus over the area that you have marked with the probe; and then extend half of the swelling upwards with a large finger; and then tie it around with strong thread or with linen, tying it properly.
And then open the middle of the extended swelling over the ligature and insert your finger into that opening and look for the intestine; and if you have found it and held it by the ligature, then you carefully extract and push the intestine back into the abdomen.
And if you have found the membrane, then grasp it with a finger; and cut away its excesses.
But if it is an artery or vein, then surround that area, and then return to your operation; and take two needles and thread strong threads into them and insert the two needles into the circle which you have made around the swelling in the manner of a cross, making them penetrate; and then tighten the swelling in four parts over the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the area until the extended flesh dries and falls off by itself; or cut it away when it is thinned and dried, and then treat it with whatever ointments are appropriate until it is healed.
And if the prominence of the umbilicus is from flesh growing there or from moisture, then it is necessary that you incise the swelling as we have said and extract the blood or moisture which you find there, and then treat it with what is appropriate and what will consolidate the area.
Chapter 53.
Concerning the treatment of cancer.
We have already discussed, in the division [of the text/subject], the types of cancer and in what manner it is possible to treat it with medicines, and what is to be avoided in its treatment with iron so that it is not ulcerated; and we have mentioned cancer engendered by the mother [i.e., inherent predisposition] and the caution in its treatment.
And the ancients said that sometimes cancer is in a location where its eradication is possible, such as cancer which is in the breasts or in the groin and similar members, to extract all of it, and especially when it is small at the beginning.
But when it is old and large, it is not fitting to attempt it, for I could not heal anyone of it, nor have I seen another who has succeeded in this.
And the operation on it, when it is located as we have said, is that you proceed and purge the patient, and remove black bile sometimes, and then bleed him, if manifest repletion is found.
And then prepare the patient with a preparation in which he is placed for the operation, and then apply to the wound ointments which are suitable for it; and then excavate from all sides with a suitable curved knife, carefully, until no part of its roots remain. And let the blood flow, and do not staunch it lightly, and press the areas, and wipe away the thick blood entirely with your hand or with an instrument which is available to you among the instruments.
But if a large flow of blood from a vein incision or artery gives you trouble, then cauterize it until the flow of blood is stopped, and then treat it with the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 44.  (Assuming a standard chapter numbering system)
Concerning the treatment of dropsy.
We have already recounted, in the division, the types of dropsy from water, and the signs of each type, and their treatment with medicines.
And that which is cured with iron is only the type of ascites, and iron is not applied to the type of tympanites or the type of anasarca, for that is mortal (fatal).
And when you have treated this type of tympanitic dropsy with medicines and your treatment is not effective, then consider: for if the patient has already developed weakness, or has another disease along with this dropsy, such as a cough or abdominal pain and similar ailments, then beware of treating them with iron, for there is danger in that.
But if you see the patient with exuberant strength, and with no other illness but dropsy alone, and neither a child nor an old person, then the manner of operating on them is that you raise the patient between your hands, and the assistant gently presses their abdomen with their hand, driving the water towards the area below the pubic bone, and then take a sharp lancet in this manner:
Let it be two-edged, long, with a sharp point like a lancet; but nevertheless, let there be some shortness in it, little by little, so that in operating with it you do not pierce the intestine and injure it.
And then consider if the dropsy originates from the intestines, for then it is necessary that with the incision you distance yourself from the navel by the amount of three fingers towards the lower parts, directly above the pubic bone.
But if the origin of the dropsy is due to liver disease, then your incision should be to the left of the navel by three fingers.
And if its origin is from the spleen, then your incision should be on the right.
And it is not fitting that your incision be on the side on which the patient wishes to lie, lest the superfluities flow to that weak place.
Then perforate the entire peritoneum with the instrument, and then introduce it into that incision, and raise your hand with the spatula between the peritoneum and the viscera, and you will scrape that; and if [it is] the quantity of a fingernail or thereabouts.
Then perforate the viscera until the spatula reaches the empty place, which is the location of the water. Then extract that and introduce an instrument of which this is the form: and it is similar to a fine silver cannula; it is made of silver or bronze or tin, to which in its lowest part there is a small hole, and on its sides there are three holes: two on one side and one on the other; and its end is incised in the manner of a feather, thus:
And at its uppermost end is a ring, because when the instrument reaches the water, then that immediately settles on the instrument, therefore evacuating the water in a half-hour quantity, for if you evacuate more of that than is necessary at the time, very quickly the patient dies from the dissolution of his animal spirit, or a fainting fit befalls him which is near death.
But evacuate that according to the quantity of its virtue and according to what the patient's dispositions, the virtue of his pulse, and his well-being signify to you.
Then extract the instrument, and retain the water, the reason being that it is immediately retained by the chain which holds the hole that is above the cup, of which I have told you that you should perforate it in that manner.
And then reinsert the instrument the next day if you see the patient tolerating it, and extract a small quantity of water, and do this for several days cautiously, and guard against error so that only a little water remains.
But if you fear for the patient, and leave a large quantity of water, then treat him with burial in warm sand, and induce sweating in a bath, and expose him to the sun for a long time; and treat him with drying medicines until he is healed.
And cauterize the stomach, the spleen, and the liver after the extraction of the water, as I have recounted in what precedes.
Chapter 54.  (Assuming a chapter numbering system.)
Concerning the treatment of children who are born with imperforate urinary tracts.
Sometimes a child is born from its mother's womb and the head of its penis is not perforated (or the opening is narrow or not in its proper place).
Therefore, it is necessary to treat it with perforation as soon as it is born, using a very fine probe, according to this method:
And then place a fine lead pin into the opening and leave it there for three or four days.
And when it wants to urinate, it should be stimulated and will urinate; and then replace the pin.
And if you do not place the lead pin, it is possible that the urine, which flows over the area, will not allow it to close.
But that in which the opening is narrow should be treated with a lead pin, as we have said, for several days until it is widened.
But those in whom the opening is not in its place, so it is said, the cause is that for them, the one that is born and the opening is below the end of the penis, so that he cannot urinate towards the inner parts, until he raises his penis with his hand towards the upper parts; and it is not generative for him, because the semen cannot reach the womb in a straight line, and it is a very long-lasting illness.
And the manner of the operation on him is that the patient should be placed on his back, and then extend the head of his penis with your left hand with a forceful pull, and incise the head of the penis at the location of the prepuce with a sharp scalpel, like a feather incision, or that you shave some part, so that the middle of it becomes similar to the head of the penis and that the opening falls in the middle place as it should.
And beware of the bleeding in your operation, for this often happens; and stop it with something that will staunch the blood, and care for the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 56.
Of phymosis, which occurs in the prepuce at the head of the penis, and of corruption and blackness and adhesion of the prepuce to the head of the penis.
Often this phymosis occurs on the penis and is a swelling of fleshy tissue.
And of these, one is malignant and the other is not malignant.
For the non-malignant type, you should suspend it with a fine thread and excise it until you have completely cleaned it; and then place on that wound something immersed in Egyptian ointment; and after that, treat it with diafinicon ointment until it is healed.
And if the phymosis is malignant, of a dark color, then you should apply cautery near its incision and its root.
But if the phymosis is on the foreskin of the uncircumcised penis, and one part of the phymosis is inside the foreskin and the other part is outside, then you should remove the phymosis that is inside first until it is consolidated; and then you treat the outside part, because when you treat both together, it is not assured that the foreskin will not be damaged.
And if you find in the genitals and foreskin blackness and corruption, then you should remove everything that is already blackened and is beginning to corrupt or is already corrupt; and then apply to it a liniment of honey, which is mixed with crushed and sieved pomegranate bark flour and crushed and sieved nuts.
And then treat it with another remedy until it is healed.
But if there is a flow of blood, then apply lunar cautery according to this method:
because cautery is helpful for two conditions at once, that is, for bleeding and for a wound when it is corrupted.
And if the head of the penis is corroded and completely destroyed, then you must insert into the meatus of the prepuce a lead cannula so that the patient urinates through it.
In the adhesion of the prepuce to the head of the penis (and this adhesion does not occur except in one whose prepuce is healthy, and circumcision is not required; and when adhesion occurs due to a wound or abscess) it is necessary that you scrape with a blunt spatula until the ligament is dissolved and the head of the penis is freed from all parts.
And if it is difficult to separate these exquisitely, then you must scrape with a spatula some part of the head of the penis and the prepuce, and this is because the prepuce, when thin, is very easily perforated by its slight thinness.
And then separate the prepuce from the head of the penis with a very thin linen cloth, which has already been moistened in cold water so that they do not adhere again.
And then let it be treated with styptic wine until it is consolidated (healed).
Chapter 57.
Concerning the cleansing of children and the treatment of what happens to those who are circumcised.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity like other wounds; however, since it is of our operation and our will, and since it is performed primarily on infants, it is necessary that we assign to it the best operation and the easiest method leading to health.
Therefore I say that the ancients did not describe circumcision in any of their books, because they did not use it in their laws, and it is only because we have acquired it through experience, which is why I have found the majority of surgeons and barbers using circumcision and cleansing with a lancet and with tweezers; and they use ligature with thread and incision with the fingernail.
And I have tried all these methods and I find no better cleansing with scissors, with ligature with thread than cleansing with the fingernail, because many times the skin escapes higher up, because the foreskin has two layers, and the lower layer remains, and is easily forced to another incision and certain pain. And with cleansing which is done with an awl, and is digital, which cobblers use, the incision of the tip of the penis is not safe, because it easily enters the hole.
But in the operation with a fingernail, it easily becomes rusty and corrupts your operation. And the foreskin of a child is short by nature, and many times it is born in such a way that it has no need of cleansing, and I have seen this.
But of the cleansing that is done with scissors and ligatures with thread, the goodness, experience has shown me, because the scissors are of proportional incision, for this reason that the sharpness that is above is like the sharpness that is below; and therefore as you hold your hand, two sharpnesses are proportioned, and you incise according to a comparison at one time; and compression is made of the thread with a shape containing the foreskin of the penis from all parts, with which no error is made at all.
And the manner of the first operation is that you make the child stay, especially if it is one of those who understands a little, that you do nothing but tie the thread on its penis only, and that you leave it until the next day.
And then do that in whatever way is possible for you, given its understanding. And then make the child lie still between your hands, prepared and not at all restless; and hide the scissors in your hand or under your foot, so that the child does not see them or any other of the instruments.
And then put your hand to his penis and blow on the foreskin, and pull it until the head of the penis is revealed, and then cleanse it of whatever dirt is gathered there.
The shape of the scissors for circumcision, this is the shape:
Let one end be non-cutting and the other cutting, so that the nail's head does not come out, and let the length of the two sharp edges be like the length of two equally sized handles.
But the error in circumcision is that you too quickly remove the entire foreskin, or part of it, below the incision.
Therefore, it is necessary that you stretch that [skin] with your fingernail before the suppuration of the area and that you incise it evenly.
And if you cannot hold it with your fingernail, then pull it with forceps and incise it.
But if three days have passed, and what is swollen remains under the inflamed penis, then you should wait until the hot abscess has subsided, and remove that carefully, and incise it as you have prepared.
And be careful of the head of the penis, for it should not be injured.
And then treat it with that which consolidates the wound, from the powders which we have recommended in the treatise on powders.
And if it is incised beyond measure from the scrotum and affects the parts above, this will not harm him.
And furthermore, treat it with what we have said until it is healed.
Chapter 58.
Concerning the treatment of urine retained in the bladder.
Retention of urine in the bladder is caused by obstruction from a stone, or congealed blood, or pus, or flesh, and similar to those things we have spoken of in the methods of treatment in the division [of the text/chapters].
And if it is not dissolved by what we have said, and the cause is severe for the patient, then it is necessary to administer extraction with an instrument which is called a syringe, of which this is the form: Let it be of silver and be subtly hollowed like a bird's quill, with a thinness of a reed, long in the likeness of a palm and a half, to which there is a thin tube at its head.
And the manner of drawing out the urine with this is that you take a doubled bladder and tie wool or cotton at its end with a good ligature; and introduce the ends of the bladder into the part below the syringe, and cut with scissors if any excess wool remains, but introduce it into the tube forcefully. And then grease the syringe with butter and egg white.
And make the patient sit on a saddle, and rub his bladder and penis with moist oils, and olive oil and rosewater.
And then introduce the syringe easily until it reaches the root of the penis; and then bend the penis upwards towards the navel; and then push the syringe inside that; and then push it until it reaches the bladder, and this shows the patient that it has reached the empty space, and this is not done according to this order, but the meatus where it goes is tortuous.
And then extend the bladder with the wool little by little, because the urine will soak the wool.
Then extract it and do this for urinary cure, and use a syringe; do not cease doing this and using a syringe until the bladder is emptied and the patient finds relief.
Chapter 59.
Concerning the method of clystering the bladder with a clamp and similar instruments suitable for this purpose.
When an ulceration or abscess occurs in the bladder, or blood clots in it, or putrefaction is constricted there, and you wish to instill waters and medicines into it, then it should be done with an instrument that is called a clamp, and this is its form:
except that its end is blunt, that is, closed. And in it there are three holes: two on one side, thus, twice.
At the beginning, there is a concave place, into which that which is to be expelled is placed, and it should be according to the quantity that fills it, without addition, until when anything is drawn from the humidities with it, it is drawn out, and when it is expelled, with it let it be drawn out, and when expelled far, as the clamp does with which naphtha is thrown in sea battles.
Therefore, when you wish to draw out the humors from the bladder, insert the end of the clamp into the humor, and draw with it that which is pressed towards the upper parts, for the humor is drawn into the concavity of the clamp.
And then insert the end of the clamp into the urethra as we have recounted in the syringe; and then expel the humor with that which you expel, for that humor reaches the bladder immediately, and the patient feels it.
The form of the subtle catheter with which the bladder is catheterized, and this is its form:
And let it be made of silver or brass, of which the uppermost part should resemble a small feather, and below it a narrow, smooth canal.
Then take a goatskin bladder, and place in it the liquid that you wish to instill into the bladder, and then tie it tightly between two [points] with a strong double ligature, and warm that liquid over the fire.
And then insert the end of the catheter into the penis.
And then squeeze your hand on the bladder with the liquid with gentle pressure until the patient feels that the liquid has reached the bladder.
But if you do not have a bladder at hand, then take a piece of parchment and make a circle from it; and perforate it all around its circumference; and then insert strong thread into these holes, so that it is tightly bound; and then bind the circle so that it is sealed shut, after you place in it what you wish of liquids, oils, and waters.
Then tie this to the instrument and proceed with it as you did with the bladder, and express it with your hand until the liquid reaches the bladder.
Chapter 60.
On the extraction of a stone.
We have already discussed in the division the types of stones and their treatment with medicines, and the difference between stones generated in the kidneys and stones generated in the bladder, and their symptoms.
And we have discussed the stone in which one operates by hand and incision.
And the stone generated in the bladder properly is that which is lodged in the bladder.
And I am narrating that with clear exposition and brevity.
Therefore I say that stones generated in the bladder mostly affect children; and among its symptoms is that the urine that is expelled from the bladder is similar to water in its thinness, and sand appears in it; and the patient rubs his genitals, and often cries out in pain, and is restless; and with this, the anus protrudes in many of them.
And the cure of children from this is easier until they have reached fourteen years; and it is a difficult cure in the elderly. But in young people it is moderate because it is between those [extremes/ages].
And of that [disease] in which the stone is larger, the cure is easier; and if it is small, it is contrary to that [i.e., more difficult].
Therefore, to arrive at the cure of this, it is necessary at the beginning that we purify the patient with a clyster which removes all the waste that is in his bowels; for this waste sometimes prevents the discovery of the stone through its examination.
Then let the patient be taken with his feet raised and be massaged and shaken gently so that the stone descends to the bottom of the bladder, or that it sometimes jumps from its place.
Then make that [patient's] body between your hands prepared, and his hands should be below his thighs, so that the whole bladder is made to incline downwards.
And then search for it [the stone] and all that is outside.
Therefore, if you feel the stone in the space, then immediately you should proceed with an incision upon him.
And if it does not fall completely under your touch, then it is necessary that you anoint with oil your index finger of the left hand, if the patient is a child, or the middle finger if he is a grown young man, and insert that into his anus. And search for the stone until it is under your finger. And bring it back little by little up to the neck of the bladder.
Then press on it with your finger, and expel it outwards to the place where you made the incision.
And command the assistant to press the bladder with her hand; and command another assistant that with her right hand she extend the genitals upwards, and with her other hand she support the perineum which is below the genitals, near the place where the incision is made.
And then introduce a well-sharpened spatula, of which this is the form: and cut what is between the anus and the genitals, not in the middle, but towards the left thigh; and let the incision be above the stone and your finger in the anus, and let the incision be transverse, so that the external incision is wide and the internal narrow, according to the size of the stone that is possible to extract, and no larger, for very quickly by pressing with your finger which is in the anus the stone will come out of the incision, and will be expelled without difficulty.
And know that some stones are angular and have sharp edges, which makes their extraction difficult.
And of those that are like a gland, and are round, the extraction is easily done.
Therefore, if it is one that has angles and edges, adjust it in the fissure.
And if it is not expelled in this way, then you must press on it, or squeeze it with a suitable instrument, the tip of which is like a file with which you press on the stone, and it does not escape; or insert underneath a thin instrument with a curved tip.
But if you cannot reach it, then slightly enlarge the opening, and if you see a flow of blood, staunch it with crushed vitriol.
But if there is more than one stone, then initially place your hand at the opening of the bladder, and then incise it; and then gently push it; and similarly do if there are more than two.
And if the stone is very large, it is unwise to incise it with a large incision, for this causes one of two things to the patient: either death or continuous urinary incontinence, because the area is not completely healed.
Rather, facilitate its expulsion until it is removed, or work on breaking it up with forceps until you extract it in pieces.
Then, when you have completed your operation, fill the wound with aloe and dragon's blood powder, and bind it tightly. And place over that a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or in rose oil and cold water, so that a hot abscess may be cooled.
Then lay the patient supine on their back, and do not unbind the bandage until the third day.
And when you have unbound it, wash the area with water and plenty of oil, and then treat it with diafinicon ointment and diabazicon ointment until it is healed.
But if a hot, burnt, and corrosive abscess occurs in the wound, similar to blood frozen in the bladder, and urine is thereby obstructed (and this is known by the passage of blood with urine), then insert your finger into the wound and extract that blood, for if it remains, it will provoke corruption and putrefaction of the bladder.
Then wash the wound with sharp wine, water, and salt, and apply everything appropriate to its treatment until it is healed.
And it is necessary during the entire treatment that you bind both thighs and adjust the medicines which are placed on the area.
And if the stone is small and is in the middle of the penis, and is lodged there, and urination is obstructed, then treat it with what I have previously recommended before resorting to incision, for often because of this treatment, incision is required; and I have proved this, and it is that you take a smooth iron borer, of which this is the form:
of triangular end, sharp, set in wood.
Then take bile, and bind the penis with it below the stone so that it does not return to the bladder.
Then introduce the iron into the penis, perforating easily until the borer reaches the stone. And turn the borer with your hand on that stone little by little, and you will enlarge the perforation until you make it penetrate from the other side; for the urine will immediately be released.
Then press your hand on what remains of the stone on the outer part of the penis, because that is broken, and is expelled with the urine, and the patient is healed.
But if this treatment is not suitable for you due to some reason that prohibits it, then tie one bandage below the stone, and another bandage above the stone. Then incise over the stone in that penis between the two ligatures. And then extract it. Then untie the bandages and clean the congealed blood which is formed in the wound.
And it is not necessary to bandage below the stone, except to prevent it from returning to the bladder; and the other bandage above is only necessary because when the bandage below is untied, close to the extraction of the stone, the kidney returns to its place and closes the wound.
And for this reason it is necessary for you, when you tie the bandage above, that you elevate the kidney to the upper parts, or that it returns to its place by your action, and close the wound as we have said.
Chapter 61.
On the extraction of a stone from women.
Stones are rarely generated in women.
But if a stone is found in one of them, then its cure is difficult, and is prohibited in many ways.
One reason is that a woman is easily made a virgin again (i.e., her hymen can easily be re-formed or the damage appears to be healed).
And the second is that you will not find a chaste woman, or a married woman, who willingly reveals herself to the physician.
The third is that you will not find a woman who knows this art well, especially the operation with the hand.
And the fourth is that the location of the incision for a stone in women is far from the location of the stone itself, therefore requiring a deep incision, and this is dangerous.
But if necessity compels you to this, then you must find a female physician who is skilled, and such a person is rarely found.
But if you are deprived of that (a female physician), then seek a cautious and skillful male physician, or a matron well-versed in women's matters, or a woman who possesses some knowledge of this art.
Therefore, have that person present, and instruct them to do everything you command, beginning with the examination for the stone; the reason is that if the woman is a virgin, they should insert their finger into her rectum and search for the stone.
Therefore, if they find it,  constrict it below their finger, and then instruct them to incise it.
But if she is not a virgin, then instruct the matron to insert her finger into the patient's vagina and search for the stone, while placing her left hand on the bladder, and compress it with good pressure.
Then if she finds it, she should carefully manipulate it from the opening of the bladder downwards with the sum of her strength until she reaches the root of the thing.
And then incise it next to the midpoint of the vagina below the root of the thing, wherever it is positioned, and center the stone in that area, and her finger should not be removed from the stone, constricting below it.
And the incision should be small at first; and then insert a probe into that incision, and if the stone is felt, then adjust the incision according to the size which indicates that the stone can be extracted from it.
And know that the types of stones are many: for some are small and large, and smooth and rough, and long and round, and having branches.
Then you should know how many types there are so that you can signify what you want.
And if you see a flow of blood, apply crushed vitriol to the place and hold it for an hour until the bleeding is stopped, and then return to your operation until the stone is removed.
And make sure you have prepared the instruments which are mentioned in the extraction of stones in men, so that you are aided by them in your operation.
And if you see a flow of blood, and you know that the expulsion of blood is from the artery that has been incised, then place powder on the area and compress it with powders and a descending bandage, and do not repeat this; and leave the stone and do not extract it, for the patient will quickly die. And then treat the wound.
Then, when the sharpness of the bleeding has subsided after a few days, and the place has healed, then you return to your operation until the stone is removed.
Chapter 62.
Concerning the incision of a strangulated hernia.
A watery hernia is nothing but an accumulation of moisture in the white sheath which is below the genitals containing the testicle, and is called a sheath.
And sometimes it is in the proper tunic which nature prepared for it in the area of the testicle, so that it appears that there is another testicle; and this is the cord of the genital between the white sheath which we have mentioned, and this is nothing but tartar.
And the generation of this hernia is due to weakness which affects the genitals, so that this matter is poured into them, and sometimes it happens due to a blow inflicted on the genitals.
And this moisture has many colors, for its color is either tending towards yellow or bloody, or red, or black and foul, or a watery white, and this is what is most common.
And the signs by which it is known where this accumulation of water is are these: for if it is in the white sheath which we have mentioned, then the abscess is round, slightly elongated like the shape of an egg, and it appears above the testicle, because the moisture surrounds it on all sides.
And if the moisture is in the proper tunic, then the abscess is round near the testicle, and therefore one thinks it is another testicle.
And if the moisture is between the tunic of the genitals and the white sheath, then it falls below the feeling (i.e., it is lower down and less easily felt).
And if you wish to know the color of the moisture, then you test the color of the moisture in the abscess with a probe inserted into it, the form of which precedes; and according to what comes out on the probe, judge what is inside.
Therefore, when we come to its treatment with iron (cautery), then it is necessary that we order the patient to be bled if that is possible for you, and you see his body full (of blood).
And then let him lie supine on his back on something a little raised, and place many cloths underneath him.
And then sit on his left side, and order the assistant to stand on his right side, who will extend his finger to one of the two sides to the sheath of the genitals and to the part of the navel of the abdomen.
And then take a broad spatula and split the sheath of the genitals from the middle downwards lengthwise almost to the perineum.
And let the incision be made straight and equally distant from the line where you would cut the skin of the genitals, until it reaches the white fat included (within the incision).
And remove that (white fat), and observe, and beware that you do not cut it; and your removal of that (fat), which you remove bit by bit, should be suspended with the most delicate and subtle scrutiny with the removal according to the quantity possible for you.
And then perforate the water-filled fat with a wide perforation, and extract all the water.
And then separate the two lips of the incision with hooks, and extend the fat to the upper parts; and do not touch the sheath of the genitals included (within the incision), and incise the fat in the way that is possible for you. And incise it in its totality or in pieces, and especially its thin layer, for if you do not very carefully examine its incision, there is no certainty that the water will not return.
But if the egg (testicle) protrudes from its sheath during your operation, then when you have completed the incision of the fat, return it inside, and then join the two lips of the sheath of the genitals with suture, and then treat it like other wounds until it is healed.
And if you find the testicle corrupted by another disease, then it is necessary that you tie the vessels which are on the spermatic cord, and remove the testicle, and then treat it as we have said.
But if the fluid (hernia) is collected in two separate parts, then know that there are two hernias; therefore incise that part in the same way that you did the other; and if it seems to you that it is one operation, then you perform it.
And then you will wipe away the blood, and insert into the incisions wool soaked in oil or rose oil; and place on top another wool that you have soaked in wine and oil, and spread this over the genitals and the area of the belly; and place over it folded cloths and powders, and bind these on top with a bandage which is according to this form:
And then two cloths should be taken and filled with carded wool in this manner; and the ends should be made with which it is bound with cotton or damp wool, and they should be added with a bandage in the waistband of the breeches, and one of the ends should go from the buttock to the groin, and the second should go to the part of the scrotum, and two ends on the two thighs below and two other ends on the two knees, and the entire bandage should be fastened in the waistband of the breeches.
And sometimes this hernia is treated artificially with cautery instead of incision with an iron; and it is that you take a thin, knife-like cautery and that you incise with it the skin of the genitals, and it is ignited as we have recounted until the white, pus-filled cyst is exposed, and then you take a cautery of this form: and it is similar to the Greek letter.
And then pierce the cyst with it, and ignite it until all the moisture is expelled.
And then stretch the cyst with forceps, and scrape it with forceps, and scrape it with a thin, ignited cautery; and incise it as much as possible until you eradicate all of it; and be careful that the fire does not touch the egg.
And know that this operation is safer and less prone to bleeding, and better than operation with incision.
But if an abscess forms contrary to expectation during incision or cauterization, or there is bleeding, then you must treat whatever opposes you in this, with the treatment that suits it as described.
But if the patient is fearful and does not expect a second operation because of its horror, then administer perforation as I have recommended it; and this is when you are certain, because of what we have said about the signs, that the hernia is watery.
Then you should make the patient sit on some elevated seat, then perforate the abscess at its lowest point with a broad spatula, and place the perforation along the length of the body.
And let the opening be wide enough so that all the water drains out.
Then place a cloth over the area and bind it, and leave it so that the remaining water drains out. And then treat it until the wound is healed.
But if there is resistance to the drainage of the water, at that time you should perforate it; and this is done only because the white pannicle in the opening of the wound creates resistance and prevents the exit of the water. Then you must place in the wound a tool with which water is extracted from dropsies, the form of which is shown previously; or apply a small pressure to the wound opening.
And know that the water will return and accumulate again after about six months or so; and when it has accumulated, then it should be perforated in the same manner, for thus the patient will pass their days.
Chapter 63.
Concerning the incision of a fleshy hernia and its treatment.
