Know that in the incision of this hernia there is a danger leading to death in most cases; therefore, in my view, its removal and being saved by that is better.
And I am recalling the operation in that case and the types of abscesses.
Therefore, I say that abscesses originate in the genitals which are formed for many reasons: either by an acute, acrid superfluity, which is poured into the genitals, or by percussion (a blow or injury).
And the color of the abscess is according to the color of the body, and no pain is found in it, and it is a serious abscess.
And very quickly is a liquefied abscess, of which the color is dark, and there is no feeling in it.
And sometimes it is caused by the knotting of the arteries, and by the inflammation of them as it is perceived in its recollection, or by inflammation of the veins.
But that which is from inflammation of the arteries is known by the dispersion of the abscess when we compress it with the fingers; and it is not fitting that one touch it.
But that which is caused by inflammation of a vein is not dispersed by any cause from compression with the fingers.
And it is possible that an incision be made on this type and on the other  fleshes; and it is so that you incise the skin of the genitals, and then extend the testicle upwards, and extract it from the white sheath, and free the suspensory ligament of the vessels, and tie them, and incise the suspensory ligament after you free it from all parts of the parts of the testicle.
Therefore, if the testicle is already consolidated with those inflamed areas, then it is necessary that you extract the testicle and incise it.
And if there is adhesion between any part of the sheath, or between that which is between the vessels, then it is necessary that you free all that adhesion and incise it with a circular incision.
But if there is a growth of flesh in the place of the adhesion which is behind, then it is necessary that you incise all that which is there, and extract the testicle as we have said.
Then, when your operation is completed, fill the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and wine; and then treat it with other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 64.
On the treatment of a hernia that is of the scrotum.
A scrotal abscess is an inflammation enveloped by a membrane similar to a boil, with softening of the testicles, and it makes movement, exercise, and walking difficult for the patient.
And in the treatment of this illness, fear is to be considered in relation to what preceded it.  (Or: And in treating this illness, the cause that preceded it must be considered.)
However, we must describe the operation as the ancients did.
And I say that you should make the patient sit on a raised seat; then pull the suspensory ligament of the testicles downwards; and then hold the scrotum with your fingers and thumbs, with the vessels that are close to the penis; and let another assistant help you, and extend those [the scrotum and testicles] with forceful extension.
Then incise with a sharp, broad scalpel, with a transverse incision, directly over the vessels, until the vessels are exposed.
Then scrape the entire area as we have described in the extraction of arrowheads that are lodged in the temples.
Then place a double-threaded needle in that area, and tie it first at the place where you made the incision, and tie it again at the other end. Then incise it in the middle with a straight incision along the length of the body, and extract what has accumulated of fetid and corrupted humors.
Then treat the wound with the same treatment as other wounds in which you wish healing to be engendered, so that the vessels which nourish one of the two genitals may not be injured, for that is not good.
But if you injure all the vessels, then it is necessary that the testicles be removed, or that the testicles be tied off along with the vessels, so that they are deprived of all nourishment by the incision of the vessels, and that they be excised, or tied off so that there is no nourishment to them.
Chapter 65.
On the treatment of intestinal hernia.
The occurrence of this hernia is due to a rupture that happens in the peritoneum extended over the abdomen, on the sides of the inguinal rings of the abdominal wall, causing the intestines to protrude through that rupture.
And this rupture is either of the peritoneum or an extension of it, and these two types occur for many reasons:  a blow, a jump, a shout, lifting something heavy, or similar things.
And the sign of this, when it is due to an extension of the peritoneum, is that it occurs gradually over a long time, and not suddenly, and it is a swelling in the groin towards the pubis because the peritoneum compresses the intestine.
And the sign of it, when it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum, is that at the beginning it causes sudden, intense pain; and there is an apparent swelling below the navel, and this is because of the protrusion of the intestine, and its body outside the peritoneum.
And sometimes the omentum is expelled along with the intestine, and this hernia is called an intestinal zirbal hernia.  (Note: "zirbal" appears to be a word specific to this text and its meaning requires further investigation within the context of medieval medical terminology.)
And sometimes it is formed with flatulence, and sometimes a tear occurs in the intestine, and it is retained there, and this is a great danger for the patient, and sometimes it causes severe pain, especially when pressed.
And in the treatment of the types of this illness, iron is feared, therefore its use should be avoided.
And the manner of operating on this is that you command the patient to reduce the intestine with his hand to the inside of its cavity if it obeys its return, and then it should be wrapped around his neck between your hands, and elevate his legs.
And then hold the cord which is the spermatic cord at the upper parts, and incise the spermatic cord completely lengthwise; and then place, in the two lips of the incision, hooks as you need to open the incision with them.
And if, according to the amount that is possible for you, the egg (testicle) is expelled from that incision.
And then scrape away that tunica vaginalis which is beneath the spermatic cord, until the hard white tunica albuginea is uncovered on each side.
Then you introduce your finger into that which is the testicle, into that which is between the tunica albuginea, which is beneath the cord of the testicle, and between the second tunic, and resolve with that the adhesion that is in the part behind the testicle.
And then gently grasp the spermatic cord at the outer parts with your hand, and with this stretch the tunica albuginea upwards with your left hand, and elevate the testicle with the tunica albuginea to the edge of the incision; and command the assistant to elevate the testicle upwards and you dissolve the adhesion which is behind with complete resolution.
And search with your fingers that there is no intestine enveloped in the hard tunica albuginea. But if you find any such thing, then you gently push it back down into the abdomen.
And then incise in the part under the spermatic cord, through the incision of which blood and pus will flow, as we have recounted in what we have said.
And then apply wool soaked in oil and place it on the wound; and apply the ligature which we have recounted (and sometimes the tunica albuginea is cauterized, which we have said near the incision to stop the flow of blood, because this often happens) and leave the ligature until it falls off by itself.
But if its falling off is delayed, then let it be bathed in warm water, and keep it there until it falls off as we have said.
And then treat the wound with the other treatments for wounds until it is healed.
Chapter 66.
On a windy hernia (likely referring to a type of hernia or swelling).
In this hernia, I have not seen anyone who has dared to treat it with iron.
And the first ones said that it is done as we have recounted in the hernia which is with wine; which is that you tie the vessels near the incision on it easily; and then dry the area around the abscess with something that opens it, until the vessels fall away. (This likely describes a surgical approach to a hernia, using wine and cauterization.)
And cure the wound as we have said until it is healed.
Chapter 67.
Of the disruption that is made in the inguinal region (groin).
Sometimes a rupture occurs in the groin as we have said, and it swells the area.
And do not allow any cause of the intestines to descend to the genitals; and if they do, it is a small amount; and it returns in all hours (it returns frequently/constantly).
But if the time is prolonged with this, a fissure is adjusted in the scrotum until the intestine descends into the scrotum; and this happens because of the extension of the scrotum which is in the groin as we have said, the cause of which is that the scrotum is extended, and then it is softened and the place is suppurated and swollen.
And its cure is with cautery, as its description has preceded.
And sometimes it is cured with iron according to this manner: and that is that the patient lies on his back between your hands, and then dry the place of the rupture of the swollen abscesses with incision in width according to the amount of three fingers.
And then tighten the white scrotum that is under the tailbone until it is uncovered (or, until it is revealed).
And then take the ray (likely referring to a surgical instrument) and place that on the prominent place of the scrotum, and press that against the pubis.
And then sew the two prominent places on the extremity of the scrotum's ray (likely referring to a specific part of the scrotum), and join one of them to the other with a suture.
And then extract the extremity of the ray (the instrument), and do not incise the scrotum completely, nor extend the wound, nor anything else like what I will let you know in the treatment of intestinal hernia.
And then treat that with the treatment of wounds, and as you incise the veins, cleanse it and restore the wound until it is consolidated; because the scrotum is contracted and the prominence of the rupture is not adjusted.
And cauterization is better in this place, because it is closer to health (i.e., a quicker, more effective cure).
Chapter 68.
Concerning the softening of the swelling of the testicles.
Many times the swelling of the testicles in some men is softened because the flesh that is between them is softened, and this hinders their recovery.
Then it is necessary for the one who is engaged in his cure to make the patient lie down according to his position, and incise all the swelling which is softened on the natural swelling; and then that he join the two lips with a suture.
And if you wish, sew in the beginning the superfluity of the softened swelling with three or four stitches and with a firm suture.
And then incise between the sutures; and then treat it with the treatment of other wounds until it is healed and the stitches fall out.
Chapter 69.
Concerning castration.
Know that castration is forbidden in our law, but because of that I am obliged to remember it in this book; and its remembrance is necessary only in two ways, one of which is so that it may be within the knowledge of the physician when he is questioned about it, that he may know the treatment of that which has happened; and the other way is that we often have need of the castration of other animals for our benefit from them, such as of horses, and goats, and pigs, and similar animals.
And therefore I say that castration is done in two ways: either by crushing, or by incision.
But in that which is done by crushing, the method of operation is that we immerse the animal in warm water until its genitals are softened and relaxed and hang down. And then grasp them with your hand until they are resolved and no longer appear under the touch.
But in castration done by incision, you must hold the animal and grasp the cord of its testicles with your left hand.
And then tie off the suspensory ligament, and incise over each testicle with one incision until both testicles are exposed.
And then incise them after you have skinned each one. And do not apply anything to those of the cyphus except the fine cyphus which is over the vessels.
And this manner of castration is better which is done by crushing, because strong crushing leaves little cause for the testicles so that the animal desires copulation.
And then care for the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 70.
Concerning the treatment of hermaphroditism.
Hermaphroditism in men is of two kinds: one is when it appears that there is a space in the scrotum, in that which is between two testicles, a shape like a woman's vulva in which there is hair.
And sometimes urine flows from that space in the scrotum.
But in women, there is one type:  it is that the woman's vulva above the mons pubis is like two testicles of a man, somewhat smaller on the outside, like a man's prepuce and two testicles.
And the cure is of three kinds: two for men and one for women. And it is necessary that you incise those constricted tissues until the swelling is released, and then that you treat them with the treatment for other wounds.
And in the second type in men, from which urine flows that is in the scrotum, there is no cure or operation for it.
Chapter 21.
Concerning swelling and prominent flesh due to causes in women.
A slight swelling is often caused by natural reasons until it is pressed and examined; and sometimes it is magnified in some women to such an extent that it is spread like in men and extends to the genitals.
Then you should hold the excess swelling with your hand or with an instrument and incise it.
And do not go too deep in the incision, especially near the root, lest a flow of blood ensue.
Then treat it with the treatment for wounds until it is healed.
But a fleshy growth is flesh that grows in the womb of the mother until it fills it, and quickly protrudes externally resembling a tail (and for this reason some first called it "tailed disease"); therefore, we must incise it as we incise a swelling, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 72.
On the treatment of gout.
Alratica (a likely misspelling or archaic term possibly related to difficult childbirth or a related condition) is when the woman's vagina is not perforated, or the opening is small. And it is either natural or accidental.
Accidental is due to a preceding illness, and this is caused either by improperly formed flesh or by a subtle or thick membrane.
And it is formed either in the front of the mother, or on her sides, or in her upper part, or in her lower part, and it prohibits conception and pregnancy, and it strongly inhibits menstruation.
And the diagnosis of this is made by examining the womb; if the obstruction is apparent, it is evident. But if it is not apparent, then you should search for it with your fingers or with a probe.
But if the obstruction is due to a thin membrane, and is close to the two labia, then you should cut and break it.
And you should place powders on the two labia, and then place your index fingers of both hands together.
And the woman should be on her back, her thighs spread, and then forcibly spread the two labia until that thin membrane is broken and the obstruction is opened.
Then take wool and soak it in oil and place it on the area, and keep it moist throughout the day so that the incised area does not consolidate (heal too quickly).
But if the membrane is thick and dense, then you should incise it with a broad spatula or with a myrtle leaf.
But if the obstruction is due to an abscess, then incise it with an instrument and drain it.
And have with you medicines that stop blood flow without biting (causing further injury), such as acacia, dragon's blood, and enses (possibly referring to a plant or resin), mixed with egg white.
And then administer a wide lead cannula so that the wound does not heal too quickly.
And keep it there for a few days and apply a poultice of dried herbs.
And then it should be treated with another healing method until it is healed.
And sometimes it happens in the birth of other fleshy growths (tumors), and then it is necessary to incise in this manner. But if this is due to a hard abscess, the hard abscess which is in the breast should not be treated with iron in any way.
Chapter 73.
Concerning the treatment of hemorrhoids and swellings and red bumps that occur in the vulva of women.
Hemorrhoids are a swelling of the openings of many veins, from which much blood continuously flows; and when they are old, hemorrhoids become swollen.
And I have already mentioned in the division the types of these and their signs, and I will say thus which ones receive treatment and which ones do not receive treatment.
Therefore I say that hemorrhoids and swellings, when they are in the womb and do not appear outwardly, then in those cases, treatment with iron should not be performed.
But those which are at the opening of the womb, upon which one can feel them, are those that are cured.
And it is necessary that you place the woman in a cold place and then extend the swelling with forceps, or with a rough cloth, and incise them at their roots.
And then sprinkle on them, to stop the flow of blood, one of the incisive powders without biting, such as acacia and similar things.
And then bathe her feet for an hour, and then place her in cold water.
And if the flow of blood persists, then make her sit in a decoction of pomegranate leaves and bark, and of walnuts, and similar things.
And then take barley flour and mix it with honey and sour wine, and make a plaster with this and apply it to her back.
And if the blood is stopped, it is good; and if not, apply two cupping glasses, to the breasts without scarification.
And then take wool and soak it in coriander juice, or in comfrey juice, or in rue juice with red wine, and apply it to the area; and then treat the area with ointments until it is healed.
And treat that until it is healed.
Chapter 74.
Of the perforation of a fistula occurring in the mother.
Many kinds of abscesses occur in the mother, such as cancer, and stone abscesses, and ulcerative, and swollen, and herpetic, and fistulas, and hemorrhoids, and fissures, and boils, and other abscesses. And I have already discussed these diseases and their types and their signs, and their cures, in the division.
And it is necessary that I speak in this treatise of a hot abscess which comes to the mother, when it is among the abscesses which gather pus; and if you wish to know in what manner its operation is done with iron.
Then you must consider if the pain of the abscess at its beginning is acute with percussion and phlegm and fever, and sometimes it reddens and if feeling falls upon it; then do not treat it with perforation, but cure it with that which aids its maturation until the acuteness of the symptoms ceases.
And then it is necessary that you make the woman sit on a stool that has two legs; and she reclines on her back and adjusts her feet to the part below her belly, and her thighs are spread, and she places her arms below her knees, and tie each with a suitable bandage.
And then the midwife follows from the right side and administers the instrument with which the orifice of the womb is opened, the form of which will come in those things that are done next.
And it is necessary, when you wish to administer this instrument, that you place with the ray the neck of the woman's womb, and that which of the instrument is inserted into the womb should not be more than the neck of the womb and it hurts the patient. But if the instrument is larger than the neck of the womb, then it is necessary that you place powders on the texture of the neck of the womb so that the entry of any instrument into the neck of the womb is prohibited.
And it is necessary that you place the turning device that runs in the instrument from the upper side and that the administrator holds the instrument and rotates the turning device until the orifice of the womb is opened.
And when the wound appears, and it is touched with the hand and is soft and moist, then it is necessary that you dry that area with a linen cloth.
And when the pus is evacuated, then it is necessary that you place in the wound a wick soaked in rose oil or green oil in which there is some astringency; and place the calca7 [presumably a type of compress or dressing] on the section in the neck of the womb.
And place outside the womb and on the vulva clean wool soaked in water in which mallows have been boiled.
And afterwards it should be cured for about two days with an ointment which is suitable for this until it is healed.
And sometimes the womb and the wound are washed so that it is cleansed with water and honey, and afterwards with water in which the root of lilies or of birthwort has been boiled, and afterwards it returns to the ointment.
And if the wound is deep within the hidden womb, then you must be forbidden from treating it with iron,  taking care of it with what we have said in the section [presumably a previous section of the treatise].
Chapter 75.
To teach midwives in what manner live embryos should be treated when they emerge in an unnatural way.
It is necessary that the midwife know at the beginning the form of natural childbirth by its signs; and it is that when you see the woman strain in the lower parts and desire to smell the air, and moreover is relieved by what is above her of the birth, and hastens the emergence of the embryo, then you know that this birth is according to natural form, and it is the emergence of the child headfirst, and the afterbirth with it, either suspended or hanging from the umbilical cord.
Therefore, when you see these signs, then it is necessary that her belly be compressed so that the embryo descends easily, because when it descends headfirst, the afterbirth descends with it, and is cleansed of those superfluities with good cleansing.
But that which is other than this birth is unnatural and unfavorable, for when the embryo is emerged feet first and its body is emerged before its head and feet, and one of its hands, or its foot, or its head and hand, or its belly, and quickly is turned about its neck, resembling those unfavorable presentations.
Therefore it is necessary for the midwife to be wise and skillful in all these matters, and to guard herself against error and deception.
And I am showing all the forms of these, and what skill is needed in them so that it may be signified by this and be established upon this.
When the fetus is emerged headfirst by natural expulsion, and this is vehement for the woman and is difficult for her, and sometimes her strength is already weakened, then have her sit, or have the women support her, and evaporate her vapor with cooked fenugreek and moist oils.
Then the midwife inserts a small, flat spatula between her labia and with it she splits the secundines, or she scrapes it with her fingernail until it removes the moisture within it. And press the woman's belly until the fetus descends.
But if it does not descend, then the woman should be poulticed with mallow mucilage and fenugreek mucilage with saffron.
Then order the person, during the poulticing, to be compressed and make her sneeze with sneezing powders; and hold her mouth and nose for an hour, for the fetus will be expelled immediately.
And if both hands of the fetus are expelled at the beginning, then you should reduce them carefully, little by little. But if it does not obey the inward movement, then place the woman on a chair and raise her legs, and then jolt the chair on the ground; and the woman should be held so that she does not fall from the jolt.
And if the hands are not returned inwards and the fetus dies, then incise them and extract the remainder of the fetus, or tie its body to its hand with a cloth and extract it gently, for it will then be expelled.
When the fetus is presented feet first, then you should push them upwards, and then gently turn the fetus, and then grasp it, and then take one foot and gently extend it.
But when it has emerged between the thighs, then you should order her to strain, and make her sneeze forcefully, for it will be expelled if God wills it.
And if it is not expelled by what we have recommended, then you should return the fetus little by little until you place it in its natural position, for it will then be expelled with ease.
And if it is obstructed despite everything we have recommended, then take mallow mucilage and dissolved gum, and pound everything well in a mortar, and then apply this to the woman's vulva and the lowest part of her abdomen. And then make her bathe in lukewarm water, up to the hypochondria (the regions below the ribs).
And when you see that the lower parts are softened, then give her a fumigation of myrtle and apply it to her. And when she has had the fumigation for an hour, then make her sit on a chair, and then make her sneeze and hold her mouth, and lift her up and press the lower part of her abdomen with gentle compression, for the fetus will be expelled immediately.
Thus of the fetus laterally, holding one of its hands. Let it be managed by reducing her hand; but if you cannot reduce it, then raise the woman and make her walk.
And if she cannot walk, then make her lie on the bed, and shake her thighs with vehement shaking, and then administer emollient remedies which I have recommended made of mucilages.
And if childbirth has arrived, then make her sit on a stool, and then work on the reduction of the hand, and grasp the fetus according to its natural form, and make the woman sneeze and command her to strain until it is expelled.
Thus of the fetus upon the neck, its hands spread out and its face is towards its mother. It is necessary that the midwife hold the hand of the fetus and then turn it little by little, and that this happens with ease.
And when it is grasped, then place on the neck of it that which we have recommended of oil and mucilages.
And then command her to cough little by little, and when you have done this, then make her sit on a stool and command her to be inclined on her right side, and then command her to bear down; and make her sneeze with a potion. The prepared fetus exits on its side.
When the horror of the mother ceases according to this manner, consider, and then you incise the secundine with your fingernail until it is emptied of its moisture.
And if the fetus is suspended over its face and neck, then you incise it at the umbilicus, so that it is not retained and dies.
And then push the fetus inwards until you place it as it should be, and tell the woman to bear down a few times, and it will be expelled easily in this way, if God wills it.
The exit of twins or multiple fetuses. Know that twins are often born, and sometimes three or four, but this is rare and seldom happens; but these are causes of nature, and they do not live.
And count those, even if they are multiple, as one afterbirth, and they are separated by a partition, distinguishing them through the ligament in the umbilicus of each one. And sometimes they are expelled as if it were a single fetus by easy natural delivery, and sometimes they are expelled by unnatural delivery, as we have recounted.
And if some of their births are difficult and lead to what we have said about unnatural presentations, then you should treat them as we have recounted.
And if the midwife is skillful and does this with ease and dexterity, and with patience, and is ingenious according to every presentation as it is prepared for her by the intellect leading to health.
Concerning the recollection of that which is formed in the womb from embryos and is aborted.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten are formed in the mother, and it has been verified by me that one woman aborted and expelled six, and another ten, all formed, which was determined by the measurement of the glorious wise men (or learned physicians).
And all this must be within the knowledge of the midwife because it quickly appears similar to that, and therefore she must oppose it with what is necessary.
Chapter 76.
Concerning the extraction of the fetus when it is dead.
When you treat a dead fetus using what we have said in the division of medication and it has not been expelled, and you are forced to use a manual operation, then you must consider if the woman is of sound constitution and is not suffering from any of the conditions that endanger her life.
Then the woman should be positioned on the bed on her side, with her head inclined downward and her thighs elevated.
And the assistant should hold her from both sides, or she should be tied to the bed so that her body does not extend during the extraction of the fetus.
And then you moisten the mother's vulva with warm oils and a mucilage of mallow, fenugreek, and linseed.
And then the midwife anoints her hand with those oils and mucilages, and gently inserts her hand into the mother, and then searches with her hand for the location where the umbilical cord is attached to the fetus.
And convenient places for this: then you consider if the fetus is positioned to descend headfirst, and then you grasp the umbilical cord at its eyes, or neck, or mouth, or palate, or under the chin, or at its clavicle, or in the areas near the ribs, or under the hypochondria.
But if the descent is feet first, then at the perineum, or in the middle of the back.
And it is necessary to hold the umbilical cord with the right hand, and place its curve in the space between the fingers of the left hand, and gently insert the hand and place the cord in one of those places which we have mentioned, until the empty space is reached.
And then place and put in opposition to it another cord, or a third if necessary, so that the traction is equal, and there is not extension only along its straightness.
Rather, it should be pulled evenly on all sides, so that its extraction is eased, just as is done in the case of tooth extraction.
And it is necessary in this matter that the tension be softened; and if some part of it is retained, then it is necessary that the midwife lubricate some of her fingers with oil and insert them skillfully and loosen what is retained with them.
Therefore, if the part of the fetus is expelled, then the cords should be moved to a higher location; and do this until the whole fetus is expelled.
But if the hand is delivered before the other, and it is not possible to reduce it because of its constriction, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it; and then pull it out until it is all expelled, and incise it either at the joint of the hand or at the elbow.
And thus you should do with the other hand and with both feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or constriction occurs during its expulsion, or there is accumulated fluid in its head, then it is necessary to insert a hooked, pointed instrument between the fingers and split the head and extract the water, or to break it with an instrument which is called an "alundat", the form of which will come with the forms of other instruments in the chapter which is next to this one.
And similarly proceed if the fetus has a naturally large head: and then it is necessary to incise it with a scalpel, or to grasp it with the alundat as we have said; and then extract the bones with forceps.
And if the head is expelled but constricted below the clavicle, then it should be split so that the moisture which is in the feet is drained.
But if it is not complicated, nor does it obey expulsion, then you should incise the head immediately as much as is possible for you.
And if the lower abdomen is abscessed or dropsical, then it is necessary that you perforate it so that the moisture within it runs out.
But if the fetus is on its feet, then its extraction is easy and its direction to the mother's mouth is easy.
But if it is constricted around the abdomen and feet, then you must extract it with a cloth which you will have wrapped around your hand, and you should incise the abdomen and feet until what is within them flows out.
But if the other limbs are removed and the head is turned, then the midwife inserts her left hand; but if the mother's mouth (vagina) is open, then she inserts her hand into the uterine cervix, and with it seeks the head, and draws it with her fingers into the mother's mouth (vagina), and then inserts into it an ounce, or two ounces, and draws it out with them.
And if the mother's mouth (vagina) is constricted by a hot abscess which affects her, then it should not be disturbed; rather, it is necessary to administer infusions of warm, oily substances beforehand. And apply plasters; and bathe the patient in waters that soften and moisten.
And if the fetus is on its side, then, if possible, it should be turned; and what we have said should be administered as for a live fetus.
And if this is not possible, then let it be incised in pieces, and extracted.
And it is necessary that no part of the afterbirth remains inside.
And when you have completed all your work, then administer what we have said concerning the treatment of the hot abscesses which might befall the mother.
And if bleeding occurs, then bathe in styptic waters and administer the other treatment.
And I have seen a woman in whose womb was a dead fetus, and soon after that, she became pregnant again, and soon after, the fetus died again. A long time ago, an abscess occurred in his navel; and it swelled until it was opened, and it began to discharge pus.
And I was called to his care, and I treated him for a long time, and the wound did not heal.
Then I applied plasters of strong attraction to him, and I extracted a bone from him, and after a few days, I extracted another bone; and I was very surprised, since there should not be bones in the belly.
And then I thought that they were bones from the dead fetus.
Then I cleansed the wound, and extracted many bones from him, the woman being in such a way disposed, and she lived in this manner for a long time, with a slight discharge from him.
And I did not consider this to be strange, but because in this there is skill and help, that the skilled physician administers with his hand for a cure.
Of the forms of the instruments that are necessary to extract the fetus.
Form of incision with which the mother is opened:
This is the form of a constrictor in which the [membranes/tissues] are grasped, which constriction has two incisions at the two ends of two poles/rods. But this incision should be more subtle than a constrictor, and be made of ivory or boxwood.
And let the width of each rod be about two fingers, and the thickness of each about one finger, and the length of each is a palm and a half; and in the middle space of the two rods are two fittings of the same wood which are already fixed, of which the length is half a palm and a little more, and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions/fittings are inserted into the mouth of the mother so that it is opened with them when you rotate the two aforementioned incisions/rods.
Another form similar to that one, but more subtle and lighter:
Let it be made of ivory or boxwood, in the likeness of forceps. However, at its extremity are two fittings like those you see, and the length of these fittings is about a palm, and the width of each is two fingers.
And when you wish to open the mother (i.e., the woman's birth canal) with them, then you make the woman lie on the bed with her legs hanging down, having opened what is between her thighs. Then open your hand with the instrument, just as you do with shears, gradually, according to how much opening of the mother's birth canal you desire, until the midwife does what you want.
The form of vertigo which the ancients described:
The shape of the instrument with which the fetus is expelled:
The form of the instrument with which the large head is held back.  (The "2" likely indicates a superscript or annotation not relevant to the core meaning).
And it resembles forceps which have teeth at the end, like this, and sometimes it is made long like forceps according to this form, like this, which has teeth like saw teeth, with which it is incised and held back. Form of a small instrument;  or,  The form of a small tool.
The form of a hook having a spine (or point).
The form of a hook having two spines (or points).
The form of a spatula having two ends with which the head is severed (or cut).
The form of two broad spatulas.
All those instruments are exercised, and when the types of these are more multiplied and prepared by the artisans, the operation is easier and more valuable to men because of their quantity.
And do not neglect any of those instruments which are prepared by you, for the necessity of those is not excusable.
Of the extraction of the afterbirth.
When the afterbirth is retained after childbirth, then you should command the patient to hold her breath, and then make her sneeze forcefully, and press your hand over her mouth and nostrils, and if by this it comes out, it is good.
And if not, then you take a pot and pierce a hole in the lid and put herbs in it, opening the mouth of the mother, such as rue, and mint, and chamomile, and dried Arabic gum, and cassia bark, and holy thistle. All these herbs, or some of them, should be submerged in water in the pot, and place that over the fire.
And then place a reed cannula in the mouth of the mother, and hold it there until the vapor reaches the mother's womb.
And then make her sneeze as we have said, and the afterbirth will be expelled quickly.
But if this treatment remains ineffective and it is not expelled, then you instruct the midwife to submerge her left hand in sesame oil or marshmallow mucilage, and then insert it into the vagina and search for the afterbirth with it; and when she has found it, then take it and extract it little by little. But if it is attached to the womb of the mother, then insert her hand as we have recommended, and when she has found the afterbirth, extract it little by little as instructed, so that the mother does not collapse from violent traction. Furthermore, it should be easily turned to the right and left sides.
And if the mouth of the mother (the cervix) is constricted, we have already recommended treatment with sneezing and medication of the oil with herbs.
But if it is not expelled by all the means we have recommended, then be careful not to tire yourself with repeated attempts at extraction; however, what has come out of her to the mouth of the womb should be left at the woman's vulva, and then cauterize it with ointment and tetrafarmaco; for that will cause it to rot after a few days, and it will dissolve and be expelled.
But when it putrefies, a foul odor rises to her head and stomach, and this harms the patient; and then fumigations suitable for this should be administered.
But some of the Ancients already tested fumigation with hellebore and dried figs.
The method of using the instrument with which a woman is fumigated for the retention of menses and afterbirth:
Let it be made of glass or brass.
And let the thin end be placed in the vagina and the wide end over the fire, and the fumigant placed on the embers of the fire.
And hold it there until that fumigant is consumed and repeat with another.
Concerning the treatment of an imperforate anus in a child.
Many children are born and their anuses are not perforated, which are very narrow and obstructed. Then it is necessary for the midwife to perforate that narrow passage with her finger; and if not, perforate it with a sharp instrument, and be careful not to touch the muscle.
And then place on it wool soaked in wine and oil; and then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if you fear that it is obstructed, then place a cannula in the opening for many days, and remove it when the child wishes to defecate.
And sometimes anal obstruction occurs due to the consolidation of a wound or abscess; then it is necessary that this consolidation be incised, and then it should be treated with what we have said about the treatment and the lead [likely referring to lead-based ointments].
Of the treatment of fistulas that develop below. (Or: Concerning the treatment of fistulas which appear in the lower area/region).
The fistulas that develop in the lowest part below are knotty and thick, which develop near the anus externally or in the space of one part. (Or: Fistulas that appear in the lowest, posterior region are nodular and thickened, appearing near the anus externally, or in the interspace of one area.)
And it is one fistula or several.
And when that nodule is old and discharges watery white moisture or subtle pus, and sometimes some of these fistulas are penetrating.
And those penetrating ones are known because something exits from them through discharge and by flatulence; and sometimes the patient suffers from discharge, and worms easily exit from them.
And some of these fistulas sometimes penetrate into the space [between things], into the bladder, and into the meatus of the penis; and some penetrate into the joint of the hip and into the nut of the buttock.  (The phrasing is somewhat awkward.  A more natural English rendering might be:  "And sometimes these fistulas penetrate into the bladder and the urethra; and some penetrate the hip joint and the buttock.")
And what distinguishes a fistula penetrating the anus from one that does not penetrate is that you insert your finger into the anus, and then insert a thin probe of copper or iron into the fistula when there is no tortuosity.
But if there is tortuosity in it, then insert a thin probe of lead or horsehair into it until you feel the probe or hair on your finger.
And if you do not feel it at all, nor does anything proceed from the opening, such as discharge, worms, or flatulence, as we have said, then know that it is non-penetrating, and then attend to its cure.
And when the fistula penetrates into the bladder or into the meatus of the penis, then its indication is the urine from that [source] and its prohibition [of the use of that urine], so that the place may be consolidated with medicine.
But if it penetrates into the joint of the thigh or into the bone, and causes pain in the surrounding area, then its sign is that the probe penetrates there if there is no tortuosity in the bone, and the appearance of pain in the surrounding area; and that there is constant putrefaction from it; and that no medication is effective, nor is it consolidated with ointment.
And in all those penetrating fistulas, there is no complete cure, and the cure of those is the work and vanity of foolish physicians, upon which they are fixed.
But in that which is not penetrating and not old, healing with iron is hoped for, according to what I shall recount to you, and this I have already proven.
And it is that the patient lies between your hands on his back, and raises his legs upwards, his thighs being inclined towards his belly, and then introduce a lead or bronze probe, if there is tortuosity in the fistula, until you know the place where the probe penetrates.
And if the patient feels pain around the anus, then you should insert your finger into the anus.
And if you feel with your finger the probe already discovered by itself penetrating, besides what you feel between your finger and that abscess or flesh, then you know certainly that it is penetrating; and then do not work on that, for there is no healing in that, as we have said.
However, some say that it heals slowly in some men.
And of the medication by which improvement is hoped for in him is that you ignite a slender cautery according to the width of the fistula, as has been previously described, and insert that ignited cautery into the fistula until it reaches around the anus; and then repeat this two or three times until you know that all those fistulated, fleshy parts resembling a bird's quill are cauterized.
And then treat that with poultices wrapped in linen until all those cauterized flesh sloughs away, and then treat it with flesh-generating and consolidating medicines, and if it is healed, good; and if not, then it will not be healed at any time by any other cure.
And if the probe enters, and does not penetrate beyond your finger which is in the anus, and between that and the probe there is a palpable thickness of flesh or abscess, and you see the fistula in that which is the surface of the skin, then dry the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you will dry it with the probe, and let it be in the fistula until the incision reaches the place where the tip of the probe arrives, and by the probe it is released and exits.
And then cleanse those fistulated tissues which resemble a bird's quill, and let no part of them remain.
But if a flow of blood happens to come between you and your operation when you are excising those tissues, then the best and most gentle instrument is to cauterize with fire or with a sharp medicine, because cauterization with fire accomplishes two good things: that is, cauterization of those affected tissues and the staunching of blood and drying of the humors.
And then treat it with poultices wrapped in linen or crushed flaxseed until the place is free of pus, and then treat the wound with ointments that generate and consolidate firm flesh.
And these are medicines in which there is stypticity and drying; when then it is restored it is healed and we do not fear recurrence.
But if the fistula penetrates to the rectum and is elongated from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the rectum and explore it with that.
And so if you feel the fistula and between you and it there is a covering of fat or flesh, and it is close to penetration, then there is no operation there except by way of trust and hope.
Which requires one of three methods: either cauterize it as we have said; or dry it until you reach its cavity so that you can insert the poultices and the cure is close; and do not proceed much with incision so that you do not cut the muscle containing the rectum, and that does not happen to the patient thus from the excision without his will; and then treat it with what we have said because it will heal very quickly; or elongate that covering and clean the pus after drying it near the rectum, as we have said, either with a probe or with another instrument of sharp point, and then cleanse those fistulous tissues which are in the incision where you can with cauterization; and then strive to achieve the restoration of the whole wound with incision, and if the orifice above is not consolidated, and the fistula remains open in the part of the rectum, it will be easier for the patient. And sometimes the fistula ends in this way: when you insert the probe into the fistula, and it is in the side of the rectum around the surface of the body with the tail and in the margin of the rectum, then take a probe with a perforated end, which is like a needle for preparing skins in this way:
and insert it into that thread wrapped in five threads or around those; and then insert a probe with thread into the fistula until you reach its cavity.
But if it is penetrating into the margin of the rectum inside, then you pull the thread from that hole so that you insert your finger into the rectum; and pull out the end of the thread and join the two ends together, and tighten each, and leave it for a day or two, and when you incise the thread in the flesh, tighten it well until those tissues are incised which are between the two ends of the thread and fall away.
And then treat the wound until it is consolidated and healed.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate, in whatever way is prepared for you, except that it is very close to the perineum, because it is not for you to do this so that you do not incise the muscle; and then do this until it is healed.
The shape of the pointed probe with which the fistula is opened: its curvature is very sharp and the other part is not sharp, so that you do not incise what is not necessary, as sometimes happens.
Concerning the suturing of hemorrhoids from which blood flows during their incision.
Hemorrhoids are of two types, for either they are between the buttocks similar to charcoal, or they are like grape husks. And of these there are small and large ones, and blood flows from them constantly and it is one, and there are many, and they are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those that are outside the anus are according to the months of little humidity, from which flows a citrine water or a little blood with continuous discharge, and are according to the complexion.
And the treatment of that which is inside the anus is that you order the patient to sit and strain until the anus protrudes and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you should grasp and suspend them with hooks or hold them with your fingernail, and then incise them at their roots.
But if the hooks cannot hold them because of their humidity and softness, then grasp them with rough cloth or cotton or a pledget of wool, and extract them with your fingers, and then incise them and sprinkle on them some hot powders near the incision, so that they act on them in place of cautery; or cauterize them as is described in the chapter on cauterization. And then treat them with the other treatment until they are healed.
And if the anus does not obey expulsion, then stimulate the patient with a mild stimulant, and let it be washed with that which is in the anus and which will easily obey expulsion when the patient strains.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then their cause is slight, and you should grasp them with your fingernail, or grasp them with a hook, and incise them, and then treat them with what we have said until they are healed.
And that which you incise, you must suture thus: take waxed thread and insert it into a needle and then pass the hemorrhoids to the upper parts, and make it penetrate with the needle into the root of the other part, and wrap the two ends of the thread in the lowest part of the needle, and it should be laterally; and tighten the hemorrhoid with a strong suture, and then knot the thread and remove the needle.
And do this to all, and leave one of them unstitched, so that the superfluity of blood may flow from it.
And then place on the anus a cloth soaked in rose oil or cerate; and place that on the anus and command the patient to rest. And then leave it until it falls off; and when it has fallen off, treat it with ointment or with another cure until it is healed.
And since they are old and that medicine is not effective, then you must scrape them with the point of a spatula or with your fingernail until they are moistened and the crust above is removed, which prevents their consolidation; and then treat them until they are consolidated.
Then remove them with a more vigorous scraping than the first, until they bleed much and are excoriated; and then treat them until they are healed.
Know that alamermar, that is, a nail (ingrown toenail), is nothing but a round knot, according to the color of the body, similar to the head of a clove, and it is found throughout the body and especially in the parts below the feet and in the fingers, and because of these, pain arises from the inflammation.
And then it is necessary that what is in the circumference of the nail be incised, and held with forceps or with an instrument, and eradicated from its root; and then treat that.
And if you wish, cauterize it according to what is described in its chapter.
Moreover, a wart is a small, rough, fistulated, round eminence on the surface of the body, and many times it appears on the hands of children.
And its cure is that you stretch and incise it, or that you bind it tightly with thread or with a skin so that it falls off. And if you wish, cauterize that, as we have said, with fire or with a sharp medicine.
Moreover, a mole is a small, hairy or fistulated, rough eminence on the surface of the body, growing often in the pubic area, and when friction encounters it, it is felt like an ant bite, and it is found all over the body.
And its cure is that you take a cannula of goose quill or vulture quill, and let it be strong, and place that over the mole so that the circle of the cannula contains the parts of it; and then rotate your hand with the cannula so that you incise that which is in the circumference of the mole, and place that in the depth of the flesh, and so eradicate the mole from its root.
And if you wish, do this with a brass or iron cannula, according to this method:
Let the upper part of the cannula be drawn out to a thinness, blunt (that is, not perforated), wrapped so that on the said part the apprehension of it may be avoided and its rotation [easier].
And if you wish, cauterize the mole as was previously mentioned in the cauterization of a boil in the chapter on cautery.
Concerning the form of the instruments that are used to administer a clyster.
Sometimes a clyster is made of silver, or tin, or cast or hammered brass; and sometimes it is made of these instruments, small and large, according to those to whom it is administered, for that which is administered in the treatment of small children should be small; and for those whose buttocks are fragile or painful, the clysters for them should be small.
The form of a large and a small clyster:
The head of its cannula, to which the bag is tied, should be wide in this manner; and let there be a distinction where the bag is tied, as sometimes happens.
And the lower extremity, which is inserted into the anus, should be blunt (that is, not open) but smooth, tapering to a point; and on one side of it let there be two holes, and on the other side one hole, and let the width of the hole be according to the thickness of the rays and a little larger.
And let the bag, in which the medicines are placed, be of animal bladder or sheepskin, and let its length be one palm.
And let it be perforated with many holes, as it is rolled up; and let the space which is between the holes be the thickness of a finger.
Then insert into those holes strong, raw flax thread, about ten threads, or thereabouts; and fasten the bag with it; and when the bag is placed in him, bind it tightly at the head of the clyster with two distinct ligatures, and then cauterize each hole:  The manner of cauterizing it is that you fill the bag with medicine which is warmed according to the patient's tolerance; and take care that it is neither too hot nor too cold.
Then let the patient lie supine on his back, and raise his legs upwards and place under him a cushion upon which are placed cloths or a sheet; and then anoint the end of the clyster with oil or egg white or fenugreek mucilage or something similar; and then insert it into the anus easily, and then the minister takes the bag and squeezes it with both hands and forcefully until all that is in the clyster is evacuated into the bowel; and then remove the clyster and the patient lowers his legs.
And if the medicine forces him to do so, let him retain it as long as he can; and if he sleeps with it all night, it is more beneficial.
But if you administer this clyster in cases of diarrhea and the evacuation bothers him, then you repeat the clyster until he is healed.
And if there are hemorrhoids or painful abscesses in the anus, then let the clyster be very fine and soft, like those with which children are given clysters.
Of the cure of plagues.
I have already said in the division of the cure of plagues what was appropriate to the book, and I say thus the cure of those with hand and with medicines according to the fulfillment/completion.  (The "fulfillment" here likely refers to the needs of the specific case).
Therefore I say that plagues are diversified according to what the plagues are made of and according to the place upon which the plagues fall.
And the causes for which plagues are often made are thus:  a stone throw, or incision by sword or knife, or a blow with a lance, or with a stick, or with an arrow, or similar to those from many causes.
But the plagues that are in the places of the body are thus: a plague which falls upon the head, or the neck, or the feet, or the belly, or the thigh, and similar to those of the limbs.
And I am recounting the cure of certain plagues so that you may apply that reasoning and rule to other plagues.
And I will begin with the simple plagues of the head, for the remembrance of the compound ones will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore I say that when a simple wound occurs in the head and there is no fracture, then consider: if it is from a stone throw or the like, and it has only broken the skin and is a large wound, and you fear the occurrence of a hot abscess in that patient, then you should immediately bleed him, and not delay this; and extract blood from him according to his strength, unless some cause prohibits bloodletting, especially if the blood that has come from the wound is little.
And let the bloodletting be on the opposite side from the wounded part; and guard the patient from repletion and from wine and heavy foods.
But if some cause prohibits bloodletting, then let his diet be lessened, and let him not approach meat nor wine.
And place on the wound, if a hot abscess occurs in him, a pledget soaked in rose oil alone or in wine in which something styptic is dissolved.
But if it is certain that a hot abscess will form, then place on the wound, if it is recent, its own blood, and air has not changed that little powder which I shall soon describe.
And if the air has already changed that [wound], then apply some ointment until it is healed.
But if the wound is large or is from a sword cut or the like, and its lips are not joined together by themselves, then join them with sutures according to the method of suturing I shall describe.
But if the wound has already exposed the bone and hangs, and there is not a small suspensory retention of it, then incise it and then treat the wound with medicines and restore the flesh in place of the bone, and order the patient to be fed foods that are thick: heads and cooked foods and similar, until it is healed.
And if you find putrefaction in the bone and it does not adhere to the bone, then you incise all that which is rotten and remove it, and then treat it.
But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which a flow of blood might occur, and it is not stopped by medicines, then you search for the artery, and if you find it uncut, then you cut it with a spatula, or tie it; and if necessity compels you that what we have said is not effective, then you cauterize until the bleeding is stopped.
And if these wounds are simple and small, then their cause is light; so that you sprinkle this powder on them, for it will consolidate them before the air alters them.
And the manner of the powder is this: take olive oil one part, dragon's blood two parts, unslaked lime three parts; let all be well crushed and sifted, and with it let the wound be filled, and let it be bound with a good bandage until it adheres well to it, and is coagulated with the blood.
And then bind it above with powders and leave it, and do not unbind it while it is thus, until it is not festering, for the medicine is not eradicated from it until it is healed.
And sometimes it does this alone with lime, when you do not have olive oil and dragon's blood at hand.
And do this same operation on large wounds when it is necessary that it be placed on them and they are fresh, and they are recent wounds with their blood, which the air has not altered.
And if the air has altered them, and especially when it is in summer time, then this powder is not indicated, but then treat those with what we have said, so that you place on them some digestive ointments, or that you place on them powders of barley flour with water and honey until they discharge pus; and then treat them with other treatment until they are healed.
But if a fracture in the bone occurs with the wound, and it is small, then you extract it with forceps.
And I will describe the treatment of head fractures in what follows.
Of the wounds of the neck.
But if the wound occurs in the neck, then there is no difference in medication between it and a wound of the head when it is simple.
But if the nerves of the neck or an artery are incised, then if it is a nerve, there is no other remedy than to avoid treating it with something that causes strong astringency, such as vitriol and similar substances, because they are harmful to the nerves and contract them. And do not apply anything cold to the wound, because the substance of the nerves is cold, and their continuation is with the brain, which is the highest of all the members.
And if it is cured with light medicines, such as washing the wound with fresh water, sometimes infused with rose oil or plain oil, and applying a poultice after washing with fresh water and other softening remedies in this manner, and moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, then administer sutures, or approximate its lips, and then treat it with powders until it is healed.
But if the wound is deep, or there is pus at its lowest part, then perforate it at the lowest point.
But if an artery is incised in the wound and there is a consequent flow of blood, then ligate or cauterize it, or tie it off if necessity demands it.
But if the vertebrae of the neck or the whole neck has been incised, and the veins of the neck have been saved, then you should approximate the edges of the wound with sutures over a neck splint and do not touch it; first equalize it and restore it to its natural shape, then tighten it with a decent bandage, and leave it for a few days; and then treat it with ointments which have styptic and healing properties until it is healed.
But if the wound has caused damage to the bone, then clean it, and if there are bone fragments, extract all those that you can; and what you cannot reach, leave it until the wound suppurates, and is separated and the injury is relieved.
And if the wound is recent, then sprinkle on it the powder which has been recommended to you. But if a fistula has formed in the wound, then treat it with that which will be mentioned in its chapter.
If it is a wound from a lance or knife, and you see its depth, then consider further if there is a rush of air from the wound when the patient breathes; then know that the wound is mortal.
And if it is not deep and the wound is fresh with its blood, then you should put powder on it in the first hour, and do not bind it tightly so that the blood is not retained in its depth and returns to the heart and kills the patient, but put on an attractive ointment, and unbind it twice a day.
And if the ointment is not at hand, then place old linen on the opening of the wound so that it draws out the moisture; and have the patient sleep on the wound so that what is in it flows out.
But if three days or more have passed since the wound and it has not caused the patient spasms, tremors of the heart, or constriction of the throat, and you see his other good dispositions, then know that the wound is safe; and then treat it with poultices and other treatments until it is healed.
But if its healing is delayed, and it remains open for a long time, then know that a fistula has formed; therefore, treat that according to its chapter.
And if the wound is from an incision by a sword or knife, and is simple on the surface of the chest or back, then treat it with what is prescribed for sutures, if it is large, or with powder, if it is small.
But if it has caused damage to the bone, and pieces have been cut away from it, then you should clean the wound and attempt to extract those pieces if they are separated. But if they are not separated, then leave them until the wound suppurates, so that their extraction is easier.
And for other accidental wounds in the other limbs, the method of treatment is the same as what we have said.
Of the wounds of the abdomen.
The indisposition which occurs in the abdomen is either great, small, or moderate.
And a small rupture is made difficult in another way, and it is such that their reduction is more difficult, if you do not take care of their replacement immediately, because it opens and swells, making their insertion difficult.
Therefore, the best [type of] rupture is moderate, because in that case the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is in the two previously mentioned types.
And know that the suturing of the abdomen is done in four ways, but two sutures are common and are suitable for suturing other wounds of the body.
And two other sutures are specific to [the abdomen], but however, they are more specific to the suturing of the abdomen.
And I will recount to you each one with explanation and clarification.
Therefore, I say that when the wound is small and some part of the intestine has come out through it, and it is difficult to reduce it, then this is in one of two ways: either because of the small size of the rupture as we have said, or when the intestine has become inflamed due to the coldness of the air.
And therefore, when this is so, then it is necessary that you warm it in such a way that you submerge a sponge or cloth moistened in lukewarm water alone, or that you boil it in water with squill, cyperus, and spikenard, and bathe the intestine with it until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes this is done with a substance that has astringent properties, and it is better than water alone in resolving the inflammation.
And then, when you see the inflammation to be resolved, then you moisten the intestine with water in which mallow and marshmallow have been boiled, for by this the entry of it is eased with little difficulty.
But if the re-entry is hindered despite this treatment, then incise the rupture a little with an instrument with which hemorrhoids are incised.
And the first treatment is better, when it is proper that there is no incision, and there is no need to worry about drying due to necessity and debility.
Concerning the instrument, this is its form:
One part of it is sharply curved, and the other part is not sharp, and the end is thin, and not as thin as a spatula, rather it should be somewhat blunt.
And thus, when the wound is widened and the intestine protrudes, then its reduction should follow its natural form, to its proper place, if possible, for that is better.
But when the rupture is wide, and is in the lower part of the abdomen, then you should make the patient lie on their back, and raise their legs higher than their head; and if it is in the upper part of the abdomen, then you should raise their head and feet higher than the lower parts.
And similarly, if the rupture is in one of the two sides of the abdomen, then always direct your attention and consideration to ensuring that the side where the wound is located is higher than the other.
And thus you should administer this in large wounds and in moderately sized wounds.
And in small wounds, place what you have prepared, and then have the assistant place their gentle hands between your hands, holding the entire rupture with their hand and adjusting its edges, and then remove from that area anything that might obstruct the suture.
And this is the common method of suturing: first, take a needle, or several needles according to the size of the wound; then, leaving the width of an annular finger from the end of the rupture, thread the needle(s), making sure you don't knot the two ends of the thread, using two ends of the tape that is below the thread of the inner parts, until you pass it through that part; and you have now joined two ends of the thread and two ends of the tape, and this makes four ends.
Then tighten with a double knot the loop of the needle, sometimes from both sides together, and then join the two edges of the wound with a neat joining; and leave the width of a finger and place another needle in that space, and tie it with thread as you did with the first needle; and continue doing this with the needles as necessary until you have completed the entire closure of the wound.
And let the needles be of moderate thickness, neither too thick nor too thin, for thin needles cut the flesh too easily; and thick ones are difficult to thread, therefore they should be of moderate thickness.
And it is important that the needle not be inserted too deeply into both ends of the thread, so that the flesh is not unnecessarily cut and the wound does not reopen before it heals.
And the sutures should not be too long, lest the wound be hindered from healing.
Then cut off the ends of the needles so that they do not injure the patient during sleep; and place upon the suture finely powdered substances that will hold the ends of the needles; and leave them there until you know the wound is healed.
And this type of suture with needles is most suitable for small wounds, because for suturing them, one or two needles, or thereabouts, suffice.
But the second suture is common: and that is that you stitch together the four ends, of the thread and two ends of the wound, at once, with a needle on which a thread is wrapped, equal in fineness and thickness.
And then, when with the needle you have penetrated these four ends, return the needle to the same point from which you began, so that the drawn thread falls from the highest part to the wound, so that the suture is like a cloth suture with which cloths are fastened.
And place between each stitch the width of the little finger.
And this suture is known to men.
And with such a suture I sewed up the wound which had happened to a man in his belly, which had been wounded with a knife, and the rupture of the wound was more than a palm, and two palms of intestine had come out from the middle of the wound, and the rupture was in the middle of the member; and then I reduced it after the intestine had been outside the wound for twenty-four hours.
And the wound was consolidated in the space of fifteen days or thereabouts, and I cured him and he is healed; and moreover, he lived for many years, and is sound in his dispositions.
And the other physicians had judged that he would not be healed; and it was by a miracle that he was, because I did not cure him with ointment, for it was in a place where ointment was not found nor any medicine, but I placed on the wound old linen twice a day and washed it frequently with honey water until he was healed.
But I will describe two kinds of sutures proper according to the account of Galen.
And it is that you begin with the suture of the chain, and insert the needle from the outside to the inside.
And then, as the needle penetrates the chain and into that same laceration, both straight and along the whole length of the body, leave the end of the thread into which you have inserted the needle; and make the needle penetrate at the other end from the inside to the outside at the other extremity of the wound.
And when you have made that penetration, then you make it penetrate again at that same extremity of the wound from the outside to the inside; and leave the end of the thread that is in this stitch, and make the needle penetrate at the other extremity from the inside to the outside.
And make the penetration with which you make that penetration in the thread at the extremity of the wound which is on his side until all of that penetrates.
And then begin on that same side and sew that with the end of the thread on the other stitch, and extract the needle from the chain which it penetrated, and then return it to that chain and sew the end of the thread which is on the other side with this extremity of the wound; and extract it from the chain which is on his side.
And do this once and again, and do this one time then another time until you sew all that is outside according to that example.
And the sum of this manner of suture is that you sew with a leather-stitching suture with which you sew skins, in such a way that you sew the thread once from the side of the extremity of the chain, and that you leave it another time until you have completed it.
But the second proper kind of suture, which is called *Gualia* and this is spoken of by him and recounted by him, is that you sew it according to the half-stitch with which certain healers sew it, in such a way that you join each part to its corresponding part in a way suitable by nature; and you apply the end of one to the other and the end of the wound to the other end of it, and this is done as I have recounted to you.
So that you place the needle in the extremity of the wound close to the outer part, and make it penetrate to the inner part in that sole place, and leave two ends of the thread; and then turn the needle and make it penetrate from outside to inside in the two ends of the thread.
And then turn them each one, and make each one penetrate from the inside part to the outside part in two other extremities of the wound which are on the opposite side.
And this manner of suture is better than the common light suture, and it is the suture of which I have previously made mention.
This is the discourse of Gualia.
And moreover, some say, the suture of the belly is rectified with two needles, that is to say, that you introduce into each of these a thread, and begin with the introduction of the needle from what is nearest to you, and make it penetrate to the other side according to the suture of those who sew hides similarly.
And know that when the disruption is in the middle of the belly, then the suture of it is more difficult than in other places of the belly.
But the judgment of the treatment of a wound is the judgment of other wounds.
The reason is that when you treat a fresh wound with its own blood before the air alters it, and you return the intestine and sew it and prepare it properly, then place an incarnative powder upon it.
And if the air has already altered the wound, then place upon it some ointments which are at your disposal until they form a hard skin and the threads fall out, and are consolidated with cypress and myrrh; and then treat it as is the treatment of other wounds until it is healed.
But if you do not have the medicines at hand, then immediately place upon it, as soon as it begins to suppurate, old linen, and change it twice a day, as you have been taught, until it is healed, for you do not need other medication in many cases when the wounds are simple.
But if you fear that the wound will spread to the principal members, then it is necessary that you submerge wool in oil of good quality, or in rose oil, and place it in the area around the armpits and under the arms.
And if you feel pain or putrefaction in the intestines, for often this happens, then cauterize it with warm, black styptic wine, especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestine and an opening has been made penetrating into its cavity.
And know that that which is in the large intestine is of easier healing than that which is in the small intestines.
And it is difficult to heal because the intestine which is called "jejunum" does not receive healing from a wound that falls upon it at all; and this is because of the great multitude of veins that are in it and because of the magnitude of those veins, and because of the subtlety of the body of that intestine, and because of its proximity to the nature of the nerves.
But if that which proceeds from the wound is the web (likely referring to a membrane or tissue), and you obtain that recently, reduce it according to your skill with which you reduce the intestines equally.
But if it has passed into the space and has already become green or black, then bind it tightly with a thread over the place that is blackened, so that a flow of blood comes, because in the web are veins and arteries.
And then incise that which is beyond that ligament, and place the two ends of the thread hanging down from the part below the wound outside it, so that the attraction of it towards the falling of the web and the emission of the arm of the wound might be relieved above you.
A reminder of wounds when they happen in the intestine.
When a rupture occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it is possible that it may be restored in some men, because of this, for I saw one who had been wounded in his belly with a lance thrust, and the wound was on the right side of the stomach, and the wound was old and had formed a fistula, and from it came a discharge and flatulence.
Then I pursued his cure, although I was not confident in his healing, but I continued to treat him until he was healed and the place was consolidated (healed completely).
And I, after I saw the place consolidated, feared for the patient that some ill accident might befall him in his belly; and no ill accident befell him because of this, and he resumed his usual habits of eating and drinking, and then he entered a bath and exercised as instructed.
And some experienced men have said that when a wound occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it should be tied in this manner:  that you take ants with large heads; and then join the two lips of the wound and place one of those ants, with its mouth open, over the two lips of the wound; then when it bites those and tightens its mouth, cut off its head, for it will adhere and not dissolve.
And then place another ant next to the first, and do not cease to do this with ants, one next to another, according to the size of the entire wound.
And then reduce (close) that and sew the wound; and let those attached heads remain in the intestine until the intestine is healed; and no harm will come to the patient.
And it is also possible that the intestine be tied with fine thread which is made from the gut of an animal, since it is thinner than a needle; and that is that you take the end of these gut threads, and it should be very clean, and then tie its end with its thread, wrapped around, and then insert that thread in needles and sew the intestine with it, and then reduce it in the disruption.
And this method of suturing with ants is only a matter of trust and hope.
But if the wound is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the small intestines, because then it is only a reduction [in size], and not a complete healing of it.
Of the treatment of a fistula and fistula.  (The repetition suggests perhaps different types of fistula or a more emphatic title.)
Know that every wound and abscess, when it ages and becomes old, and becomes an ulcer, and is not consolidated and discharges pus constantly and is not excised, then, in whatever limb it may be, is universally called a fistula, and we call that a zuchem.
And a fistula, in truth, is a hardened,  hard, white knot, which is not painful and which has a concavity like the concavity of a bird's claw; and for this reason it is called a claw-like ulcer, that is, a claw.
And in some men it is moist, which discharges pus; and quickly the moisture is excised in a few hours. And sometimes this moisture is thick, and it is little, and it is coarse, and it is subtle.
I have already shown all those causes, and the signs of them, and their treatment with medicines in the division.
And it is necessary that I make you know the treatment of fistula which is done by way of operation with the hand.
And know that fistulas occur in all the members of the body; for there are fistulas that reach the large veins and arteries, or the nerves, or the diaphragm, or the intestine, or the bladder, or to one of the ribs, or to one of the vertebrae of the back, or to one of the joints composed by composition, as is the case with the hand and foot; and those that damage the proximity of a principal member are similar.
