Therefore, that one of these fistulas which is according to this manner is among the illnesses of difficult healing, and does not receive a cure, and occupation with it is labor and ignorance.
And if it is one of the fistulas whose depth does not reach those places that I have mentioned, and you hope and trust in its healing, and you wish to arrive at its knowledge, then take a probe of bronze or iron, and if the fistula goes straight, and search for it with it.
But if the fistula is tortuous, then investigate it with a probe of fine lead, for lead, with the lightness of its body, is eased and bent along the tortuous path.
And if the fistula has many horrifying turns, it is not possible for you to determine its course with a probe.
Then, inject a clyster into the orifice, for the humidity with which you inject it will go through the other orifices and clean them.
And then inquire exquisitely, in whatever way is possible for you to know if there are bones or nerves here, and whether the depth of the fistula is far or near, or if it is a fistula that has many orifices, and base this entirely on your strength, on your questioning, with which you question the patient, and the discovery of the pain through your compression with the hand on the place, and similar signs and accidental causes of the abscess.
And when you have considered all those causes with true judgment, then proceed to the treatment confidently: that is, you consider then if the fistula is near the surface or in a safe place, far from a joint, or from a nerve, or from an artery, or from a vein, or from one of the places which I have told you about.
Then you dry the fistula as was said before in its description, and remove what is in that filtration and of corrupted flesh, and that which has grown in the orifice of the flesh, or of skin and the like, and cure it until it is healed.
But if the fistula is of great depth, and is straight, then it is necessary that you incise it in front as much as is possible for you, and then cleanse it of all its corrupted flesh; and then administer cauteries wrapped in sharp medicines, and introduce them into the depth of the fistula, which you have grasped with an iron instrument.
And do this with those cauteries several times until the sharp medicine corrodes all that is rotten in the depth of the fistula from the corruption.
And then restore it with ointments which generate healthy flesh until it is healed.
But if it is not healed by this, then you cauterize it as we have recounted.
But if the cause of the fistula is bone and you are certain of this, then you should investigate it and dry it as we have recounted, if you do not prohibit any cause from a vein or nerve or principal member as we have said.
But if the bone is exposed to you and there is some corruption and blackness in it, then you scrape it until all the corruption is removed; and then treat it with that which will cause it to regenerate flesh until it is healed.
And if it is not healed and discharges pus as it did before, then know that you have not achieved the total eradication of the corruption; and then uncover it again and meticulously perform its scraping and cleansing with your utmost strength, and then restore it.
And so if it is healed, good; and if not, then know that that corruption is in the deep recesses of the body, which you have not reached, and then for you in that case there is nothing to do but leave it.
And if the corrupted bone is small, or it is possible for you to pull it out, then pull it out with fine forceps which are suitable for this.
And if there are many bones, then meticulously perform the extraction of all of them, and of these do not leave anything behind with your strength.
But if any of the humors cause you difficulty, and there is no fear or error in their incision, then incise those ligaments or flesh if they are there, and free the bones.
But if you are not prepared to remove the bones at this time, then apply something to the wound that will suppurate the wound, and leave it until it reaches the area around those bones of flesh and ligaments, and watch the wound so that it does not heal, and keep constricted the incision which you made in your operation.
Furthermore, if you are afraid, place on it a cloth soaked in crushed rue with oil, or a cloth soaked in Egyptian ointment, or in any of the green ointments, for when those ligaments suppurate, and the bones are separated from them, their removal and their extraction is eased.
But if it is a large bone, such as the bone of the leg or thigh or similar to those, and only its surface is corrupted, then scrape it with a rasp only superficially until that blackness and corruption is removed, and then restore the wound.
But if the corrupted part is large, and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to close that and incise everything up to the place where the corruption has reached, and then treat that until it is healed.
And I recount to you what happened to a man in his thigh, so that you may use this as an example and help in your operation.
There was a young man, around thirty years of age, who experienced pain in his thigh, and from the internal parts a diseased matter moved through the body until the matter reached the thigh, a matter of abscess with a large abscess, and there was no external cause for it.
And then time was extended with that because of the error of the physicians until the abscess was opened, and many matters flowed from it, and its healing was delayed until the leg was fistulated, and many orifices were made in it, all of which discharged pus and humors of the body.
And then many physicians treated him for about two years, and there was no wisdom in them nor in their art, until I was called.
And I saw his leg and the matters that flowed from those orifices in great quantity; and the body of that man was completely emaciated and his complexion was jaundiced.
And then I inserted a probe into one of those orifices and the probe reached the bone.
And then I examined all the orifices and found them to reach to the bone from all parts of the leg.
And then I considered and incised one of those orifices until I uncovered a part of the bone, and I found it corrupted, and it was already earthy, corrupt, and blackened; and a part of the leg was already corrupted, where it had penetrated to the marrow.
And then I sawed off what was uncovered and prepared of the exposed bone.
And I believed that in the bone there was only that corruption which I cut away and sawed off, and I had already eradicated it.
And then I applied the restoration of the wound with consolidating medicine for a long time, and it is not healed.
And then I iterated and uncovered the bone a second time, upon the first uncovering, and I found continuing corruption in the bone.
And I sawed off what appeared to me to be that corruption, and then I was forced to perform its cure and restoration, and it is not restored.
And then I uncovered it again and did not cease to incise the bone little by little, particularly and forcefully attempting its restoration, and it was not restored until I incised about a palm's breadth of it, and I extracted that with its marrow, and then I restored that with medicines, and it is lightly consolidated and healed.
And further iteration in its operation and section is not necessary except for the disposition of its fragility and for the scarcity of its tolerance and fear of death, because syncope afflicted him at all hours due to superfluous evacuation of ill humors.
And therefore it is healed by a complete healing, and there is nothing in place of the hard bone flesh; and its disposition in its body is made good and its strength has returned; and it is converted to its dispositions and caused him no impediment in walking or harm in any matter.
But if the bone is prominent in the place of the flesh already removed, then it is necessary that you treat that according to this manner: that you take a ligament and bind that to the extremity of that prominent bone, and command someone to extend it upwards; and place another woolen ligament, thicker than the first ligament; and then bind that over the flesh that is below the bone, and extend its extremity so that you draw the flesh to the parts below, and you uncover the flesh over the place which you want to saw so that the saw does not injure the flesh.
And place wood or a board beneath the bone decently, for when you do this, the saw is not prohibited from cutting the corrupted flesh.
And it is necessary that the sawing be done over the corrupt place little by little, for if the corruption is in the concavity of the bone and does not appear in its apparent presentation to the senses, then force the sawing of it another time.
And if the bone is corrupt and not prominent, but one part is continuous with the other, and the corruption is in the middle or part of it, then you uncover the flesh from all parts completely; and then place wood underneath, and then saw that from the first part where the corruption is until the sawing is continuous with the other part.
And let the sawing be according to the location of the corruption, little by little, as we have said.
And if the corruption is in the joint, incise that corrupt joint, and saw the bone where it is continuous with the other part.
And if the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then there is no reason for it but ingenuity/skill.
And if the corruption is in the chest/breast of the hand or foot, it is very difficult; however, it is necessary that you investigate the corruption in whatever manner appears to you, and that you open it, and clean it according to any disposition which is possible for you, and with each tool be rectified for you, when it does not injure you by vein or nerve.
And know that the incisors and saws to incise these bones are many according to the placement of the bones, and the preparation of those, and the thickness of those, and the subtlety and the size and the smallness of those, and the hardness and their rarity; therefore it is necessary that you prepare for every kind of operation an instrument convenient to that operation.
And know that this operation signifies to you the type of instrument which you should choose; when with you there is great study and long and complete knowledge of the methods of this art, and experience of kinds of illnesses, because then you will already have for yourself what is convenient in instruments for every illness.
And I am forming for you at the end of this chapter the number of instruments which you will place among the simple ones with which you will proceed in your practice which you will experience over the others. Form of saw:

And it is only suitable that with that (saw/rasp) the heads of the joints are scraped when they are corrupted, or the opening is very wide.

And it is made of Indian iron, and similarly, it is necessary that all the things we have said of razors and incisors and saws [apply to this instrument as well].
Of the incision of extremities and the sawing of bones.
The extremities may be affected either by an intrinsic or extrinsic cause; or if you have treated that corruption with medication and the treatment is ineffective, or if you see the corruption spreading through the limb without any apparent cause, then you should incise that limb to the point where the corruption has reached, so that the patient escapes death or a malignancy that is a greater impairment of the limb.
And the sign of that which afflicts him is that the limb is blackened as if burned by fire, or the blackness spreads until the putrefaction enters that limb so as to affect it and reach the whole body; therefore, treat it with an incision.
Similarly, if the cause of the corruption is the impact of some poisonous creature, such as a sea scorpion, a viper, or a spider, and the like.
And if the corruption or the injury is in the extremity of a finger, then you should incise the finger and not give space to the corruption so that it spreads to the hand.
And similarly, if it affects the hand, then you should incise it from the root of the wrist, and do not give space to the corruption so that it spreads to the forearm.
And if it affects the arm, then incise the arm up to the elbow at the joint.
And if the corruption passes beyond and you see it around the elbow, then in that case the patient's death is certain; and to administer anything else besides this in treatment is a waste of effort/virtue.
And similarly, if corruption affects a toe, then you should incise it up to one of the ankle bones.
And if it takes hold in the instep of the foot, then you must incise the whole foot. But if it rises up into the knee, then you incise the leg up to the knee joint.
And if the corruption has already reached the knee, then there is no remedy for him, only release, and leave the patient to die.
And the manner of incising or excising a limb is that you tighten a ligature on the part below where you wish to incise, and tighten another ligature above the area; and the assistant stretches a ligature on the lower parts, and the assistant stretches another ligature above; and you separate the flesh that is between the two ligatures in such a way that all the flesh is exposed; and then it should be incised or excised.
And it is necessary that a linen cloth be placed on all parts so that it does not touch the healthy area, and that the patient experiences appropriate injury and a hot abscess.
But if you encounter a flow of blood while operating, then you should carefully cauterize the affected area, or apply some blood-stanching powders; and then return to your treatment until it is completed.
And then bind the wounded limb with a suitable bandage, and treat it until it is healed.
And I recount to you what happened to a man in his foot, this same accident which I have recounted to you, which is that a blackness occurred in his foot with a burn that resembled a fire burn.
And that corruption occurred first in his toe before spreading to the whole foot.
And then that man prepared himself, for he saw the cure proceeding to the limb with such vehemence that he was overcome by pain and burning, and he incised that foot up to the joint, and it healed.
For a long time he suffered the same corruption in the finger of his index hand.
And then he came to me, and I attempted to repel that excess with what I applied to his hand in medicine, namely the purification of his body; and the excess was not repelled and began to spread to another finger, until it began in the hand.
And he asked me to incise his hand, but I did not want to do that, hoping for the repulsion of that excess, and moreover fearing lest the incision of his hand cause death, because the patient's strength was failing.
And after he departed from me, he returned to his land, and it came to me that that man prepared and incised his entire hand, and he was healed.
And this narrative is added only to be of assistance in the treatment of this illness, and to give a meaning by which it may be understood and according to which treatment may be undertaken.
Concerning the treatment of sinuses and the nature of clysterization with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in fleshy limbs and the space of the abscess is extended until it forms a collection of pus, and then is broken and perforated, and the pus flows out, and the place remains empty as if it were a vessel, and the skin that is above it is like a thin cloth, and it has not reached further corruption, and does not affect the bone or nerve or ligament, it is called a sinus, and not a fistula.
However, if the space is extended to the point that the corruption affects any member, then it is called a fistula and not a sinus.
And its treatment is to incise that skin completely as it is swollen, and especially when it is thinned and made like a cloth, and ensure that it does not adhere to the place through its corruption.
But if you expect that the skin will adhere and that the corruption has not spread further, and there is looseness of the skin, then you treat that with a clyster.
And it is that you consider the sinus, and then if it is large, and the arm that is there has a foul odor, then you should clysterize it with an Egyptian medicine which is of oil and sharp wine and vinegar, taking equal parts, and they are mixed in a vessel, and cooked over the fire until the medicine becomes thick and reaches the consistency of honey.
Then you take what is necessary from it and dissolve it with water and honey; and clysterize the sinus with that, and constrict its opening, and leave the medicine in it for the amount of two hours.
And then extract that with a syringe; and do this for so many days until the sinus is cleansed and the foul odor is removed.
And sometimes it is clysterized with lye water, when you do not have this medicine present; and it is that you take the ash of vine wood, and put water over it; and then strain that and clysterize with it until you are convinced that the sinus is then clean.
And if the patient does not tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the lye water, then clysterize with water and honey with crushed vinegar mixed in, and clysterize with wine and honey mixed, for it is the property of honey to cleanse and wash, and the wine makes it adhere to the sinus, especially when the wine has an excess of astringency and dryness.
And when you have done this several times and are convinced that the sinus is now clean, then clysterize it with something that causes flesh to grow there.
And it is that you take diafinicon ointment and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine; or that you clysterize with some other medicines and ointments that have the properties which I have already written about in the treatise on ointments.
And if the opening of the sinus is narrow so that the clyster nozzle cannot enter, then you should widen it little by little with a small instrument, and place a small tent wrapped in Egyptian ointment or basilicon ointment inside until it is widened.
And similarly, if the opening is wide, then you should approximate its lips with sutures and leave an opening large enough for the clyster to enter without further enlargement.
And if the opening from which the pus drains is situated higher up, then dry the lowest part of it so that the pus may flow downwards, for when the pus is confined to the depth of the sinus, flesh will not grow there.
And if it is not possible for you to dry the sinus to the lowest point as you wish, then endeavor to prepare the limb with a preparation by which the pus will drain slightly, according to your ability, so that nothing obstructs the flow of pus.
But the cloth that is over the opening of the sinus, change that frequently so that the pus that collects there may drain. And so that you may know if the pus that flows from it is much or little, or is digested, or is undigested.
And with this, you should consider the location of that sinus, whether the patient feels pain there or not, and if there is an abscess there.
And as your treatment is prolonged in this manner, and you see that the matter ejected onto the opening of the sinus is already adhering, or is about to adhere, then remove it on the third day; and when you remove the dressing, then change the cloth for another and renew the ointment.
But if near the sinus there is a subtle poison, then do not despair of its adhesion, for its healing is near, for there are some men in whom the generation of flesh in their wounds is slow, and others in whom the opposite is true.
And if undigested pus has been issuing from the sinus for many days, then know that the sinus will not adhere; and if the adhesion of the sinus is delayed and the cause prolonged, then you should adjust to drying medications; and if the medications are moist in their substance and in their effect, they are dry like diachylon ointment when superfluous addition of resin is adjusted to it.
And the best remedy with which it is cured, is that you take diachylon ointment and dissolve it in rose oil, and add old,  strong wine to its substance, and carefully mix that with it, and use that.
And it acts similarly in that situation, when there is no pus: diachylon ointment, honey cooked until thickened, and use that; or take myrrh, aloes and incense, crush everything and pulverize it over the honey while it is on the fire, and then apply this on a cloth and bind it over the sinus; and apply that with the honey that you have cooked until thickened, and powder it over a cloth and bind it.
And sometimes are administered with it aristolochia longa and roots of lily of the valley (and it is lily of the valley) and flour of rye and centaury, and use these simples or compounded, crush those, sift them and powder them over the honey which has been mentioned; and use that.
And if the sinus is prolonged, and is not healed by this treatment, then know that the corruption is in the depth of it, and it has already spread to the bones or to the other hard parts or ligaments; then you treat it with fistular treatment, as above mentioned.
Of the treatment of paronychia and worn nails and added digit incision and section.
Paronychia is an abscess which arises under the nail of the finger or toe of the hand or foot, and quickly spreads to other fingers, and as it is prolonged and its cure is less valued, or it is inflamed by a hot abscess, or it is corrupt, and throws out pus until it corrodes the root of the nail, and quickly corrupts the whole of it, and quickly spreads its corruption to the bone until there is a foul smell to it, and the extremity of the finger becomes swollen, and its colour becomes dark.
And therefore when you have treated this with what we have said in the division and your treatment does not succeed, then it is necessary that you incise with an iron all the superfluous matter that remains of the nail and then cauterize the wound nearby, because a hot cautery is very helpful in this.
But if the bone is healthy and the nail is healthy, and the additions to the nail, now empty, compress the flesh at the inner parts, and begin to prick and injure it, then it is necessary that you place a fine thread under the angle of the nail that pricks the flesh, and lift it up and incise that flesh easily, and place on what remains of the flesh some corrosive burning medicine until all is removed; and then cure it with ointment until it is healed.
But if the corruption has already reached the bone, then it is necessary that you incise that bone and extract it, for a bone that is corrupt will never heal.
And if you see the corrosion and corruption going into the finger, then you should incise it in one of the joints, according to the way its treatment is prescribed, and then cure it until it is healed.
But if the nail suffers from percussion or attrition, and there is vehement pain in it, then you should first bleed the patient, and then cut the nail with a sharp instrument with a transverse section from above downwards, and be careful that the section does not reach the flesh that is under the nail, for you will cause the patient intense pain, and it will cause the generation of superfluous flesh in that place; and then treat that place with something that relieves pain.
But a finger that is born with such a condition in the hands of some men is completely fleshy, and very quickly it is bony, or very quickly it is in the nail.
And some of them are born at the root of the joint, or their origin is in some of the joints of the fingers. But that which is born in some joint of the finger does not hurt, and that which is born in the joints is very quickly painful.
And of those that are born fleshy, the incision is easy, and it is so that you incise that one below its root with a broad instrument.
But of that which is born at the root of the joint, the cure is difficult; and therefore beware of its excision.
But in that which is born in a finger between the knuckles, it is necessary that you first incise its flesh with a round incision down to the bone, and then saw it with one of those saws suitable for this, and then cure it until it is healed.
But consolidation which happens to the fingers between their knuckles, either by the birth of a man thus, or by the consolidation of a wound, or by burning with fire, or similarly, then it is necessary that you cut that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural form.
And then place between them cloths soaked in rose oil so that they are not lightly consolidated, and separate them, or place between them a thin lead plate until it is consolidated as necessary.
And similarly, if consolidation occurs in some fingers with the palm, then you cut that consolidation according to what is prepared and suitable for the shape of the limb.
Concerning the incision of vices, these are varices.
Vices are varicose veins, and they are swollen, thick veins full of melancholic superfluities which appear in many parts of the body, and most often in the legs, and especially in the legs of travelers, laborers, and porters.
Then it is necessary that you administer a purging of the body of black bile a few times with a strong expulsion, and then bleed the patient from the basilica vein.
But the treatment of them with iron is according to two methods: one is that they are incised and that black blood is drawn out, and the other method is that the vein is elevated and completely extracted.
The incision is done in this manner: first bathe the leg with very hot water until the thick, feculent blood of the leg is resolved. Then bind the patient's leg tightly from the highest part of the thigh to below the knee with a bandage. Then incise the vein in one, two, or three places with a wide incision. Then draw the black blood out with your hand, from below the leg upwards, and from above downwards, until the quantity of blood you desire and deem sufficient has been released, and which will remove the strength of the ailment.
Then bind it up, and instruct the patient to abstain from foods that engender black bile.
And repeat the treatment by phlebotomy when the veins are full and this harms the patient.
But their elevation is according to this manner: you who shave the patient's leg, if it is very hairy, and then put it in a bath and bathe the leg with hot water until it reddens and the veins are prominent; or if a bath is not available,  have the patient perform strenuous exercise until the limb is heated.
Then incise the vein in the right place along the vein, either behind the knee or at the lowest point of the leg near the ankle.
Then open the vein with an incision and scrape the vein on all sides until it is sensitive, and it is from its appearance that you see this redness tending toward blackness.
And then, as you remove it from the vein, you see that white thing which is like a cord.
Then you place a leech below it so that it is elevated and is drawn to the vein, and suspend it with golden thread.
Then make another incision near that incision for three fingers' breadth, and scrape the vein from the upper part to where it appears.
Then elevate it and incise it in the back section near the heel, and then extract it and elevate it until it is released through the second incision, and then extract it from the incision which is above it.
And do this until you extract it from the incision that is most superior or highest of all the incisions, until all of it is out; and then incise it.
And if it does not obey you to be extracted and elevated, then introduce a needle with strong double thread and tie it and extract it, or introduce a leech beneath it and move your hand in all directions until it is drawn out. And be careful that it is not incised; for if it is broken, it is made difficult for you to elevate it much, and it becomes harmful for the patient because of that damage.
And when that is entirely removed, then place on the site of the wound wool soaked in rose-scented wine and olive oil, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the vein is constricted, having a tortuosity to which there is a convolution in the parts, and it is not according to the straightness as we have said, then it is necessary that you incise it at every part of its tortuosity and at the places of its convolution, and then suspend it with hooks until you elevate it entirely.
And be careful in your incision that you do not cut the vein and that you do not injure it, for it will be difficult for you to elevate it; and use your prudence in this.
Form of the incision with which the vein is cut:
It is not as sharp as hooks, nor is it of a sharp extremity so that it does not injure the vein; and it is of considerable flexibility, for if it were thin, it would cut the vein because of its thinness; it has superfluous thickness as we have said.
Of the extraction of the vein of the leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot lands, such as Al-Ategi in the land of the Arabs and in the hot and arid lands of India, of little moisture, and in which neither grass nor trees grow.
And it is easily generated in other places of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is from putrefaction that comes from under the skin, just as serpents, worms, and scabies, and worms are generated between the skin and the flesh.
And a sign of the onset of this vein is that a vehement inflammation occurs in the leg; and soon the area is blistered; and then the vein begins to emerge from the place of that blister, which resembles the root of a plant.
And when its extremity appears, then you should wrap around it a piece of lead weighing one drachm to two drachms, and tie it and leave the lead suspended from the leg.
And as many times as you see some cause of it in the outer parts, wrap it in lead and tie it. And if the growth is prolonged, then you should incise a part of it and wrap the remainder; and do not incise it at the root until it has emerged, for if you incise it at the root, it is retracted and enters the flesh and causes an abscess and suppuration in the area and a bad ulceration.
Therefore, it must be treated gently and extracted little by little, until it is completely removed and no part of it remains in the body.
And from this vein it has emerged in some men to a length of five palms and fingers, and it has even happened to me that one emerged of twenty palms.
And if it is incised at the time of its treatment, then insert a probe into the opening and perforate it with a long opening to the body, until everything that is in the matter is evacuated, and treat the putrefaction of the area for several days with medicines; and then treat it with the treatment for abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, and especially when it appears at the joint of the foot or in the foot, and many openings appear on it, and a branch emerges from each opening.
And therefore treat it as we have said in the division and with what has been said above.
Concerning the incision of worms generated under the skin, and it is called bovine illness.
This illness is called in some lands near us bovine illness because it often happens to cattle, and it is a small worm which is born between the skin and the flesh, and it goes throughout the body, ascending and descending, and it is shown by the feeling of its movement from limb to limb until it breaks through the skin and comes out.
And its generation is by the putrefaction of certain humors, just as worms and serpents and earthworms and ascarids happen in the belly.
And it is feared because of its malice, for when it goes into the body and is elevated to the head, and it has reached the eye, then very quickly it enters and destroys the eye, and this happens many times.
Therefore, when you want to cure this and extract it, then it is not done except by its movement and appearance to the touch, and then it is necessary that you constrict what is above it and what is below it with strong binding and constriction, and then cut above it and extract it.
And if it is deeply embedded in the flesh and you do not find it, then you apply a cautery with fire to the place until it dies, and the greatest thing that is feared is its corruption, by which it corrupts the place as we have said.
But if you see it has reached the head near the eye, then bind above the eyebrow with a strong ligature, and then cut above it, and extract it; and it is necessary that the patient use purification of their body with medicines evacuating bad putrid humors, and guard against foods generating putrefaction.
Concerning the incision for the disease *nafir*.  (Nafir likely refers to a specific illness; without further context, a precise translation is impossible.  It might be a transliteration of an Arabic or other non-Romance word.)
This illness is called *nafir* in our land, and it is a pain which comes in certain limbs, and then is moved from limb to limb.
And I have already seen that, as I shall relate to you. I was called to a sick woman in a village, and I uncovered her arm and saw a small inflammation in the superficial vein of the arm, and after it remained for an hour, I saw that inflammation moving like a worm climbing upwards, but more lightly than a worm, as if it were quicksilver when it runs from place to place.
And then the pain left that place and fixed itself in the muscle, and remained there for about an hour.
And then it ran to the other side until it reached the other arm.
And then I remembered that it moved through her whole body as I had seen; and I was very amazed at its movement and its speed from limb to limb. And I had seen this before with my own eyes in this manner, however I have seen many who find pain shifting from limb to limb. But I did not see it with my eyes as I did in the woman.
And I considered that this was so because the woman was old, with dry flesh and exposed veins, and in this case, that windy sensation was apparent in its movement.
And it is necessary that it not appear, according to this backward cure, in those who are delicate and with moist bodies and hidden veins.
And if you want to cure him, and the patient feels that pain, then it is not apparent to your eye as we have said, then you press on that place lightly and dry it until that constricted windiness has exited, and cauterize the place.
But if you do not see it with your eyes, then you treat it with chest percussion and with things that expel winds, and drive them out with foul-smelling pills and pills of serapium and the like.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to their types, and according to the places in the body where they land, for their diversity is that some have large points, and small ones, and concave ones, and blunt ones, that is, solid ones; and some that have three angles, and some that have barbs; and some that have feathers.
And of the signs of the brain, when an arrow falls into it and penetrates the bone, and comes out of the skull which is above the brain, for there happens a sudden violent headache, and dimness of vision, and vertigo, and redness in the eyes, and inflammation and redness of the tongue, and spasm and loss of understanding, and vomiting of bile, and quickly speech is cut off and the voice is destroyed, and there comes from the place of the wound a white moisture similar to pus, and the eyes appear watery like the flesh.
And therefore if these signs appear to you, then abstain from treatment and from the extraction of the arrow, unless it is outside [the skull].
But the signs of the arrow when it falls into the heart, is that it is near the left nipple, and feels as if it were stuck in something hard, and not in something empty; and very quickly the movement of the arrow is similar to the movement of the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, and there is coldness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and syncope; and then know that death approaches without any doubt.
And the signs when the arrow has wounded the lung are thus: the expulsion of frothy blood from the wound, and the vessels that are near the neck are inflamed, and the color of the patient is changed, and he breathes heavily, and he desires the intake of cold air.
And if the arrow falls into the diaphragm which is in the chest, and the pain then is near the small ribs, and the wing is made large with violent pain and with sighing, and not all the members of the muscles are affected.
And if the arrow falls into the stomach, then very quickly some undigested food comes from the wound.
And if the arrow falls into the abdomen, and is stuck in it, there comes some discharge of pus from the wound, or intestine which is ruptured, and there is no hope in its cure without the extraction of the arrow.
And if the arrow falls into the bladder, and urine is released, and some matter proceeds from it to the outer parts, and very quickly pain comes upon the patient, then know that it is mortal.
But the other members, such as the face, the neck, and the throat, and the shoulders and the armpits, and the vertebrae of the back, and the clavicle, and the hip, and the leg and similar members, are saved according to the best dispositions when the arrow does not find a vein or artery and is not a poisoned arrow.
And I recount this to you that I have seen of the nature of these arrows, so that you may know from this about your own treatment.
The reason is that in one man an arrow fell into the lacrimal duct of his eye at the root of the nose, and I extracted it for him from the other side, below his ear, and he was healed, and no harm befell his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew which fell into him in the corner of his eye, below the eyelid, and the arrow was already hidden and I only found a small tip with which it adhered to its shaft; and it was a large arrow of the sharp arrows of the Turks, with a lion head on it which had no barbs.
And the Jew was healed, and no ill accident befell his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian man which had fallen into his throat, and it was an Arabic arrow, and this one had barbs; and then I made an incision on it between two guides, and it was already submerged in his throat; and then I was careful with it, and then I extracted it, and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man which had fallen into his belly, and I thought that he would die from it; and therefore, since thirty days had passed and nothing had changed about his condition, I made an incision over the arrow and pulled it out, and he was healed, and no harm befell him.
And I saw another man in whom an arrow had fallen into his back, and the wound had healed over him, and since it was then about seven years, the arrow came out at the root of the thigh.
And I saw a woman into whom an arrow had fallen in her belly, and the wound is healed within her, and nothing is changed in her condition, and the woman finds no impediment in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man into whom an arrow fell in his face, and the wound is healed and the arrow broken, finding here no great pain, and many similar to these.
And I extracted the arrow from the man, one of the king's princes, that had fallen into the middle of his nose and had already inclined a little to the right side, and the whole arrow was hidden.
And I was called to his treatment for the arrow wound within three days.
And then I found the arrow wound very narrow; and then I examined it with a thin probe, and did not feel it; but he felt a prickling below his right ear, and then I suspected that this prickling was due to the point of the arrow.
And I plastered the area with a plaster which has attractive and digestive virtue, trusting that the area would fester so that the location of the arrow would appear to me, so that I could cut it out.
And no significant incident occurred in the area indicating that the arrow had reached that place; and I continued with the plaster on him for many days and no incident occurred; and the wound consolidated meanwhile, and the patient rested from its extraction for many days until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and reported this to me.
And then I applied a sharp, corrosive medicine to the wound for many days until it was opened, and with a probe I examined it, and found the tip of the arrow, which adhered to the shaft.
And then I applied to the opening of the wound that sharp medicine until the point of the arrow appeared to me visibly, and I spent time with this for a period of up to four months.
And then, when the wound was enlarged, and it was possible for me to introduce forceps into it, and remove it, and I did not succeed in this way, then I did not cease to refine and contrive on it with ingenious methods, until I succeeded one day in taking it out with cleverly made forceps, as the form of these will appear at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it; and then I restored that wound.
And some doctors judged that the cartilage of the nose would not be restored, but I restored it and the wound is healed, and the patient is completely healthy, and he suffered no harm from it in any way.
And I recount to you the manner of extraction of certain arrows so that you may establish a rule and understanding concerning that which I have not said, for the details of this art and its definitions are not comprehended in a narrative nor do books contain them.
And wise artisans compare the little with the much; and the present with the absent; and find new operations and instruments because of the unexpected occurrences of strange things in the art.
Therefore I say that arrows are not removed from the limbs in which they are fixed except in two ways: either by traction from the place where they enter; or by counter-traction from the opposite side.
And that which is removed from the place where it entered, is an arrow lodged in fleshy tissue, and is pulled and removed.
And if it does not yield to such treatment at the time of extraction, then you must leave it for a few days until the flesh around it softens, and its extraction and removal are facilitated.
And similarly, if it is lodged in bone and does not yield to such treatment, then leave it for a few days and repeat the traction and movement each day, and it will eventually be removed.
And if it still does not yield after a few days, then you must perforate the bone around the arrow on all sides with a fine drill until the area around the arrow is widened, and then extract it.
But if the arrow is hidden within the body and is beyond feeling, then you should probe for it with a probe; and if you feel it, then extract it with instruments suitable for its removal.
And if you cannot reach it because of the tightness of the wound and the length of the projectile, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein there, then you should enlarge the wound so that it is possible to extract the arrow.
And if it is held by barbs, then you should free the flesh in which it is embedded from all sides with all the skill you can muster; and work on this, if you cannot, by evicting the flesh in the area of the barbs and twisting them until they release.
And when you perform the extraction of the arrow, wherever it may be, then use twisting with your hand and forceps on all sides until it yields; and proceed with great subtlety so that the arrow is not broken and so that its extraction is not made difficult for you.
And if you cannot do it at that time, then leave it for a few days until the parts surrounding it soften, and then repeat the attempt, for then it will be easier. But if you encounter excessive bleeding, then administer what we have said concerning its treatment in the appropriate chapter.
And be careful to avoid cutting a vein, nerve, or tendon, and use every possible means to remove the arrow; and do this with ease, calmness, slowness, and firmness, as I have recommended to you.
And therefore make the arrow straight so that the patient is positioned according to the way the arrow entered, for this is more helpful. But if this is not possible for you, then administer what is possible according to the circumstances.
But if arrows are protruding from the opposite side, or if any part of it projects outside the body, or if the arrowhead causes swelling at the extremity of the wound, and this swelling is noticeable, then cut above it; and let the incision be of such a size that the forceps can grasp it; and then pull it out, for it is easily removed in this way.
But if it is lodged in the bone, then twist the blade in the opposite direction until the arrowhead enters the bone, and widen the opening, and then extract it.
And if not, leave it for a few days, and then try again until it comes out.
And if the shaft of the arrow remains inside, then push it in completely.
But if the shaft has fallen out and you want to administer a push, then introduce, if it is blunt, a concave instrument so that the concavity fits onto the end of the arrow and then push it in completely.
But if the arrowhead is concave, then push it in with a strong instrument entering it, for the arrowhead is thereby eased.
And if the arrow is poisoned, then you should cut away all the flesh in which the poison is, if it is possible for you to do so, and then treat it with whatever is appropriate.
And if the arrow enters the foot, or the belly, or the bladder, or the side, and is close enough that you can touch it with a probe, and it is possible for you to cut around it, then cut around it, and be careful not to cut any veins or nerves, and extract it, and then sew the wound if necessary, and treat it until it is healed.
The form of forceps with which arrows are drawn:
Let their ends be like the beak of a bird, which are made like a saw when they grip the arrow so that they do not let go of it.
And of these, many types are made, large, small, and medium-sized, according to the size of the arrow and its smallness, and the size of the wound, and the tightness of the place.
like a ray, so that the entry into the concave arrow is eased and is pushed with that.
Of phlebotomy of the veins.
The veins for phlebotomy, of which the customary number is thirty, of which sixteen are in the head; that is, two pulsating veins which are near the ears, called alhasisain; two veins which are in the two temples, apparent; and two veins which are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes which are called aspicientz; and the vein situated in the middle of the forehead, and the vein which is at the tip of the nose; and two guidegi which are at the neck; and two veins which are in the upper lip; and two veins which are in the lower lip; and — these are the veins which are called algelic —; and there are two veins which are under the tongue. But the veins which are phlebotomized in the arms are five; for of these, one is the cephalic, and it is on the wild side (of the arm); and the common people call it "the vein of the head", and it is a blind vein, and it is a median vein, and it is composed of a branch of the basilic, and of another branch of the cephalic; and the people call this one "vein of the whole body", and the basilic is the vein which is situated on the inner side of the arm, and is called "citillar", and the vulgar name it "vein of the belly"; and funis of the arm, and it is situated on the forearm, and it is that in which phlebotomy is performed, and it is that which appears above the elbow by manifest appearance; and salvatella, and it is the vein which is between the ring finger and the little finger, and there are two branches in it.
And in the foot and leg there are three veins; one of these is below the bend of the knee on the outer side; and the second is the saphena, and its location is below the leg on the inner side; and the sciatic vein, and its location is below the leg on the outer side; and in the leg there are other veins similar to those.
The benefit of opening the veins near the ears is for old cataracts and migraine and sahafati (a likely corrupted or archaic term, possibly referring to a specific ailment) and old, malignant head ulcers.
And the manner of bleeding those veins is as I recount to you:  it is that the head of the patient should be raised, and the head of the patient should be rubbed at the occiput, at the location of both veins, with a wide cloth, with good friction; then the patient should tighten their neck with a bandage until the two veins and their locations appear near the ears in two depressed places of the head; and feel for them with your finger, and if you feel the pulse of those with your finger, then bleed there carefully.
Then you take the lancet, which is sharp, and then you insert it below the vein in the incision until the phlebotomy reaches the bone. Then raise your hand with the vein and the incision to the upper parts and incise the vein with a sharp incision. And let the length of the incision be the amount of two fingers laid together, or thereabouts; and let blood escape as much as you wish.
Then bind it with powders and leave it until it is healed.
And already and previously, at the beginning of the book, the incision of these and their cauterization was described.
But of the two arteries that are at the temples, bloodletting is helpful for chronic and sudden severe migraine, persistent ophthalmia, and a body flooded with acute superfluities affecting the eyes.
And the manner of bleeding them is as I have recounted to you: constrict the patient's neck tightly until the two veins appear clearly to the touch and their pulse is felt beneath your finger; and then you shall bleed with a lancet.
Then you raise the skin above the vein at the upper part with your index finger, and insert the phlebotomy lancet underneath, and lift the vein upwards; and incise it as you have done the other two veins; and let the blood flow according to your need.
Then loosen the ligature on the patient, and place your finger on the vein for an hour, and then apply a compress and powders, and bind it tightly above, and leave it until it is healed.
And now the recollection of them, and their incision, and their drawing has been preceded in the beginning of the book.
Therefore, bloodletting from the frontal vein is useful for phlebotomy of the cephalic vein in chronic facial diseases, and for migraines, stiffness, and foul ulcers.
And the manner of his phlebotomy is as I recount to you; that is, that the patient constrict his neck with a bandage until the vein appears; and then take the instrument which is named a fossor, of which this is the form:
Place the prominent point which is at the head of the fossor upon that vein and strike it with a hammer or other such thing in the manner of it, and let the blood flow according to the quantity you desire; and then loosen the perforation of the patient and bind it tightly until it is healed.
And sometimes it is opened with a lancet, however it is not fitting that the lancet be of a sharp point, like the other lancets, but rather of a broad point, and phlebotomize it across, because the bone is near and easily broken there by the lancet when it is thin.
But the benefit of the two veins that are at the tear ducts of the two eyes is for scabies of the eyes, for redness and swelling in the eyes, and for diseases of the face.
And the manner of the phlebotomy is that the patient constrict his neck with a bandage, and then phlebotomize them, and you stand at his head.
And let the phlebotomy be done across its length a little with a small lancet, because in that place there is no flesh; and if the lancet is thin, its point is easily broken.
Then let the blood flow as much as is necessary to you, and place cotton there, and bind it for a night, and then unbind it.
But phlebotomy of the vein of the nose is beneficial for acute fever and violent headache, and for diseases of the face and migraine and redness that happens in the face, especially when it is chronic.
And the manner of his phlebotomy is that the patient constricts his neck, and then hold his nose with your left hand, and take a long, thin lancet and insert it into the middle of the nasal bridge in a straight line, because the vein is not apparent to the touch here, and because the blood begins to flow immediately.
See that you move your hand with the lancet little by little and let the blood flow according to your need; and then bind it for a night, for it is lightly restored.
But the help of phlebotomy of the two jugular veins is for constriction of the throat and the beginning of leprosy and many diseases and impetigo and bad, corrosive ulcers, as well as herpes.
And the manner of phlebotomy of these is that the patient should bind each side of his neck with a bandage; and the surgeon should stand over the patient's head, and the patient should be sitting on a stool.
And then phlebotomize the vein lengthwise with a wide lancet little by little; and then extract blood according to the tempered quantity or according to what you see is necessary; and then do likewise with the other vein.
And then untie the bandage and bind the two veins with a moderate constriction so that the patient is not suffocated, and leave it until morning because the wound is healed.
And the benefit of phlebotomy of the algerich veins helps small abscesses and pustules of the mouth and corruption of the gums and bad ulcers and fissures, and bad ulcers that are in the nose and around it.

Of cupping and the qualities of using them.
Cupping glasses are made of horn, or of brass, or of glass.
And cupping is done in two ways: one of them is with scarification and extraction of blood, and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping, which is without scarification, is done in two ways, for it is either with fire or without fire.
And cupping glasses, which are administered with scarification and bloodletting, are applied to fourteen locations on the body, of which one location where cupping glasses are placed is at the nape of the neck, and that is the back part of the head; and the middle location, on both sides of the neck together; and cupping glasses on the chin below the jaw, below the mouth; and cupping glasses on the two shoulder blades; and cupping glasses on the hypochondrium, and that is above the end of the rib cage; and cupping glasses on the two flanks, and they are on the sides of the body; and cupping glasses on the two hips; and cupping glasses on the two thighs.
And cupping does not extract blood except from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and therefore it does not diminish the strength as it does with phlebotomy; nor should cupping be done with or without fire in any illness caused by repletion until the whole body is evacuated.
But if necessity requires cupping for illness or for a continuous condition, we administer it at any time, at the beginning of the month and at the end of it, and in the middle of it, and at any time.
Therefore, when one sees any of these conditions, and especially if it is in the third or middle part of the month, then we order cupping to be performed after two or three hours of the day have passed.
And the benefit of cupping the nape of the neck is that when it relieves heaviness in the head and that which is settled in the eyes; but this should be after evacuation of the whole body.
And this cupping is in the place of phlebotomy of the cephalic vein.
And it is not permitted to use it on one who has a cold complexion, or in whom there is catarrh, for it causes great harm to such a one.
And therefore it is not fitting to use this on the elderly, and on those whose heads are afflicted with cold illnesses.
But to one upon whom this is continually done, oblivion is brought about; and therefore you should order the cupper to place his hand a little with the cupping glass at the lower parts, fearing the generation of oblivion.
But cupping with alcohol is in the place of phlebotomy of the vena fusca and the basilica vein: and for that reason it benefits asthma, and the constriction of the throat, and the rupture of an instrument of the throat (possibly referring to a throat condition or instrument), and cough, and repletion (or fullness).
And it is necessary that the alcoholic cupping glass be applied a little higher, for if it is placed lower down, it generates weakness in the body and in the stomach.
And cupping with *alhagdahan* (a substance whose exact nature is unclear from this text alone) benefits accidental pains in the head, and ophthalmia (eye inflammation), and migraine, and suffocation, and pains in the roots of the teeth; and it is in place of phlebotomy of the basilica vein.
And it is necessary that you command the cupper that he not press his hand with scarification lest he wound the artery and cause a flow of blood.
And cupping below the chin benefits ulcers in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and similar conditions of the mouth; and it stands in place of phlebotomy of the [veins] near the two lips (referring to veins in the lip area,  "aliheric" likely being a specific vein or area).
But cupping which is done above the two shoulder blades benefits against *caphatan* (a disease or condition whose exact nature is unclear from the text), which is caused by an excess of heat.
And cupping on the two inner parts of the ankles benefits as much as phlebotomy of three veins, that is, the basilica, median cubital (fusca - probably a mis-spelling or variant of mediana), and cephalic veins, because it draws blood from all those smaller veins which are in the flesh, and those small veins from the other larger veins until the attraction reaches the three named veins.
And command the cupper that he not proceed excessively with scarification, because the place is bare of flesh, and under it are nerves and arteries.
And there is a cupping glass with which one cups above the buttocks, and it benefits against hemorrhoids of the anus and deeper ulcers.
And it is necessary that you command the cupper that it be a large cupping glass, and that it be of brass, because the place requires strong suction; and very easily are thin cupping glasses broken and it should be scarified with scarification.
But cupping of the legs diminishes pain through evident depletion, because it draws blood from the whole body, and it benefits against old pains of the joints and of the womb, and of the bladder; and it provokes menstruation, and it benefits against swelling and dropsy; and it stands in place of phlebotomy of the two saphenous veins, and of the two iliac veins.
However, they greatly weaken the whole body and cause syncope in many men.
And of the iliac cuppings, the benefit is similar to the benefit of cuppings of the two legs.
And the quality of the placement of the cupping glasses is that you place the cupping glass initially empty and draw it by moderate suction, and do not prolong the placement of the cupping glasses, but place them lightly, and remove them carefully, and that the humors come to the place by equal admixture. And do not cease to iterate this and continue until you see the place redden and swell and the redness of the blood appear.
And then you scarify and iterate the suction little by little, and then consider the disposition of the humors.
Because that man who has tender flesh and rare pores, it is necessary that you scarify him once and no more, lest the place be ulcerated.
And command the cupper to amplify the scarification and make it a little deeper, and temper the suction with ease.
And if the blood is thick, then it is necessary that you scarify twice, for in the first time the way of thinning is opened to the blood and its watery part. But in the second [scarification], to the refined extraction of the thick blood.
And if the blood is very foul, then let a third scarification be done so that it achieves the desired result.
Finally, when we want to extract the blood little by little or a small amount, one scarification is enough for us.
And if we want extraction of much blood, we make many scarifications; and if we know that the blood is thick, then it is necessary that we scarify with a deep scarification.
And the term scarification in which [the depth is] only that of the elbow.  (This likely refers to the depth of the cut, comparing it to the width or depth of an elbow.)
And if the one whose elbow is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are tight, it is necessary that the places for the cupping glasses be anointed with lenitive, aperitive, and resolutive ointments; if it is in summer, such as aloe oil, or violet oil, or sweet almond oil, or poppy seed oil; and if it is in winter, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or chamomile oil, or blood [oil], and similar things.
And if the superfluity is thick and cold, then the oil should be amaranth oil, or balm mint oil, or aloe oil, or anise oil, and similar things.
But if the patient has large pores and tender flesh, then the use of oil should be prohibited, and for such a person the places for cupping should be washed with rosewater, or with cold water of nightshade, or with poppy water, or with water of borrage, and similar things.
And the one whose blood is of much humidity, wash his cupping places with sour wine, or with myrtle and sumac water, and similar things.
And for the one whose superfluities are thick, wash his cupping places with old wine, or with water of sanicle (self-heal), which is marjoram, or with a decoction of anise, or of chamomile, and similar things.
And the cupping should be done before the bath; rather, it should be administered about an hour or two before the bath, and he should sleep after the cupping.
What is it that the person to be cupped or bled ought to observe before cupping?
It is necessary to consider at the beginning: if the one to be cupped or bled is choleric and has sharp blood and inflammation, then he should take cooling things, such as sour pomegranates, and endive in sour wine, and lettuce, and dried hemp, and julep, and similar things.
And give him foods such as chicken, and meats of domestic birds, and foods seasoned with acids, and the like.

And it is necessary that on the day of cupping and bloodletting, his drink should be greater than his food.

But the cuppings that are without scarification should be cuppings that are placed on the liver, and on the spleen and gout, and on the navel, in the places of the knees, and the groin, because these members do not tolerate scarification upon them.

And it is placed on the throat when it suffers from obstruction or swelling, for by virtue of strong attraction it quickly opens the obstruction and eradicates the swelling from its place.
And similarly it acts when it is placed on the liver and spleen because of flatulence enclosed within them.
And these cuppings are administered empty with suction alone; and sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of tepid water in diseases of the spleen.
And this is so that the cupping glass is filled and is larger; that is, with hot water alone or with water in which herbs suitable for this purpose have been boiled.
Method of applying a fire cupping glass: let the width of the opening be two fingers, as we have formed it; and the length of the cup be half a palm; and let it be on the patient's side, in the area that is in the circumference, half of it; the hole should be small enough for only a needle to enter it.
Let it be made of bronze, copper, or citrine, with thick edges, smooth, even, and polished so that it does not injure the limb because of its position on it.
And let there be a transverse bar of bronze or iron in the middle of it, on which a candle with fire is placed.
And let this cupping glass be larger than the one we have described; and a smaller one, depending on the illness and the age of the patient.
And the cupping glasses for infants and the weak are different from the cupping glasses for youths and those of robust physiques.
And the way to place this cupping glass with fire on the limb is to extend the wick with a decent wood fire in front of a small wax candle, and place it on the middle of the transverse bar in the center of the cupping glass, so that the fire is lit around the part underneath so that the patient's body is not burned.
Then it should be placed on the patient's limb, and the finger should be on the hole we have mentioned, until when we hold the cupping glasses and do not need to remove the finger and let the vapor escape through that hole, then the cupping glass will immediately be loosened.
And then ignite the wick according to its method, and repeat it if you need to.

Leech application is not administered to many limbs, but only to those limbs where the placement of cupping glasses is not possible due to their small size, such as the ear and gums and similar; or on a limb devoid of flesh, such as a finger and nose and similar.
And the quality of using those is that you should find leeches in fresh, clean, stagnant waters.
And then let them be left day and night in fresh water until they are hungry, and so that no substance remains in their bellies; and then let the body be evacuated at the beginning with phlebotomy and cupping; and then rub the patient's limb until it reddens; and then they should be applied to it.
And when they are full and fall off.
And if it is possible to draw blood from the area with a cupping glass, it is more beneficial afterwards.
And if not, then wash the area with wine vinegar and then with plenty of water; and let it be rubbed and squeezed.
And if the flow of blood is prolonged near the placement of the leeches, and it oozes, then you dip a cloth in cold water, and place that on top until the oozing stops.
And if the bleeding is excessive, then sprinkle crushed vitriol, or gallnuts and similar styptic substances, on it until the bleeding stops; or place halved, scorched beans on the area and leave them until the beans adhere to the area and the bleeding stops.
And it is necessary, if needed, to reiterate the application of leeches, when it is possible to change those.
And if the leeches are prevented from attaching, then rub the area with fresh blood or prick the area with a needle until blood comes out; and then apply them, for when they sense any blood they immediately attach.
And when you want them to fall off, sprinkle a little crushed aloes, or salt, or ash on them, and they will fall off immediately.
Thus begins the prologue of the third part: On Restoration. This chapter is very often necessary in the art of medicine; and it is the restoration of fractures and accidental dislocations in the bones.
O, son, know that foolish and common physicians boast of this chapter, those who have never looked at the books of the Ancients or read a single letter from them.
And for this reason, this skill of the learned in our land is kept private; and I have never forgotten this at any time; and I have not acquired this except through long reading of the books of the Ancients and studying their understanding, until I extracted their knowledge.
And then I dedicated myself to experimentation and study throughout my life.
And I have already described in this chapter everything that my knowledge contains and on which my experience is based; since I have abridged it for you and freed it from lengthy discussion; and I have shortened it for the sake of brevity; and I have explained it clearly; and I have shown many forms of instruments that are used in this, as is done by way of explanation, just as I have done in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter One.
Summary and collections from the essence of bone fractures, from those whose premise is necessary, before we begin the recollection of fractured and diverse members, each one by itself. Therefore, I will say in the prologue of this chapter the summaries of the discourse; and the chapters to the understanding of which, at the beginning, the artisan is obliged and based on the truth of those things; that is, you and those who are ready to know or learn this noble art, besides yourselves.
And therefore I say that when a fracture, dislocation, sprain, or fall happens to someone, then you should begin with phlebotomy or purging, or both together, if no cause prohibits it, such as weakness of virtue, or if the one to whom it happened is an infant, or an old person in decrepitude, or it is a time of intense heat, or intense cold.
And then let him be content with a diet of cold herbs and the meat of birds and rabbits; and we prohibit thick meats and filling oneself with foods and any food that fills the veins with blood, until you are sure of a hot abscess, and if you do not fear effusion of matter to the limb.
And then return the patient to his former regime with which he is accustomed.
And so, as the broken bone begins to be restored, then the patient should be fed nutritious foods, very thick and heavy foods in which there is viscosity, such as foods made from wheat cooked in water, and rice, and heads [possibly referring to a type of grain or seeds], and feet, and bellies of cows, and eggs, and fresh fish, and thick wine and things similar to those, and with this regime, the coagulation of the fracture is made more easily and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in men who are in their first youth and in the decrepit, cannot be rejoined and consolidated according to their nature because of the desiccation of their limbs.
But however, nature adjusts and applies to the broken bone from every part some substance similar to glue, in which there is thickness that adheres and binds it until they are together glued and bound together, until they come to the end of strength and firmness, just like fire in the beginning, until no cause prevents it from its work.
And for this reason it is necessary that you give the patient foods in which there is weight, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the types of fracture are diverse according to the diversity of the limbs, because a fracture of the bone of the leg is different from a fracture of the bone of the head; and a fracture of the bone of the foot is different from a fracture of the bone of the arm; and similarly all the other limbs are different from one another.
And I, coming with a remembrance of every type of fracture, expounded in its own chapter distinct from the others.
Many diversities of fracture in itself. And the types of bone fracture are diversified in themselves; for there is an arcuate fracture besides those which happen in the stars (perhaps meaning splintering); and sometimes a fracture is made according to the length of the bone: and so are the fractures of the shaft and splinters and additions proceeding and non-proceeding, just as is done in the quill when it is prepared for writing, and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption in the head, and there is a small fissure fracture.
And to each type there is its own art or torment in its restoration, as will be its remembrance distinctly in its place (in its own section/chapter).
Many signs of bone fracture. And by this a bone fracture is known: its crookedness, and the prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and it is of it when we press it with one's hand.
And therefore when there is no apparent crookedness in the place, nor is it made evident by your touching when you touch the bone with agitation and the patient does not find great pain, then there is no fracture here.
Having said this, it is possible that there is a crookedness or small fissure, where it is not fitting that you move it with your hand and with total compression; having said that, place on it the medicines of which mention is made near this point which pertains to the location; and then it should be held tightly by a subtle bandage.
And know that if the place is bent and not split in two, or other such thing does not happen to it suddenly, as if the whole part is torn from its counterpart, then you must begin at your earliest opportunity the rectification of it before an abscess occurs there.
And if a hot abscess occurs there, then leave it for a few days until the abscess ceases; and then treat it according to whatever method you can of lightness and ingenuity.
And know that the restoration and healing of it is easier than of a bone in which a sudden injury has occurred.
And then tighten it according to the indication/remembrance of it. (Meaning:  tighten the bandage or dressing as needed, following the instructions/observations made previously).
But if the bone is injured, then extension of that broken limb from two parts is necessary, if it is the hand or the foot; or with your hand if the limb is small; or with two cords or with one cord and your hand.
And let your position be such that you place the injured limb in a position according to its natural form, while the broken parts of the limb are extended; then it is necessary that you return those additions with skill and in whatever way you can to their places.
And take care, according to your strength, that no pain or injury befalls the patient because of your operation; and it is necessary, according to your ability, that you apply one bone to the other, its counterpart, according to their best forms (or natural alignment).
And it is necessary that at that time you skillfully touch that with your hand, and if you see anything amiss there, rectify it and adjust it to the best of your ability.
And avoid vehement extension and compression, as many foolish physicians do, who often cause a hot abscess through their operation, or cause contraction in the limb, as we have seen happen through their operation many times.
And the nature of the binding of the quieted limb is that which I recount; so that you may know that broken limbs are diversified in their smallness and in their largeness and in their form.
And for those that are small, such as the arm and the finger and the penis and similar to those, then the bandage should be of very fine soft cloth.
But for those that are large, such as the thigh or the leg, it is necessary that in those there be a wide and firm bandage, because the wide bandage connects the large limb and binds it on all sides with even pressure, so that no blemish enters it.
Then begin, after your equalization evacuation, to place a bandage suitable for this, over the place of the fracture, with three or four layers depending on what the limb requires; and tighten your hand a little with the bandage.
And then come with that to the highest part of the fracture site, and tighten less than you did your binding with which you bound the fracture site. And then lengthen the bandage from the fracture site a little, and loosen the binding little by little, until you reach a large part of the area.
And then take another bandage and wrap it again over the broken place with a bandage, and then come with the bandages to the lowest part of the fracture; and let your work be in the tightening of the bandage and in the loosening of it according to what we have said in the first bandage above.
And then place between the bandages some lion's wool or cloth with which the crookedness of the fracture is padded, if there is crookedness; and if not, do not place anything there.
And then place another bandage over it, and then apply over these bandages suitably made splints immediately, if there is no inflammation or hot abscess in the area, and then apply to it what drains the abscess and reduces the inflammation; and leave that for a few days; and then you tighten the splints over it.
And let the splints be of half a reed, smooth and cleverly shaped; or let them be of pine wood; or of palm branches; and let the width of each splint be according to this form and this figure:
However, it is necessary that the splint that is placed over the fracture be a little thicker and smoother than the other splints, and the length of the splint according to what is appropriate to the limb in size; and then tighten over the splints with another bandage according to your first tightening; and then bind over it with thread suitably according to what we have said of tightening, and it is that your tightening should be according to the place of the fracture itself, and the further it is from the fracture, the less the tightening.
And it is necessary that the threads be moderate in thickness and fineness, and be of linen; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen in the practice of some foolish people placing their threads of twisted rope, and this is a great error, when the tightening lacks uniformity.
And similarly, very fine threads are not suitable, because you will not achieve with those what you want by tightening, and it is not suitable that there be less than a finger's breadth between splint and splint.
And if the patient is harmed by the ends of the splints near the binding in those places, then place under those some carded flax or wool until that does not cause him injury.
Sometimes a fracture is accompanied by a wound, and there is a rupture in the bone, and I will come to the recollection of that in the following chapter according to its particularity.
And know that not every broken limb is bound with splints on the first day; the reason for this is that, when the limb is large, you should not place splints on it until approximately five or seven days, or more, according to your assessment of the risk of a hot abscess forming.
And this is the manner of the plasters with which the Ancients restored [injuries], which are placed on fractures, dislocations, and sprains.
Method of a common plaster, a shortening of the restoration of fractures, suitable for many constitutions and especially for women and children, because it is something which neither heat nor cold overcomes: and that is to take the powder of the mill, which is the pith of the flour that hangs from the movement of the millstone on the walls of the mill.
And make it like this, except that you sift it with egg white, and make the confection neither thick nor thin; and use it in this way.
Another method of a plaster restoring fractures, dislocations, and sprains: take of asafoetida, of ladanum, of acacia, of elecampane, and of megrach (which is the root of wild pomegranate), of each one dram (x); of myrrh and aloe, five drams (v); of aloes, twenty drams (xx); all are to be crushed, sifted, and mixed with aloe water or with egg white if the patient's constitution is hot; and use this plaster, for it is of good composition and restores slightly broken bones, and benefits many men.
Another method of a plaster which benefits fractures and sprains: take of megrach and of white mallow, ten drams (x); of myrrh and of aloe, five drams (v); of acacia, six drams (vi); of Armenian bole, twenty drams (xx).
All are to be crushed in a mortar with much grinding, and sifted, and made into a confection with water or with egg white and applied.
Method of a plaster for joints and bones separated from their places, and if pain occurs in these and in fissures and fractures: let soiled wool be taken and submerged in sour wine and cooked oil and placed on the area.
However, this plaster does not have restorative virtue; it rather draws out hot abscesses and expels pain.
Another method of a plaster that restores a broken bone: take fresh fig leaves and wild poppy leaves; pound everything and make a plaster of them.
Method of a brief plaster administered for the restoration of a bone fracture and when you desire resolution of the remainder of an abscess: take roots of marshmallow, of chamomile, and violet flowers, of pea flour, of all an equal part; let all be crushed and made into a confection with wine, if the limb is not inflamed.
