But if it is inflamed, then make this confection with moist coriander water or with water and use it.
Another method of a stronger plaster in resolution than the first, when a hard abscess occurs during the restoration of the limb: take roots of marshmallow and linseed, and of pomegranate, and melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, of all an equal part; let all be crushed and made into a confection with willow water or with fresh water or with wine, all this according to the quality of the limb and the condition of its color.
But Hippocrates did not say in his book that it should be placed on the broken limb during its restoration except a cerate made of wax and oil and nothing else; and he recommends that it be middling between thickness and thinness.
But in Gaul it is seen that they are placed on the broken limb during its restoration, and the things which are drying with little heat are thus, like myrrh, incense, and similar to those things which we have mentioned.
Method of a plaster to apply to injury and pain: take millet, of wax, of shaved human hair or bird down, and of marshmallow, and salt, equal parts; let them be crushed and sifted and made into a confection, and let a plaster be made.
But the amount of time the bandage should remain and then be loosened is this: consider if no pain or itching has occurred, and the bone is not broken or dislocated, then you do not loosen it for many days. And if vehement pain or itching or inflammation occurs at the place, then immediately loosen or untie it at that hour, and remove the plaster from it.
And take linen cloth and a moist sea sponge, and submerge that in lukewarm water, and with that wash the area until that itching ceases and the pain is calmed; and then let the limb rest for an hour; and then place over it clean wool submerged in sharp wine and oil or rose oil, and bind it on for a night until you are sure of a hot abscess, and that the inflammation of the limb is reduced, and its pain is lessened.
And then repeat that thin bandage and small plaster and do not tighten it as much as your first constriction, but refine it with that until it is healed.
And if you see that the abscess, and the redness, and the pain, and the inflammation, and all the symptoms are now completely reduced, and you judge there is no need for plaster or bandage, then you repeat that as you did at the beginning, equally.
And if none of the conditions we have mentioned occur in the limb, then do not untie it before three or four or five or seven days; and sometimes all this is left for twenty days, depending on what appears to you from the loosening of the limb, as we have said, until the fracture adheres, and approaches the coagulation of the flesh over it.
And then adjust the bandage more tightly than your first bandage, and adjust it to the thickening of the patient's flesh, as the recovery of the patient proceeds.
But if you see the site of the fracture drying and thinning more than is appropriate, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it; and therefore moisten it with lukewarm water every time you loosen it; and let this be done every three days; and loosen the bandage a little, and by this operation nourishment will return to the limb, and it will heal easily.
But what foolish restorers of a fractured limb do, when it is not set correctly at the beginning, as it should be, and is set crookedly, is an error in their operation, and a great danger, and if this were correct, the Ancients would have enlarged upon it in their books and would have worked with it.
And I have not seen anyone among them do this, wherefore it is more reasonable that you not do it.
Chapter Second: Of a fracture when it happens in the head.
The types of fractures are many, and their forms are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for some fractures are caused by a blow from a sword, and the blow breaks all the bone into chips, which are below the bone, as happens in a staff, and for this reason this type is called *ascial*; or the sword cuts a part of the bone, and cuts its flesh and does not penetrate to the back of it; and this type of fracture is called *levatio absoluta*.
And the wound of these two fractures is either large or small, and of a fracture which is a contusion or attrition; and its cause is a blow with a stone or a fall on a stone, and the like.
And this fracture is either penetrating, almost reaching the pannicle which is below the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone, and the wound of these two fractures is either large or small.
And there is a fracture which is hidden in the bone, as fine as a small hair, and for this reason this type is called a capillary fracture.
And there is a fracture which is caused either by a fall or by a blow from a stone and the like, which causes some lamina of the bone to enter the inner parts, and is made in a concave place, as happens to the edges of a ram when a blow is struck and a part of it is driven inwards.
And this happens especially in the bodies of moist bones, such as the skulls of children.
And in all these types of comminuted fractures, the pieces are like separated stars. And I will return everything to its place with repositioning.
And we recognize all these types of fractures by probing them and by inquiring about them with probes, and by the presence of corrupted flesh that is over them.
And a hairline fracture is known because it is uncovered on the bone, and is wiped clean, and is touched with a probe, because the fracture appears black.
But in the treatment of fractures, consider first the patient's symptoms, and then if you see by the symptoms that signify by manifest indication a danger, such as vomiting of bile, and stretching out, and loss of consciousness, and loss of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and swelling of the eyes and redness of them, and similar symptoms in the most dangerous conditions, it is without doubt not healed.
And if you see no unusual symptoms you expect healing, then you begin his treatment.
The reason is that if the injured person comes to you at the beginning as injured, and this is in the winter days, then it is necessary that you study the condition of the bone before the fourteenth day according to every condition.
And if it is in the summer days, then it is necessary that you try to set the bone before the seventh day, before what is under the bone is corrupted by the panniculus (tissue), and that the symptoms that we have recounted occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the pannicle covering the brain, and is with small fragmented pieces and attrition, then it is necessary that you incise the comminuted and atrited part, as I shall recount to you.
And it is that you shave the head of the injured patient, and uncover the bone according to custom and manner as much as possible according to the nature of the wound, and that it be light on the patient.
And if you encounter during the uncovering of the bone,  a flow of blood or a hot abscess, then you oppose it with what is necessary, and that is to fill the place with cloths infused in wine and rose oil, and let the wound rest until the abscess is subsided, and you are sure of the flow of blood; and then begin with the circling of the bone and its removal.
And this should be according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you incise the bone with a narrow incisor, and this is its form:  And then administer another incisor like this, a little wider than that one; and this is its form:
And then administer yet another, wider incisor than the second.
And it is necessary that you have a number of different incisors, some of which are wider than others; and some of them are shorter than others and are sharpened at the ends, and are made of Indian iron or steel.
And use them easily with percussion on the incisor so that you do not strike the head and injure it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then it is necessary that you perforate its circumference before you administer the incisors with the drills, which are called shallow drills.
And they are not called so except because those do not reach the limit of the skull bone to that which is near it, because the drill has a round end or edge on that which is below its sharp head, similar to an edge or small circle, which prevents it from being submerged and passing the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take a large number of these drills, each of which suits the thickness of each bone, until you have present in all parts of the skull an end which is sharp, the quantity in length and shortness being according to the quantity of thickness of that bone.
And this is the form of three types of drills: The method of perforating the circumference of the broken bone is that you place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone is penetrated; and then move the drill to another place, and place that which is between each hole the size of the radius or thereabouts.
And then incise with incisors that which is between each hole in the bone, and make it as thin as you can in order to lighten it, as we have said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand or with some other instrument which you will have prepared for this purpose, such as fine tongs and forceps.
And it is necessary that you take great care that the incisor does not touch anything of the brain.
And so, when you have encircled the bone by drilling, and it is separated from the brain if it is continuous with it, and you have extracted it, it is necessary that you scrape and smooth its roughness with another instrument similar to an incisor, but however it is necessary that it be finer and more delicate than the other incisors.
And if some small and splintered bone fragments remain, then take them away easily with whatever instrument you have; and then treat the wound with lint and ointments which we have mentioned.
And the other method of operation is easier, far from fear, which Gualia describes and praises highly; and this is his account: it is necessary for you at the beginning to begin to uncover the part of the bone at the place where the fracture is most severe and most evident, until, when you have uncovered that part and have placed under it the end of that slow and gentle incisor of which this is the form:
And it is so that the lenticular part is broad and does not cut anything, and the sharp part is split in two lengthwise, like a wedge; so that the lenticular part is fitted to the brain with its face, the sharp part entering into the bone.
And then strike the incisor on one side with a small mallet until you cut all the bone easily as it is curved, and you are sure of the periosteum that no accident happens to it in any way, even if the physician were the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, and even if he were sleeping.
Then if any matter attached to the bone remains in the periosteum in some part of the bone, cut that away with the lenticular end of that incisor, and remove it easily, for it is removed without harm and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the periosteum, and what is cut off or removed from the bone is its surface, and there remain roughness and fine splinters, then you must rasp away that roughness and remove those splinters with fine rasps, of which you have prepared a number, of various sizes, so that it is possible for you to use at each place the most suitable and the most appropriate of them according to how your operation proceeds on that broken bone and its shape.
And you must proceed in your rasping of the bone, beginning with the broadest of those rasps, and then gradually use the finer ones, and do not cease to do this continuously until you come to use the finest and subtlest of all those.
But in each of the other small, hair-like fractures and subtle fractures, you must administer care according to what you are doing to rectify them.
And it is a matter that is not hidden from all those who have studied in that art. And this is what we have written and shown regarding large fractures.
And if the panniculus (flesh) is retracted when you rasp the bone, then you must take a linen cloth according to the size of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound; and then take another doubled or tripled cloth and immerse it in wine and rose oil and place this over the thin cloth, and do this as lightly as possible, so as not to burden the wound; and then apply a linen bandage over that, and do not tighten it except enough to hold only the wound.
And leave it with this dressing for a day or two until you are sure of a hot abscess; and then untie it and apply medicines with which there is superfluous dryness, such as flaxseed, barley flour,  powder of lentils and birthwort, and things similar to those.
And it is so that you make a powder of these medicines to be sprinkled on the wound, once they are dry; and universally, apply to it any medicine whose property it is to cleanse and not bite (i.e., cause further irritation).
And take care, according to your ability, that the wound in your treatment be clean; and that there be no dirt of ointment or anything else; nor allow any virulence to gather there; for when it is applied to the panniculus (flesh) of the brain, it corrupts and rots it, and this evil brings about malignant illness to the patient.
And it causes harm to the wound of the head, as the bone is denuded of its covering, and especially when its care is neglected, and there is blackness on its surface; and therefore, if the patient suffers the accidents that we have described, know that it is perilous without doubt.
And if there is blackness not from the medicine that is placed on top, but it is in the virtue of that medicine that produces that blackness, then you must take one part honey and three parts of rose oil, and with these, moisten a cloth, and then place this on the wound; and then treat it with the healing remedies that are appropriate until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Concerning the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken except in two parts together, or one of those, at certain times, because in it there are two bones, for the lower part is cartilaginous, and is not broken and does not suffer except attrition, crookedness, and sinuosity (deviation).
But if one of its two parts is broken, then you must insert your little finger into their nose, and that you align that fracture inside with your index finger, and with the thumb outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And let this be done by you with ease, and be careful that you do not cause the patient pain through your operation.
And if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and the finger does not reach it, then it must be set with the end of a thin rod.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do the same for both.
And take care with its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, then around the seventh or tenth day you should treat the resulting abscess with cautery.
And then insert into the end of the nose a small roll of linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part, or place two small wedges, if the fracture is in two parts, and let the wedges be of sufficient size to fill the nasal opening.
And some of the ancient restorers said that the wedges should be soaked in a medicated paste and changed daily, but I have not seen this done; rather, it is necessary that the linen roll be soaked in egg white mixed with finely ground powder; and then leave the wedge in place until the bone is set and the cartilage is hardened.
And sometimes, instead of a wedge, a goose feather, wrapped in linen cloth, is inserted into the nose; and this is done if the nasal fracture is severe and the patient's breathing is not impeded.
And this method is not necessary for you; and if you wish, do this, and if you wish, use wedges.
And if another time a hot abscess occurs, dress the nose with a plaster or lint soaked in sour wine and rose oil, or with diachylon.
But if a hot abscess does not occur, then it is necessary to plaster that area externally with sesame flour and cooked linseed powder mixed with egg white; and then place linen cloth over it, and do not bind the nose with anything.
But if the nasal bones are broken with a small fracture or are comminuted, then it is necessary to make an incision over them, and extract them with instruments suitable for this; and then sew the incision, and treat it with what promotes granulation and consolidation, and with plasters suitable for this.
And if a wound occurs inside the nose, then it is necessary to treat it with wedges and administer lead canulas until it is completely healed.
Chapter 4.
On the restoration of the mandible when it is fractured.
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, observe then if the fracture is only external and is not broken in two and is bent inwards at the parts, then its diagnosis is easy.
And it is necessary therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, that you insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly if the fracture is in the left mandible, then insert the index finger of the right hand; and push with it the hump of the fracture inwards, towards the outer parts, with your other hand on the outside of the bone; thus properly equalizing it.
And if the fracture of the mandible is already separated into two, then it is necessary that you administer extension from two parts according to straightness until its equalization is possible.
And if there is concussion of teeth with disruption, then strengthen those which you hope will remain with a thread of gold or silver or silk; and then place over the broken jaw a cerate, and then place over it a doubled cloth, and place over the cloth a well-made large splint, or a piece of wood equal to the length of the mandible; and then bind that over it according to how the binding is prepared for you and as the application requires, until it is healed.
And instruct the patient to use rest and tranquility, and give him food of sorbitive lions.  (Note: "Sorbitive lions" is likely a mistranslation or archaic term.  It probably refers to a type of easily digestible food or broth).
And if you estimate that the shape is already altered in any way, then you should examine it and unbind it on the third day; and then rectify that which is already altered and plaster it with powdered barley, with egg white, or with starch, after you have removed the cerate from it, and place wool over the plaster.
And then, since the plaster is attached and adjusted to it, and the position of the bone is not altered, leave it, do not unbind it until it is healed and strengthens the fracture; for many times this fracture is healed in three weeks.
And if another time a hot abscess occurs, then administer that which we have said many times in its sedation until it is healed, and that abscess is softened.
And if the fracture is with a wound, then consider if a piece of the bone is separated or splintered; then you should carefully remove those splinters with what is appropriate for that in the removal of them from the wound.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, then you should widen it with a fleam according to your needs.
And then, when you have removed those splinters, and nothing remains of them, then you sew the opening of the wound if it is wide; and if not, place on it one of the plasters that is suitable for it and heals it, until it is healed.
Chapter 5.
Concerning the restoration of the clavicle when it is broken.
Often the clavicle is broken in the front part above the muscle, and this fracture occurs in one of three ways.
Because it is either broken, or separated, or separated into two parts without splinters; and it is easy to restore; or it is so that splinters are present, and it is difficult to restore; or it is so that the fracture is accompanied by a wound.
And the operation in that case, when the fracture is without a wound, is that you have two assistants present, so that one holds the fractured clavicle in place, and the other extends the neck towards that area.
Then align the fracture with your fingers until its form is as it should be, and there is neither too much prominence nor concavity.
And if you need more extension, then you should place under the patient's armpit a roll of cloth or wool, and its size should be according to your need; and extend and elevate the clavicle and compress the roll with your hand until you align the fracture as needed.
And if you cannot pull the end of the clavicle to the outer parts, because it has reached the acromion, then it is necessary that the patient lie supine on their neck, and you place a moderately sized cushion on their muscle.
And the assistant presses the muscle downwards until the bone of the clavicle that is on the acromion is elevated, and then you rectify the fracture and align it with your fingers.
But if you feel splinters of the clavicle and they are jagged, then you should incise over it and extract those splinters easily.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you make an incision with one of the scalpels that you have prepared for this, after you have placed under the clavicle the instrument that serves as a support; and this instrument is of wood or iron, of which this is the form:
And it is similar to a spoon, of which this is the concavity; and let its width be according to your need, according to the size of the bone or its smallness.
But its length should be as long as is possible for you in the operation; let it have two ends like a fork, and one end be wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound which you have incised when you extracted the splinter from the bone is wide, and there is danger of a hot abscess, then you should approximate the two lips of the wound with suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear a hot abscess, then you fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the size of the wound opening.
And if a hot abscess occurs, then soak a cloth in sour wine and rose oil and wine, and apply it there.
And ensure that the splint is not displaced from the broken bone; therefore, you must see the patient each day. If the bandage is loosened or you see that the splint has shifted, then you must correct this and tighten the binding.
Then place the patient's pillow under their neck, and place a small cushion under their testicles at night on top of the pillow; so that by this means the injured area is elevated, and thus the fractured collarbone is raised relative to the muscle; or their arm is bound to their neck.
And do not loosen the bandage unless an abscess or severe inflammation occurs at the site, until twelve days have passed.
Then renew the plaster if you see the need, and adjust the binding, restoring it, and leave it until the fracture of the collarbone is healed and consolidated.
And it is strengthened and healed, according to the condition, in twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some men it is done in less time than that.
Chapter 6.
On the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The shoulder blade is broken on its lateral side, and not shattered, only the edges are broken.
And so when the shoulder blade is broken—and it is broken in the middle of the shoulder blade—and is not known except by touch, then according to the nature of the fracture, forcefully prepare it and reduce it to its natural shape in every way possible to you.
Then place on the area powdered finely ground egg white and lion's wool; then apply a poultice of doubled cloth; then place over it a thin linen bandage, according to the size of the whole shoulder blade or a little more.
But if the bandage is concave in any place on the shoulder blade, then you fill that concavity with lion's wool, and then tighten the bandage evenly.
Then bind it tightly above with a long bandage, with a decent constriction and firmness of the bandage until it is not moved from its place.
And examine the bandage each day; and every time it is loosened, bind it again and tighten it, and adjust the bandage if it has moved from its place.
And have the patient lie on their healthy side.
And the shoulder blade is restored in twenty days or in twenty-five.
And so when this number is complete, then you unbind the bandage, and you should know for sure that it is from the bones of which the beginning is not feared nor their dissolution.
And if it proceeds from the location of the fractured bone and you place it under the bandage, then you incise over it, and remove it, and act as we have said in the fracture of the clavicle concerning the treatment of a hot abscess if this occurs.
Chapter 7.
On the restoration of a fractured foot.
The foot is sometimes broken in the middle, and this happens slowly; but its extremities break much more quickly, being crushed and comminuted.
And among the accidents [complications] when the middle of the foot is broken is that it inclines inward, and great pain occurs, and difficulty in breathing, and coughing, and a strong fever and vomiting blood; and the cavity in the broken bone is apparent and not hidden from feeling.
And its restoration is that the patient should be supine on his back, and a cushion placed between his shoulders; and then press on his muscles, and adjust his bones with the hands on both sides, and be skillful in the operation and in the setting, in every way possible and appropriate, until the bone returns to its proper shape.
Then place a plaster and lint over it, and place over this a thin splint of willow or alder, or something similar in lightness, after you wrap it in cloth; and then be skillful in binding this over the broken bone so that it is not loose; and use a bandage in a circular fashion around the dorsum sometimes, and tighten it with a decent constriction; and then check the bandage frequently, and tighten it when it loosens.
And if necessity demands loosening of the bandage because of corrosion or itching, or pain, or an abscess that occurs in the area, then examine and unbind it, and remove the plaster, and correct what has happened by appropriate treatment; and then reapply the plaster, if the situation requires it; and adjust the bandage until it is healed.
Chapter 8.
On the restoration of ribs when they are broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs occurs only in the thicker parts, where there are two; and in their extremities, in the anterior part, no injury occurs because they are cartilaginous. And its recognition is not hidden from perception through its examination with the fingers.
And the restoration should be thus: align the fracture with the fingers as much as possible, until it is aligned as needed, and then apply a plaster and bind the broken bone with a bandage, if that is necessary.
And if the fracture of the ribs is towards the inner parts, then the patient experiences vehement pain and a stabbing sensation, like the stabbing pain that occurs in someone with pleurisy, because the bone pierces the membrane; and it causes difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting of much blood; and this is difficult to cure.
And the Ancients were ingenious in this with many devices.
And of them it is said: it is necessary that you give the patient foods that produce inflation and flatulence, so that the belly is inflated and extended, and the fracture is pushed outwards.
But we avoid this so that it does not lightly cause a hot abscess, if it does not already exist; and if it does exist, then it already helps with that.
And some of them said that a cupping glass should be placed on top and that it should draw strongly; and this seems reasonable, but however, it is feared that the cupping glass will draw superfluities to the place because of the weakness.
And some of them said: the place should be covered with wool that has been submerged in hot oil, and powders should be placed between the ribs until it is filled, so that the bandage is even as it wraps around that roundness; and then the patient should be treated with a treatment for pleurisy, with diet and medicine.
And if a vehement cause constricts the patient, which he cannot bear, and the bone pierces the membrane with a painful puncture, and we fear for the patient, then it is necessary that we make an incision over the place, uncovering the broken rib, and then place over it an instrument that preserves the lung, the remembrance of which has been previously mentioned, and that we cut the bone easily, and extract it.
And then we should join the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with stitching, and treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an abscess occurs inside, then you should take and pour powders into rose oil and place it on the place, and the patient should be treated with what is appropriate for the internal abscess.
And let him lie supine on the side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Chapter 9.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the spine and neck.
Sometimes a fracture occurs in the bones of the neck, and later it leads to their attrition, and similarly to the vertebrae of the back. And when this happens to someone and you want to know if he will be healed or not, then consider this: if you see his hands already softened, stupefied, and dead, and he cannot move them, extend them, or clench them, and when you constrain them with your hands or prick them with a needle and he feels no pain in them, then know that he will not be healed, and in most dispositions, it is mortal.
And if he moves them and feels constriction in them with the hands and puncture, then know that the marrow of the bone is saved and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if you see in the vertebrae of the neck something similar to that and you want to know if he will be healed or not, then consider his feet.
Then, if you see that they are already softened, that which happened to the hands happens to the feet, and further, when he lies on his back, he experiences flatulence and excretion without his will, and sometimes he is lying on his back and wants to urinate but cannot, then know that it is mortal, and then do not labor in his cure.
But if none of those things happen, the cause is better and lighter.
And his cure is that you should make a perforation for the sedation of the hot abscess, that you place on the afflicted vertebra, only pure rose oil, or with boiled egg yolks; place that on it several times a day until the hot abscess is sedated.
And then apply one of the comforting and stimulating plasters, and bind it tightly; and command the patient to use rest and tranquility and that he not sleep on the side where he finds pain until he is healed.
And if it happens that because of the attrition of the bone splinters or some part that is already separated from it, then it is necessary that you dry the wound and remove that bone; and then approximate the two lips of the wound, with sutures if it is large; and then treat it with some incarnative ointment until it is healed.
But if the back part of the hip bone is broken, and it is the end of the coccyx, then it is necessary that you insert the index finger of your left hand into the anus, and that you adjust the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible according to the second articulation; and then place a plaster and bandage on it, if you have any of those, and then bind it tightly.
And if you feel broken splinters in it, then you should probe that area, and remove them, and treat the wound with the treatment that has been previously described until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Of the restoration of the hip.
The hip bones are rarely broken, and if they are broken, then their fracture is only a separation at the extremities of those bones and they are broken lengthwise and lean inwards; and the patient experiences pain and throbbing in the area, and the leg is numb when it is fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it until it is over the fracture, which is the shape of it.
And if the fracture is only at its extremity, then you bind that according to what is prepared for you by equalization, binding until it is similar to its natural form.
But if the fracture is lengthwise or it leans downwards, then you make the patient lie on their stomach so that the restoration of that fracture is prepared for you. And then when you have bound it, place a plaster over it, and then place a splint of wood or whalebone over that, and tighten it with a bandage over which you do not fear puncture of the fracture or movement of the splint.
And bind the concavity with something that fills it until it takes the bandage according to its equality, and command the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you cease the extension of it and its restoration until the hot abscess is settled, and place on it that which settles it as has been described before; and then return to its restoration, and bind it as is necessary.
And if it happens that the bones are jagged or comminuted, then you pull them out.
Chapter 11.
Of the restoration of a fractured humerus.
The humerus is that which is between the hip and the head of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then its restoration is in one of two ways, of which one is that you take a wooden splint of moderate thickness according to this form:
And let two bandages be tied at its two extremities, and then let it be suspended from a raised position.
And place the patient on a seat; and then place their broken arm on the splint until it reaches the armpit, in the middle of which the curvature of the splint should be attached.
And then suspend some heavy object from it, or the assistant extends it downwards; and then the physician should set the fracture with his hands, until the fracture is properly reset.
And the other method is that the patient lies supine on their back, and suspend the arm from their neck with a bandage; and then order two assistants, that one of them hold that which is above the fracture with their hands; and the other hold the lower part; and each of them extend to their part.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then you tighten it above the fracture with a bandage and above that with another bandage, and each of the two assistants extend to their two parts.
And so if the fracture is close to the extremity of the muscle, then it is necessary that half of the bandage be placed on the armpit and the other below the fracture near the elbow.
And similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, then it is necessary that the bandage be placed on that spot and on that elbow.
Swallow that with ease and without effort, and then when it is swallowed as it should be and is applied with good application, then tighten it unless a hot abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then leave the constriction until the seventh day, and place on it soiled wool soaked in sour wine and rose oil until the abscess subsides, and then you tighten that.
And the way to tighten it is to place a plaster over the fracture, and then place a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster; and then adjust the arm on that support, and place your open hand over their muscle.
And place the cloth and tighten the arm on the support so that the arm is in a stellate position, unless some cause of the broken bone prevents this.
And then use splints.
And it is that you place over the fracture that splint which is wider and stronger than the other splints; and place between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with the addition of three fingers on each side.
And then tighten the splints with the bandage which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your bandage over the place of the fracture should be tighter. And the longer the fracture, the less tight the bandage should be.
And so if you see the placement of the splints and the bandage as we have said at the time when you restore the limb to its proper position, then you do [the procedure].
And if you fear a festering abscess, then you leave the bandage and splints in place until the seventh day, as we have said.
And then observe the bandage in all parts for three days so that swelling or inflammation does not occur at the site; or it is forbidden for food to reach the limb due to the tightness of the bandage; and then rectify all this according to what we have said.
But if you are confident that none of this will happen, then do not untie the bandage until after three days or more.
And let the patient's sleeping position be on their side, and their hands should be on their stomach.
And place over the injury a poultice full of wool, evenly distributed, and check every hour of the night and day that the shape of the broken limb is not loosened or dissolved, or that the bandage is not softened; therefore, rectify all this with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order which we have previously mentioned, so that the food is light at the beginning until he starts to recover strength; and then it is fitting to increase his food.
For it is customary for the injured limb and his leg to be bandaged for forty days; and then it is necessary that they be unbound and a bath be administered, and that it be medicated with ointments which rectify that.
And if the fracture is badly damaged, then you should not unbind the bandage and splints from that for ten days or for up to two months.
Chapter 12.
On the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called two focils; of which one is small, and it is that which is next to the little finger, and the other is large and it is that which is placed below the small finger, on the lower side.
And very easily the larger bone is broken alone, or the smaller one, and very easily they are broken together.
And when the small bone is broken above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the bone is broken below, the fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when both bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the smaller bone above, then it is convenient for the physician, after its restoration, that he apply slight extension easily until it is aligned.
But if it is the large bone that is broken, then it is necessary to apply a stronger extension.
And if both bones are broken together, then you must apply the extension much more strongly.
And it is necessary that you place the shape of your hand during its restoration and its extension stretched out on a cushion, and the thumb of the hand should be on the upper parts, higher than all the other fingers, and the little finger lower than the other fingers, and the patient should be lying on a flat couch.
And let there be a cushion in front of him at his height or level, so that the patient does not suffer discomfort.
Then let the assistant extend the arm from the lower part with his hand or with a bandage, and another assistant extend it from above similarly.
Then the physician should manipulate the bone until he reduces it to the best possible position.
And if in the fracture of the bone there is a jagged break, then you must forcefully return all the pieces to their place with your skill.
And if a separated, jagged piece appears that is detached and you have no hope of restoring it, then you should saw it off and remove it according to the method which we have described above.
But if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, I have already dealt with this in a separate chapter, and therefore take care of it accordingly.
And if an abscess occurs early in your treatment, then you should apply a cloth with a plaster made of rose oil and white wax, of moderate consistency, neither too thick nor too thin, and bind the cloth tightly over it until the abscess subsides; and then remove the plaster and apply a poultice prepared from fine powder with egg white, and then apply a bandage; and let the bandage that is applied over the fracture be firmer and tighter.
And know that the number of bandages on the arm varies depending on the circumstances, whether the fracture is in one bone or in two bones at once.
Then apply your bandage over the fracture site firmly and tightly, and where the bandage is over the parts above the fracture or the parts below it, apply the bandage a little more loosely, as was mentioned at the beginning of this chapter.
And the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture should be soft and damp, not hard or rough, and the thread with which it is bound should be of medium thickness, neither too fine nor too coarse, as we have already recommended.
And examine the limb and the bandage after a few days. And if any problem arises that requires correction, such as itching that occurs in the limb, then you should bathe the limb with warm water until the itching stops.
And leave the limb unconstrained until it rests, and then reapply the bandage.
And if the fracture is now softened and the bone is healed and similar to the others, then correct everything with your skill.
And consider further whether nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightness of the bandage, for then you must loosen it a little and leave it so for a few days until nourishment flows to it, and then tighten it again.
But if the patient does not experience any of the problems we have mentioned, then the bandage should not be loosened for about twenty days or thereabouts.
And then the patient's hand should be suspended by its own power of movement, and make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is healed in thirty days or in thirty-two days, or very quickly in twenty-eight days, and all this depends on the constitution and the strength of the patient.
Chapter 13.
On the restoration of a fracture of the wrist and fingers.
Know that fractures rarely happen in the wrist and finger knuckles; instead, they often suffer attrition (wear and tear).
And when a fracture or attrition occurs in the hand, then the patient should lie down and place a cushion in front of him, evenly, and then place his hand on the extended cushion.
Then the assistant extends the broken bones, and the physician joins them until they are properly set with a good union.
Then it is necessary to apply a plaster and lint, unless a hot abscess is present; and then apply a bandage over it according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
But if the fracture is in the inner parts of the palm of the hand, then make a kind of ball of cloth, and instruct the patient to grasp it with their fractured hand; then let it be bound tightly with a long cloth.
And let the bandage be of leather with cloth in which it is softened, so that the leather is applied to the palm; and tighten it as necessary.
But if the fracture is in the outer parts, then it is necessary to place a bandage above and another bandage below on the palm, so that the hand is open and straight; and then apply the splint, as the hand is placed on the splint between the fingers with bandages.
And if a fracture occurs in any of the knuckles of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it should be bandaged as necessary and then bound to the palm; and if you wish, place a small, straight splint on it so that the fracture is elevated and not moved.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as in the middle finger, or the index finger, or the ring finger, or the little finger, then it should be bandaged and tied to the finger next to it; or all should be tied together consecutively, which is better; or place a small splint on it, as we have said for the thumb.
And consider, in your restoration, and alongside that, the disposition of a hot abscess and oppose — with your operation — what is necessary when any of these things occur, according to how it is described.
Chapter 14.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the thigh bone.
The thigh bone is often broken and is felt according to whether it is broken in the front part or the upper part.
And its restoration is done in this way: a bandage is tightened above the fracture and another bandage below the fracture, and the patient is laid prostrate on their stomach and face.
Then the physician extends the entire bandage to its proper place, according to the nature of the fracture, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if it is close to the hip joint, then let the bandage be close to the knee on the side of the patella, so that the extension pulls upwards, and the other bandage below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the knee, then let the bandage be placed near the knee, and let the extension be in the parts below.
Then the physician aligns the bone with both his hands until he reduces it to its natural form and the bone is joined by good conjunction; then it is necessary to apply a plaster and bandage, unless an abscess occurs in the limb. But if an abscess occurs there, then you leave it for a few days until the abscess has matured and subsided, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be made as follows:  You must wrap a firm bandage around the fracture, and fold it two or three times, removing the excess; and then wrap the leg until it reaches the ankle and the hip joint.
And insert a long piece of felt between the thigh and the leg near the knee in the lower parts, and let the ends of the felt come from the upper part of both sides, and then wrap around the leg and thigh what is superfluous of the bandage.
And then place splints on the thigh at the site of the fracture, and place one splint over the bone of the leg.
And then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the bandage is firm.
And then begin with a firm bandage in the middle over the fracture site with three or four wraps, and the further you move away from the fracture site, the looser, lighter, and softer your bandage should be.
And then extend the two ends of the felt to the lower parts until it reaches the ankle, and tighten both ends of the splints on the other side so that the bandage is not loosened from its place.
And then leave the bandage on, provided that it does not cause corrosion, a hot abscess, swelling, or anything similar to those; and if any of these things occur, then carefully loosen it, and correct whatever has happened, according to what we have already said many times.
But if there is a piece of bone in the bone that protrudes, then you must reduce it as best as you can; and if not, dress it and leave it, and treat it with what has been previously mentioned until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the thigh is tightened in addition to splints being applied, as we have said in the case of the help (treatment) for the arm.
However, in the restoration done in this way, lameness does not occur to the patient; and if it is restored alone, other than having the other leg applied to it, then it is necessary that he limps on the affected side.
And know that the thigh is bound for 40 days or a little more or a little less, and all this is according to the diversity of complexions and other dispositions (of the patient).
Chapter 15.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the kneecap.
Know that a fracture of the kneecap heals slowly, but often causes considerable attrition (or wearing down/damage).
But if a fracture occurs, then it is only a fissure or hole in its parts, and this is with a wound or without a wound, and everything about it has been observed with feeling (i.e., through examination).
And its restoration is such that what is broken of its parts should be joined together with said things until it is added and joined as much as possible by equalization, ease, and fabrication (or skillful construction).
Then apply a plaster and place a round splint over it, if you need one; and bind it above with a suitable bandage.
And then consider all the provisions that we have recounted in the other fractures, such as a hot poultice and similar things, so that every accident is opposed with what is suitable for it until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Concerning the restoration of a leg fracture.
Know that in the leg there are two bones, of which one is large, and is called by the name of tibia [shinbone]; and the other is thin, and is called fibula.
And the same kinds of fractures happen to these two bones of the leg as happen to the two bones of the arm, and therefore its restoration is done in the same way as the restoration of the arm, and the operation is one and the same.
And if both bones are broken together, the leg is deformed in all parts.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is deformed in the front parts.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you will find that below, and it is one of those things that are not readily apparent to you.
Then administer extension, setting, and ligation of the splints according to that same manner.
However, if the leg fracture is extensive, having many fragments, the extension should be gentler and lighter, and alleviate it with your skill.
And in the leg, there is some similarity of operation to the arm; and when you align the splints and complete your operation, then you will take pieces of pine wood which are placed under the bandages which are placed on the sores, and they are made especially of palm branches; choose those that are of small thickness and not slender ones; and let their length be according to the length of the leg, from the knee to the very bottom.
Then wrap around each [splint] a cloth in two layers according to its length, and place one on one side of the leg and the other on the other side, and let them be from the knee to the lowest part of the foot.
Then bind the two splints in three places: at the two extremities and in the middle; for with this structure, the leg is prevented from bending to the right and left, and remains in a good position.
And now a small wooden splint, according to the length of the leg, has been provided; and let the leg be placed upon it so that it retains that [leg's] movement.  (The "that" refers to the proper alignment/position, not necessarily free movement).
And especially you should do this in a fracture, and when there is a wound specifically with it.
Then examine the leg each day and study it carefully, and watch it for abscesses and inflammation or other similar things; and if any of these things occur, then attend to it with what is necessary until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Of the fracture of the talus and of the bones of the foot and of the toes.
A fracture of the whole talus does not often occur; but fractures of the bones of the foot occur sometimes; and fractures of the toes rarely occur, and they do not suffer from anything but attrition in cases of improper positioning.
And if a fracture occurs in the bones of the foot and sometimes those bones are raised up amongst themselves, then the patient should place his foot on the ground prepared as before.
Then place one foot on what is elevated of those bones, and then press down and pull them until they return to their places.
Then apply a plaster and lint on top, and place under the sole of the foot a small board to which two flat pieces are attached.
Then bind that tightly to the foot with a descending bandage, after you have wrapped it with cloths and other things which are needed.
And when three or four days have passed for him, then untie the bandage, for you will find the bones united, or whether they are fractured or dislocated, and the operation in those cases is as you know.
But if some say they are fractured, then restore them and set them as I have recounted to you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then place on the fractured finger a splint according to the length of the finger, and let it be a little longer than it; and place under the foot that board which I have mentioned to you, and bind it with a suitable bandage.
But if what is fractured of the bones is two or three or more, then you place on each finger a splint of its size wrapped in linen cloth, and bind the board to the lowest part of the foot, and let it stand on one of the ends of the board beyond the instep of the foot so that it holds it with good retention.
And it is convenient for you that you do not forget to consider all the things that I have said to you in the other fractures of the accidents that we have mentioned; and oppose yourself to every accident with what is appropriate.
Chapter 18.
Concerning the fracture of the woman's hip and of the pubic bone, and of the man's penis.
When the hip of the woman is fractured, then make her lie down on her side, and gently incline her towards the back a little, and let her be held there.
And then the midwife fills the hip with cotton, and it should be made into a ball in her hip; and then the woman should be held, and her back raised little by little, for all this is pushed until the fractured part of the hip is in place like a sphere, and the broken bone returns to its position.
And then place powders on her back, and when she wants to urinate, remove the cotton easily and let her urinate, and reposition and return it according to the manner in which you arranged it at the beginning, and then return to the first powdering.
And do this for seven days or thereabouts, for then it will be healed.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and attach a reed tube to its opening; and introduce the whole bladder into her hip, and then inflate it through the tube until the bladder is inflated inside the hip, for this will help the fracture to reset.
And then it should be filled with cotton and remain thus for some days as we have recounted, until it is healed.
And when the bone of the pubis of a man or woman is broken, then administer for its restoration and healing that which we have recounted for the hip bone.
And that which is right is not hidden in these proximate fractures which seldom occur, especially to him who has some study and use of and understands this my book well; for this fracture signifies to you in the greatest dispositions the rightful way in its restoration and in its binding, and therefore understand well.
But in the penis of a man, when it is broken, take goose grease, and put the penis in that; and then place a cloth bandage over it, and let it be wrapped, and then let it be left for three days or thereabouts, and it will be healed.
Chapter 19.
Of the restoration of bone fractures when accompanied by a wound.
It is convenient for one who suffers a fracture with a wound, and especially if the bone is large, such as the thigh bone or the humerus, or similar to that, that you carefully and immediately phlebotomize him if the conditions for phlebotomy testify to you as we have said above.
And if the wound flows blood, then it is necessary that you attend to its retention so that you pulverize crushed egg whites over it, if no other cause is present to you.
And then begin to restore the fracture on that same day, and do not prolong this, lest an abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you should leave its restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess has subsided, and do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, for you will cause bad accidents.
But if the broken bone is prominent above the skin, then it is necessary that you carefully reduce it and align it with your hands easily and with little extension.
But if extension is not prepared for you and the alignment of it with your hands, then you reduce it with your hands using this instrument; and this instrument is made of iron, its length is seven or eight fingers, and its width is according to the size of the wound; and for this, it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of them, according to the quantity required for this in the treatment of every type of fracture.
And let it be round, and let it be of little thickness so that it is not bent due to the compression on it during the operation.
And let it have a sharp end which has a margin at its ends; and let the top of it be of increasing thickness, and from the middle of it to the ends.
And this is its form:
And it is named in Greek "labre volentz atelarem", that is, a small splint.
Therefore it is necessary that the sharp end of it be placed raised above the end of the prominent bone.
And drive that in at once until the bone returns, and is set with some equalization/setting.
And then strive to align the ends of the fracture between them; and if the broken end of the bone is thin and does not accept the instrument well, then it is necessary that you incise the end of that bone until the instrument has power over it.
But if you cannot reduce the bone entirely with what we have recounted, then incise it with whatever is suitable from the incisors which we have mentioned; or saw it with one of the saws in whatever way is possible for you.
And then scrape away what remains on the bone of roughness and small splinters.
And when the bone is reduced, and after the reduction the patient finds vehement, persistent pain, then know that the bone is not returned to its natural place; and then do what you can to give the patient great relief/assistance.
When your restoration of the bone is completed, then submerge a cloth in black styptic wine, especially if it is summer, and do not place on the wound cerate or anything else containing oil, lest it cause putrefaction and corruption.
And then administer stitches at the time of your completion of the bone's restoration, and leave the wound uncovered so that you may incise the opening with scissors and use bandages according to the size of the wound.
And guard yourself with all caution that you do not constrict the wound with the fracture, for many times foolish physicians have done this, and it has caused death or a corrosive or suppurating illness, that is, gangrene, in their patients. And if your constriction is light, soft, different from the constriction of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or a bad wound, and you fear some bad accidents which we have recounted, and you find pain in the place, then it is not fitting that you place stitches there, but apply a bandage of hard cloths instead of stitches and bind it with them.
And when after a day or two the wound begins to generate pus, then you remove the cloth which you had placed over it with wine, and then administer poultices, ointments with which it is our custom to treat wounds, such as tetrapharmacon ointment and similar ones.
And it is necessary for you to untie the bandage and examine the wound each day, morning and evening, until it is consolidated and healed, and it is necessary that the limb be positioned in such a way that the pus flows from it to the lower parts easily.
If many days pass and the wound is not healed, nor is the pus discharged from it, then know that here there are signs of small foreign bodies, wherefore it is necessary to probe the wound with a probe. And of those foreign bodies, those that are separated, remove and extract them; those which are not separated and which hinder the limb and cause pain, force yourself to incise them as much as is possible for you.
And if there occurs in the wound swelling or ulceration, that is, erysipelas, or another kind of corruption and putrefaction, then it is necessary that you treat each of these with that which is appropriate to it, from the treatment of which a description has preceded in its chapter.
And this is what my discourse should focus on, and you should take note: when a large bone is fractured, elevate the limb, as with the bone of the thigh and the humerus and similar large bones, and do not attempt traction or extraction of it, for many times death results from this; rather leave it until it suppurates, for it will fall off by itself very quickly, in about twenty or thirty days; and then, if you deem it necessary, treat the wound, and if not, leave it.
Chapter 20.
Chapter 20. On the treatment of callus that occurs in the place of a fracture.
Often this callus occurs at the site of healing of a fracture, especially if it is near the joints, and it deforms the shape of the limb; and it quickly prevents the limb from its natural function.
Therefore, consider if the callus is recent and apply medicines that have astringent properties, such as aloe, incense, myrrh, cerecloth, and acacia, and similar substances; so that you take some or all of these, and mix them with astringent wine, or with egg white, or with sour wine, and apply it to the callus on a piece of lint, and bind it tightly.
And leave the bandage tightly bound for many days; then unbind it and reapply another, until the callus is softened; or apply a thin lead plate on it, because lead has the property of resolving anything swollen in the limbs.
But if the callus has already turned to bone and is tight, and necessity compels its removal carefully, then incise it at its highest point, and excise the excess and prominent part, or rasp it with a file until it is softened; and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture, when it is restored but the limb remains thin and weak, not according to its natural state.
When a bone fracture is healed, and yet the limb remains thin and weak, this is caused by many things: one of which is excessive loosening of the ligament and its binding not as it should be; or by excessive tightness of the bandages, preventing nourishment from reaching the limb; or by excessive or superfluous application of poultices; or by excessive movement not at the proper time; or by a lack of blood in the patient's body and resulting weakness.
And the cure for this is to give the patient nourishing food and to comfort their heart so that their blood is increased; and administer baths; and instill joy and happiness in them.
And then apply a poultice to the limb, so that the poultice draws much nourishment to it, or continuously apply warm water poultices until the nourishment flows and the limb returns to its natural form.
Chapter 22.
Chapter 22. Of the treatment of fractured bones when they are healed crookedly, and preventing this from happening.
When a previously healed limb suffers from crookedness and prominence at the site of the fractured bone and swelling, and this causes deformity of the limb, but the limb is not prevented from its natural function, then you should not heed the words of one who claims that the limb is newly fractured.
But many foolish physicians and healers have done this in our land; and this practice is most reprehensible, leading to great fear, the sign of which is mortal.
But if the lameness and swelling are recent, then they should be bathed in lukewarm water in which softening herbs are boiled, such as mallow leaves and roots, melilot, and similar herbs; and a softening plaster should be applied, such as well-made diachylon; or a mucilage of mallow root should be prepared and mixed with white of egg or olive oil, and applied as a plaster; or ripe figs should be crushed with pigeon droppings, and similar softening and consolidating remedies.
And sometimes it is resolved with gentle, frequent rubbing with moistened hands, and the limb should be moved in every direction at all times.
But if the crookedness has progressed and become severe and hardened, and surgical treatment is required, then the excess should be carefully removed, and the continuity of the bone dissolved, and the superfluous swelling around the bone should be incised with fine scalpels, and this should be facilitated with care and diligence.
Chapter 23.
Concerning a discourse on dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of one of the joints from its place, whereby movement is prevented, the shape of the limb is distorted, and it causes great pain and injury to the patient.
Therefore, when a dislocation occurs to someone, it is necessary to strive immediately for its reduction, and not delay that reduction, for if it is delayed, the place becomes inflamed and thus the reduction of the dislocation becomes very difficult.
And for this reason, it should not be moved or stretched at the time of inflammation, for many times it causes spasms and intense pain.
But when this happens, then you should attend to the phlebotomy of the patient, and let him rest until the inflammation subsides; and then the limb should be bathed with warm water and oil, and then it should be reduced with ease.
And every limb should be treated in such a way that its proper function will return.
And I have already ordered the chapters on dislocation according to the order followed in fractures; from the most superior part of the body to the most inferior part.
Chapter 24.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
If the two jaws are dislocated, it is not delayed; and the dislocation of both is done in one of two ways: either they are dislodged from their places by slight movement, and are somewhat softened; or they are dislocated by complete and total dislocation, until they are softened at the joint, to the point that saliva flows from the patient and they cannot retain it, nor close their mouth, and their tongue is entangled in their speech.
But when their dislocation is slight, then in most cases it returns by itself with little intervention.
But if the dislocation is complete and total, then a reduction must be administered promptly, and not delayed at all.
And it is so that the assistant holds the patient's head, and the physician inserts the thumb of one hand into the root of the jaw's dislocation inside the patient's mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or that he inserts both thumbs together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of his hands are outside with which he pulls; and he commands the patient to relax their jaws and allow them to be moved from each side; and the physician pulls the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, and especially if both jaws are dislocated together, then administer a vaporization with warm water and oil, until the reduction of both is made easier, and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And so when they return and are aligned, and the patient's mouth is closed and not softened, then place on it powders of cloth with cerot (a type of plaster), and it should be made of wax and rose oil; and then it should be bound easily with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on their side, and their head should be resting between two pillows, so that it does not hurt to the right or left side; and do not force them to chew anything; rather, their food should be broth, until when the pains are lessened and the jaws are healed, then let them eat what they want.
And this should be administered with ease, and do not work on opening the mouth during eating, drinking, and speaking, until the jaw is healed and sound.
And if the reduction of the two jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at any time and are not returned to their places, then many times fevers and persistent thirst occur, and very quickly the patient's belly is distended, and very quickly and violently they vomit pure bile.
And when you see this, then know that it is perilous; and many times he for whom this happens dies within ten days.
Chapter 25.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle and of the extremity of the muscle.
The clavicle is not dislocated inwards from its connection with the breastbone; and sometimes it is dislocated outwards, and this is apparent to the touch.
And the restoration of it is that the patient lie on his back and extend his arms. And then constrict the area with your palm with firm constriction, for it will return.
And then place a plaster and powders on that and bind it tightly.
But the extremity of that which is the hip and is continuous with it, is not dislocated in any way; then it must be reduced and bandaged as we have said and as is appropriate for you.
And then place a plaster, and powders, and a bandage on that, and command the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until he is healed.
And with this same treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter 26.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that a muscle is dislocated in only three ways, of which one is that it is dislocated from the upper part to the lower part.
And the second is that it is dislocated to the upper part of the muscle, and this is done late; and it is not dislocated to the parts behind by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated to the parts in front by the nerves.
And mostly it is dislocated and protrudes to the lower parts below the shoulder, and especially in those whose flesh is scant, it is easily dislocated; but in those in whom there is much flesh, it protrudes with difficulty.
And it quickly happens to some men by a blow or fall, and their muscle is inflamed by a hot abscess, and it is believed because of this that it is dislocated.
Then it is necessary that you prove this before you are completely certain of it, and then you proceed to its cure.
And the dislocation is known when it is in the lower parts below the shoulder; so that you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the healthy muscle, because you find a manifest difference between them; and you find in the head of the muscle a concavity, and below the shoulder near the touching of the head of the muscle, like an egg, and the patient cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it in all movements.
If it is dislocated below the feet or above, and you then find a manifest touch, and it is not hidden.
And the reduction of this dislocation is easily done when it is fresh and the patient is a child.
And the reduction of it is that the minister raises the patient's hand to the upper parts; and then places his hands below the patient's shoulder, and raises the joint forcefully up to its place, and the minister raises the patient's hand, and extends it upwards; and then places it below because it returns easily.
But if by what we have said it does not return, and from the beginning that the dislocation happened, many days have passed, then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water and that a poultice be applied which softens and lubricates, such as boiled roots of althea, fenugreek, and melilot in water, and it should be applied.
And then the patient should be supine on his back and a wool ball be placed under his shoulder, of a consistency between soft and firm, and then the doctor places his heel on the ball, and raises the head of the muscle with force, and pulls the patient's arm so that it is not pulled downwards, and another minister holds the patient's head so that it is not pulled downwards, because it then immediately returns to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it according to this method:  that you have present a man who is taller than the patient, and he stands on that side, and inserts his shoulder under the patient's shoulder and raises the patient's shoulder to the upper parts until the patient is suspended in the air, and another minister pulls the patient's arm from the lower parts of his belly. But if the patient is light, then it is necessary that something else be suspended with him, in order to weigh him down, because the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is reduced easily in another way: that a long pole be fixed in the ground, the top of which is round in shape, like a pestle, neither thick nor thin, and then it is placed under the patient; after having placed linen cloths on top of the pole, and the patient is standing along the length of the pole, and then his arm is extended downwards on the other side; and his body is extended on the other side with strength, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
And if its reduction is difficult for all the reasons we have mentioned, then use this treatment: that you take a pole the length of which is two braccia, and the width of which is four fingers, and its thickness should be lessened at the top near the shoulder according to this form:
And then on the round top, linen cloths should be tied so that the pole does not hurt the patient.
And then it is placed under the armpit of the patient, and the hand or arm is extended over the pole down to the lower parts, and the pole is tied around the armpit and the arm and the extremity of the hand.
Then the arm is placed on a rung of a ladder according to its width, and the hand is extended downwards, and the other side of the body is suspended on the other side, for the joint enters immediately.
And when its entry is completed in whatever way is possible for you, then you must place under the armpit a ball of wool of suitable size; and then apply a plaster prepared from powdered  millet and egg white over the whole muscle, so that it is wrapped around it, and then tighten under the armpit with a decent bandage; and the bandage should be wrapped over the plaster, and his hand should be attached to his neck, and it should be left, and his hand should not be moved for seven days.
And it is necessary that the patient's diet be light until the limb is strengthened, for this is more conducive to his healing.
And then the diet should be increased after seven or five days and some movement should be exercised; and then if it is firm and not softened, then it is healed.
But if the joint is dislocated many times due to humidity affecting it or some other cause, then cautery with three holes should be administered there, as previously described in the chapter on cautery.
And if all this is done and after seven days the bandage is untied and the joint is not firm, and the plaster and the bandage are reapplied several times, and it is not firm and hangs loose and cannot raise that arm to the upper parts, then know that its nerves, which are at the head of the muscle, are severed or stretched or softened, and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
Regarding the dislocation which occurs between the foot and the ankle or the posterior parts, the reduction is done with impulse and extension with the hands, until it returns [to its place].
And another bandage should be applied to it, and treat it until it is healed.
And if during the healing process there is heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement, then the patient should use baths many times until that heaviness is relieved and it returns to its former natural state.
Chapter 27.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated elbow.
Know that the elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated in all directions, and more so to the front than to the back, and its dislocation is not hidden from you because it is apparent to the sight or to the touch, depending on how it is dislocated. And when you compare the dislocated elbow to the sound one, this is clearly shown to you, because the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm, nor can it touch its muscle with it.
And it is necessary that you act quickly and reduce the dislocation before a hot abscess develops, for if a hot abscess develops, its reduction is difficult, and it is not easily healed completely; and especially when it is in the posterior parts, for it is worse than all other types of dislocation, and causes greater pain, and often leads to death with it.
And its restoration, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the patient's hand should be extended with two hands, and their arm expanded, and the doctor's hands should be on and under the elbow, and he should push the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the root of his palm, until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is in the front, then return it in such a way that you bend the hand with your hand, until you strike the elbow with the root of your palm.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer strong and very forceful extension.
And it is that two assistants extend the arm, and two other assistants hold it firmly so that it is not moved during the extension.
And then the arm should be rotated in every direction, after it is wrapped in a long folded cloth over the patient's hand, or a bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil so that this helps lubricate the joint easily; and then push the joint with strong pressure until it returns to its place.
And after the reduction, he should be placed on a plaster which has styptic and drying properties with egg white, and it should be bound tightly with a descending bandage, and the arm should be suspended from the neck, and left like that for some days.
And then it should be unbound; and if the joint is formed in its place, then unbind the bandage and leave it.
And if you see the joint is not well tightened, then repeat the plaster and bandage, and leave it for some days until it is tightened; and then unbind it.
But if the patient experiences pain after the reduction of the joint, and their disposition is agitated, then administer moist heat in a bath and gentle friction, and compression, until relief is obtained; or place on the joint an emollient, that is, something very oily; and then it should be bandaged, and left on the bone day and night. And then remove that, and put him in a bath; and when he is sweating, then rub the joint with a temperate friction, and then again apply the emollient, that is, something very oily, once, and a second time, and a third time. with the use of a bath, until it is softened.
And if you wish, place on it warm, moist cow dung and bind it tightly; and do this several times, because it is moist and brings it back to its proper state.
Chapter 28.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocation of the wrist, that is, of the joint that is between the arm and the hand.
The wrist is sometimes dislocated, and its reduction is easy, unlike other joints; however, speed is necessary in its reduction at the time it is dislocated, before the place becomes inflamed or a hot abscess forms there.
And the reduction of its dislocation is that you place the patient's wrist joint on a table and the assistant extends the patient's hand, and the physician places his hand or his palm on the prominence of the joint, and pulls it until it returns to its place.
However, it must be considered whether the palm is already turned towards the belly of the hand; then the patient should place the back of his hand on a table during extension and reduction. And if the dislocation is towards the back of the hand, then the position of the patient's hand should be on the table with the palm down, so that the physician's hand can fall on the prominence of the joint.
And so if it returns immediately, it is good; and if not, then compress it with a poultice to soften the inflammation and leave it; and do not repeat this, for otherwise, you will not be able to achieve reduction once several days have passed.
However, if the joint remains twisted and causes no harm to the patient except that the hand is weakened and cannot grasp anything, then you should know that the nerves are pinched or bruised, and then there is no remedy except to cauterize near the injury and very quickly apply a bandage.
Therefore, when the limb is set, then apply the poultice we have recommended, and let it be applied and bandaged for five days; then it should be unbound and left, and the patient's hand should be exercised.
