But if its movement is impeded and some gravity occurs, then soothe it with warm water and massage several times until it is relieved.
Chapter 29.
Concerning the treatment of finger dislocations.
Sometimes the fingers are dislocated in their entirety.
And when a finger is dislocated towards the back of the palm or the belly of the hand, then straighten the finger and force its dislocation back with your hands until it returns to place.
Then support the head of the finger, and suspend it downwards from the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it like that for two days.
Then suspend it and extend it until it is straightened and aligned on that same day.
And when night comes, bind it according to that description, and do not cease to unbind it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bind it at night; and do this for several days until it is tight (or healed/firm).
And do likewise if it is dislocated on the palm of the hand, and bind it to that part and perform your first operation in this way until it is healed.
And similarly do this when it is dislocated in other parts.
Chapter 30.
Of the treatment of the dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When a complete dislocation happens to any of the vertebrae of the back or neck, and many vertebrae are removed from their places, then in that case there is no cure, for death is expected for the patient.
And a sign of this is that the patient's excrement is expelled involuntarily, and he cannot retain it; and often some of his limbs are paralyzed,  either both legs or both arms, or one of them; or a vertebra is dislocated, for often it is dislocated, and sometimes the dislocation affects the four limbs.
Therefore, that is dislocated in the back parts and is called paralysis.
But the treatment is that then you consider if the paralysis comes from an infection (or fever), because then in that case there is no cure nor healing at all.
But in that which comes from a fall or from a blow or something similar, the Ancients already described the methods of cure with lengthy discourses, of which the most returns with profit; but I will now abbreviate that, whose brevity excuses its length, for my proximity to my intended exposition, with which I have expounded and formulated this, and formed it from diverse instruments, with which I will show this and explain that.
And of those, only that which affects the two (legs) properly is cured with what I have recounted.
And it is so that the patient is laid on their face on a soft cushion close to a wall, and a soft mat is placed under them so that their feet are not irritated.
And then a straight pole is placed, fixed in the ground at the head end of the bed near the patient's head, and another pole at the foot end of the bed, near the patient's feet.
And the assistant holds the pole, and it should not be fixed in a pit; and another assistant holds the other pole in the same manner.
Then wrap a strong linen bandage around the patient's head and under their chin; and extend the end of the bandage to the pole which is by their head, and tie it there; and then tighten another bandage around their hips, their buttocks, and their legs; and then adjust all the bandages and tie them to the other pole which is by their feet.
Then each assistant stretches the bandages to their respective poles, and the two poles and the bandages are not removed from their positions, but however, they are not fixed as we have said.
And the physician places his palms on the spine with firmness until it returns to its place, or places a board there, and then adjusts his feet on the board until it returns to place.
But if it does not return to place by this method, then take a board whose width or length is about three braccia, and make a hole in the wall which we have said is near the patient, where the end of the board will fit. Then place half of the board on the spine, and the physician places his feet on the other end, and presses firmly until the spine is compressed and returns to its place.
And if you wish, open with a drill which is turned by hand; and you should place in the ground, beneath the patient's head at the back of the neck, two poles, the length of which is one braccio, and beneath the patient's head at the back of the neck, two poles; and let the distance between each pole be one palm, and let a hole be made in each pole through which the drill passes, and let each be firmly fixed in the ground so that it does not move.
And let there be placed a round pole in which is the drill, and let it be rotated in the two holes of the two poles.
And at its end is a hole in which the pole is fitted, the length of which is one palm including the revolving part; and in the two other poles is something similar to that.
Then tighten the straps that you have tied to the patient's feet at the hole which is under their head, and the straps that you have tied to their thighs at the drill which is under their feet.
And then, by each drill, let a servant stand who twists the drill with their hand, and the physician sets the vertebra as we have said.
Then, once the vertebra is returned and the place is set, you should apply a drying plaster with egg white, and then lint; and then place over the plaster a tablet of wood whose width should be three fingers or thereabouts, and whose length should be sufficient to cover the area of the swelling, and the healthy part of the vertebra.
Then it should be bound with the straps we have mentioned, and feed the patient a light diet until they are healed.
And if any swelling remains in the area at the end of healing, then medicines should be administered that soften and smooth, with the application of the wooden tablet for a long time, which we have recommended; and a lead plate should also be applied to that area.
And sometimes swelling occurs in the back of the spinal vertebrae, and is caused by that dislocation; and it is that bone which already swells, so that no attempt should be made to treat it with this medicine, for it will very quickly cause death.
Chapter 31.
On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the muscle joint suffer only dislocation, and they do not experience what happens to other joints of small movement and concavity.
And the hip joint is dislocated in four ways, for it is dislocated inwards, or outwards, or forwards, or backwards.
And it is mostly dislocated inwards, and rarely forwards or backwards.
And a sign of its inward dislocation is that, when you align the patient's healthy leg with the affected one, the affected leg is longer and the knee more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend their foot without pain, and it is the place where the pain becomes an abscess, because the head of the femur has already reached there.
And the sign of that to which dislocation happens outwards, is that its symptoms are contrary to these symptoms.
And a sign of that to which dislocation happens forwards is that he extends his leg completely, but yet cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if he forces himself to walk he cannot do so on the front part; and his urine is retained and his pain becomes an abscess, and he cannot walk on his heel.
And a sign of that to which dislocation happens backwards is that he cannot extend the knee, nor can he bend it before bending the hip, and his leg is shorter than the other, and the hip is softened, and the head of the femur is behind that place.
But reduction of the types of this dislocation is that you consider, then, if the dislocation is old and chronic, that it is already healed with having it near you, you administer its reduction and it remains according to its disposition, then it is not in that cure of all; and then it is not fitting that presumption be made in that.
But in one in whom the dislocation is recent, and it is one of the four types of dislocation, then take care that you return the joint and extend it inwards and outwards, and move it to the right and left, for it quickly returns and does not need other treatment.
But, if it does not return in this manner, then it is necessary that you prepare a strong assistant and extend his leg from the lower part, either with your hands, or with a bandage which is tied above the knee; and another assistant extend the hand of that one from above so that he places his hands under his heels; and then tighten with a strong bandage on the root of the thigh, and let another assistant hold the end of the bandage.
And let the extension be from him or from the inner part to the hip at the part of the fork (groin), or from the back part to the part of the back.
And let the extension of all of them be at once until the patient is raised with his body from the ground and remains suspended.
And this type of extension is common to all four (types of dislocation).
And therefore, if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it will be good; and if not, then it is necessary for every type that which we shall say of its proper cure.
In the reduction of the hip itself, when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is on the inner parts, it is necessary that the patient be supine on his sound side; and then place the ligaments on the root of the thigh in that place which is between the head of the thigh and the place which is below the hip; and then extend the ligament up from the part of the hip to the highest part of the groin.
And then another strong attendant should take his arms, and firmly grasp the thick part of the affected thigh and extend it outwards with a strong extension, for by this it returns to its place.
And this type is of easier cure, with which this member is reduced, than the other types.
But if it is displaced upwards and does not yield to treatment with this type of cure at all, then it is necessary that the patient's two feet be bound together with a strong, light ligament, above both ankles and both heels.
And let the length of each (ligament) be the quantity of four digits, and let the affected leg be extended more than the other by the quantity of two digits.
And then let the patient be suspended from the head by a beam, and let him be indoors; and let his height above the ground be the quantity of two arms' lengths.
And then order a strong young man to reduce the head of the thigh, and let another young man be suspended with the patient, and the other young man, reducing the joint with forceful pressure, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
But in the proper reduction, when it is a dislocation to the outside, it is necessary that the patient lie on his back as we have recommended in the case of coldness; and let the ligament be tightened around the affected leg properly and around his feet.
And then two beams should be placed, one at his feet, and the other at his head; and then let adjusted beams be placed in the middle of the elevation, upon which wet cloths are wrapped, so that the patient is not harmed because the beam is between his thighs, so that during the extension to the lower parts it is not pulled.
And then each assistant extends on his side, and the physician with his hand guides the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the front parts, then it is necessary that the leg be extended lightly, and it is according to this same narration on a support; and the physician places the palm of his right hand on the patient's patella, and then grasps that with the other hand, and with that grasp places that extended to the lower parts to the part of the knee.
And if the dislocation is in the back parts, then it is not necessary that the patient be extended to the lower parts, and he is elevated above the ground;  instead it is necessary that he be placed on a hard surface, just as it is necessary for the raising if his hip is dislocated outwards, according to what we have said about his lying on two cushions; and if it is on his face and the ligaments are extended, as we have said, there is little harm.
And it is necessary that compression be administered with a board on the place where the joint is.
Therefore, when the reduction is completed as it should be — and the sign of his reduction is not hidden from you, and that is that you extend the patient's two legs, and when you see those equal and the patient bends his leg and extends it without difficulty, then you know that the limb is returned as it should be — and then you adjust the two thighs and apply a plaster and bind it so that the hip is not moved in any of the parts; and let the patient rest for three or four days.
Then untie the ligaments and the plaster, and compare one leg with the other.
Then, if you see them equal in appearance, then you know that the dislocation is now healed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any cause for softening, then you return and plaster that, and bind that according to your first binding, and leave it for three days.
Then untie it, and have him delay walking on it for a few days, until it is strengthened.
Chapter 32.
On the treatment of knee dislocation.
The knee is dislocated in three ways, for it is dislocated outwards, and inwards, and downwards, more towards the back; and it is not dislocated forwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you command the patient to apply his leg to his thigh; then you will know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all its dislocations is that you make the patient lie down straight, so that he extends his leg if he can; apply your two hands to his face, and place his foot between your feet; and then apply his knee to your palms, and hold those between your fingers on his knee firmly, and another assistant extends his foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its reduction is that his leg adheres to the thigh easily and without effort.
And then plaster that and bind the leg to the thigh, and then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days, and then unbind them.
And do not allow the patient to walk for some days, until he is healed and strengthened.
But if its reduction is difficult for you, then administer strong extension with the bandages of which the treatment of the hip has preceded narration. (or: ...with the bandages, the use of which in the treatment of the hip has been previously described).
Chapter 33.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the talus (ankle bone).
The talus is sometimes dislocated by a slight movement, and sometimes completely dislocated, and its dislocation is either inwards or outwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that sometimes the talus swells, protruding towards the side to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment of its slight dislocation is by gentle reduction, and that is to be extended easily with the hands and bandaged until it returns (to its place).
But in treating it when it is completely dislocated, you must make the patient lie down straight, and have a strong and capable assistant hold him from behind at the back of his back, in the middle of his body.
Then you, with your right hand, take the foot from the upper part, and with your left hand the lower part of the foot at the ankle.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then with your left hand twice, and then extend with the left; and push the foot with the right towards the leg without effort.
And do this twice, as we have described.
Then, push the foot towards the leg a third time, and you will be at the ankle.
Then it will return in one or two attempts according to this method, and if you see the foot set correctly, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation, for it will return.
But if reduction by the method we have described is forbidden to you, then have the patient lie on their back on the ground, and tightly fasten a small pillow to the ground, which should be placed between their thighs, and you should have wrapped a cloth around it so that it does not hurt the patient.
Then let the patient's attendant hold their thighs, and let another attendant extend the foot with their hands or with a bandage tied around the ankle; then let each attendant extend against the extension of the other, and let the pillow remain between the patient's thighs holding it, so that their body is not pulled downwards against their extension.
Then let the physician set the dislocation with his hands, and let another attendant hold the healthy thigh lower down, because the dislocation easily returns.
And so when the dislocation returns and the successful reduction is clear to you, then apply a plaster and bind it tightly with bandages, and bind the foot to the leg with a bandage.
And it is necessary that you fear or beware of the nerves which are behind the heel, so that the bandage over it is not tight and strongly injures them.
Then leave it for two or three days; and if the bandage is loosened, tighten it.
Then unbind it on the third or fourth day, and forbid the patient from walking for forty days. But if he is forced to walk before that time, there is no guarantee that the dislocation will not be dissolved and corrupted, and therefore will not receive a cure.
And if a hot abscess occurs to him, then it is necessary that you administer in its treatment that which we described previously, in another place of treatment and application, until it is healed.
Chapter 34.
On the treatment of dislocations of the toes.
It is necessary that what is dislocated be healed with little extension without work; and this which is not difficult, beforehand is light/easy.
But if the dislocation is in some joint of the toe, then it is necessary that you make the patient place his foot on a level surface of the earth or on a table, and be standing there as if he were walking.
And then you stand, and place your foot on what protrudes from the joints, and then press that with your foot with force until it returns, and that you see that it is already healed and no prominence appears in the place.
And then place under the sole of his foot a board that covers the whole foot, to which are two ends, and then tighten that with a decent and firm bandage for three days; and then untie it and make him rest from walking for many days, until it is strengthened and its return is assured.
Chapter 35.
Of the types of dislocations that are made with a wound or with a fracture or with both together.
When any of these causes occur, and you are diligent in its cure and in his restoration, many times death ensues.
And therefore it is not fitting that anyone should presume upon his cure, except one who is wise in the art, of long experience, merciful and compassionate, slow, not rash nor presumptuous; and who uses in the beginning only such medicines as lessen hot abscesses, and leaves the patient to divine judgment, except for one in whom you hope for salvation from ruin, with alleviation of the sickness, and in whom some hope appears. And then strive at once to reduce it at the beginning of the cause, before a hot abscess develops.
And then, if the limb returns as we wish, then administer a regimen which lessens hot abscesses and treat the wound with what is suitable for it of drying ointments.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and the jagged ends of the bone are separated, then you must strive to join them, and do in this what we have said in the simple diseases which precede this in those locations.
And guard and avoid, according to your ability, that which enters into the paths of fear as my preaching has preceded you; for this is better for you before your Creator, and more permanent for your glory, and more salutary to your true self in your time. Thus end the words of Albucasis perfectly, for which may God be blessed. Amen.
